Market Talk: Election Analysis Roundup; Read our compilation of concise, timely analysis by Wall Street Journal reporters related to financial regulation, the financial-services industry and more: Dodd-Frank could be "done for"; Trump administration puts FSOC at risk; Big policy changes in store at CFTC; SEC agenda in doubt; Trump win heralds weaker CFPB

1,727 words
9 November 2016
22:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Warren, In Statement, Offers to Work With Trump

One person who isn't a candidate but suffered a big loss on election day is Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D, Mass.), who could've seen her influence significantly expanded with Democrats still in the White House. On Wednesday the Wall Street critic issued a statement saying she was frustrated that a Democrat nominee had won the popular vote but lost the presidency, which also occurred in 2000. But she held her criticism for now:" President-Elect Trump promised to rebuild our economy for working people, and I offer to put aside our differences and work with him on that task." (ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

FSOC Meeting Next Week to Discuss Asset Managers

The Financial Stability Oversight Council, headed by Treasury Secretary Jacob Lew, is set to meet next week to review asset management products and activities. At the Nov. 16 meeting, staff by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission will give a presentation on stress tests of central counterparties, according to a public notice of the meeting released Wednesday. That portion of the meeting will be held privately in executive session due to confidential information that will be discussed tied to the council's review of asset management products and activities. The open session will include an update on alternative reference rates and revisions to the council's regulations under the Freedom of Information Act. (donna.borak@wsj.com, @donnaborak)

Former FDIC Chairman Sheila Bair Praises Donald Trump

President-elect Donald Trump gained support from a former top bank regulator Wednesday morning when Sheila Bair, who chaired the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., tweeted: "Gracious and unifying victory speech. Congratulations Mr. Trump." Ms. Bair, a Republican, is president of Washington College in Chestertown, Md., and had served as FDIC chairman from June 2006 through 2011. Her tweet immediately got a question back from another follower on whether she would consider a position in the next administration or at the Federal Reserve. She has yet to respond. (rachel.witkowski@wsj.com; @rachelwitkowski)

Trump presidency will see end of global bank regulator, risk-weights

The G20's Financial Stability Board will become obsolete under a Trump presidency, says a veteran Washington DC policy adviser close to Republicans. Peter Wallison, a senior fellow at the free-market American Enterprise Institute, said a Trump administration effectively spelt the end of the global body, which was created in the wake of the financial crisis to co-ordinate implementation of post-crisis reforms designed to make the financial system safer. "The Financial Stability Board is out of business. They can't do anything until they have a partner in the US, and they aren't going to have one," he said in an interview. Mr. Wallison, who has been a key adviser to Congressional Republicans, said a Trump administration would bring "new thinking" to financial regulation, which would probably include dumping the complex system of risk-weights that underpinned the current rules. "We need a strong and simple set of capital regulations that are based on the ideas in the Hensarling bill," he added, referring to a bill favored by House Republicans he has advised on. (Adam.Creighton@dowjones.com)

Analyst: Trump Administration Could Rescind AIG, Prudential  Sifi' Tags

A Trump administration could rescind the "systemically important financial institution" labels from American International Group Inc. and Prudential Financial Inc., Capital Alpha Partners analyst Ian Katz said in a note to clients. The tag, applied under the Obama administration to the two large insurance companies, comes with stricter oversight from the Fed. Mr. Trump will have the power to appoint a Treasury Secretary and regulators who vote every year on the designations. These new appointees may take months to act, but will probably eventually look to deregulate the large insurers, Mr. Katz said. (Ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Election Leaves Future of Fed's Tarullo Uncertain

Of all the financial regulators whose moves will be watched in the coming months, one of the most important will be Fed governor Daniel Tarullo. The architect of the central bank's regulatory policy for the last eight years now sees those policies in jeopardy. A Trump administration could appoint regulators who want to tear them down. Will Mr. Tarullo retire? Could he stick around longer into his term, which ends in 2022, to fight to preserve his legacy? A Fed spokesman had no comment Wednesday. (ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Credit Unions Bid to Reduce Regulation

The head of a trade group for U.S. credit unions said in an email to its members Wednesday morning that he was "launching credit unions' attack to reduce regulatory burden now." Investors have bid up the shares of bank stocks following Donald Trump's win partially because of an expected rollback of the Dodd-Frank financial reform law, which may also benefit credit unions, too. Jim Nussle, president of the Credit Union National Association, added in the email that credit unions spent "upward of $7 billion" on "excessive" regulatory demands and that he would work with President Trump ease those costs. (peter.rudegeair@wsj.com; @rudegeair)

Financial Analyst Warns "Dodd-Frank Done For"

A top financial analyst on Wednesday warns that the 2010 regulatory reform law signed into law by President Obama will be unwound under President-elect Donald Trump. "Dodd-Frank is done for," Karen Shaw Petrou, a managing partner of the policy analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc. wrote clients. "Although the law is the law and will only change over time, its systemic, structural assumptions are as smashed as those behind post-crisis Federal Reserve policy." In her post-election forecast, Ms. Petrou said U.S. banks will face the "most acute political challenge" next year with populists forces on both sides of the aisle. She also believes Republicans will seek to dismantle the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and the Financial Stability Oversight Council through administration action and then by law. (donna.borak@wsj.com, @donnaborak)

Dodd-Frank Under Pressure: FSOC Challenged -- Capital Alpha's Katz

"One of the key questions is how far Republicans will go to gut Dodd-Frank," writes Ian Katz of Capital Alpha Partners. "We would expect a move to either eliminate or severely restrict the authority of the Financial Stability Oversight Council," as well as a attempt to raise "the $50 billion threshold that makes banks systemically important and subjects them to Federal Reserve oversight." Even with the GOP victory, he says, it's unclear they have enough votes in the Senate to pass those rules -- or how much banks, heavily invested in Dodd-Frank compliance, want dramatic changes. (jacob.schlesinger@wsj.com)

Big Policy, Personnel Changes in Store At CFTC

Donald Trump's victory Tuesday means J. Christopher Giancarlo, the sole Republican member of the CFTC who has spent much of his two-year tenure at the top U.S. swaps regulator as a voice of dissent, will likely become the regulator's acting chairman by mid-January. It also means a series of Democratic-backed regulatory initiatives, such as a proposal to rein in high-frequency trading firms, will be significantly scaled back or scrapped altogether, according to regulatory sources. (andrew.ackerman@wsj.com; @amacker)

Financial Trade Group Urges Donald Trump To Improve U.S. Economy, Innovation

The Financial Services Roundtable on Wednesday pressed President-elect Donald Trump to "act swiftly" to propose new policies to improve the U.S. economy and protect both consumers and taxpayers. "Our country faces big challenges, but it also has incredible opportunities for innovation and growth," said Tim Pawlenty, chief executive of the trade group, which represents 100 of the biggest U.S. financial firms, in a statement. Top priorities for the trade include: regulatory transparency, housing finance reform and cybersecurity. (donna.borak@wsj.com, @donnaborak)

SEC Agenda In Doubt, "Victory For Asset Managers" -- FBR's Mills

The Republican sweep Tuesday "changes the balance of power" at the Securities and Exchange Commission, writes Edward Mills of FBR & Co. This comes as the agency "is pursuing a variety of rules related to the asset management industry," which are now in doubt. "A Trump victory changes the balance of power at the Commission, likely ending this effort, a victory for asset managers," he adds. (jacob.schlesinger@wsj.com)

Trump Win Heralds Weaker CFPB, Possible Fannie Privatization, Mixed Bag for Banks -- Cowen's Seiberg

The clean Republican sweep in Tuesday's elections is "a clear positive for consumer banking" as the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau likely "will be weaker," writes Jaret Seiberg of Cowen Washington Research Group. The election also "has to open the door for the government to return Fannie and Freddie to private ownership." Dodd-Frank, he predicts "will not disappear but it will become less onerous for big banks." Yet he sees the new GOP as "pro-business," not pro-mega bank," and risks remain for higher capital requirements and "a modern version of the Glass-Steagall Act." (jacob.schlesinger@wsj.com)

What a Trump Presidency Means for Chinese Financial Regulation

What impact a Trump presidency will have on Chinese financial regulation likely depends on the two countries trade ties. Mr. Trump has pledged to raise both import and export tariffs on China, a major U.S. trading partner. Such moves could weigh on China's economy and get leaders here evermore focused on a growth agenda, away from their reform goals, says Jonas Short, policy analyst at NSBO Research in Beijing. But, "I must stress that that's a worst case scenario... Beyond trade, China is more sheltered than people think from global events. If market sentiment is weak, then the [People's Bank of China] is likely to add more liquidity through discreet operations... but I doubt China's financial risk policy will change as that is clearly a long-term goal from the very most senior members of government," he adds. US
Analysts on Trump Administration: Less Regulation, With a Measure of Uncertainty; Trump White House, GOP Congress seen scaling back Dodd-Frank, ousting CFPB Director Cordray

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON President-elect Donald Trump's surprise victory left Wall Street analysts scurrying to assess how a Republican administration with a Republican-led Congress might oversee financial institutions.

Throughout the 2016 election, Mr. Trump has sidestepped the subject of regulating Wall Street, limiting his remarks to closing the carried-interest tax loophole and temporarily freezing all new agency rules. The New York businessman has repeatedly said he would "http://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-took-aim-at-dodd-frank-on-the-stump-1478691726", but hasn't offered details on what would replace it.

Financial services analysts widely predicted a Trump White House and a GOP Congress would scale back Dodd-Frank, and open the door to a "break up the banks" bill pitched as regulatory relief for community banks.

"Dodd-Frank is done for," Karen Shaw Petrou, a managing partner at Federal Financial Analytics Inc., wrote to clients in her postelection forecast on Wednesday. "Although the law is the law and will only change over time, its systemic, structural assumptions are as smashed as those behind postcrisis Federal Reserve policy."

KBW Inc. analyst Brian Gardner doesn't see Republicans fully unraveling Dodd-Frank, largely because it will be difficult to move such legislation through the Senate, where Democrats will hold 47 seats enough to block legislation. "Chances for wholesale changes" to Dodd-Frank "are still low," he wrote to clients. "Consensus will be the only way to pass bills."

Large U.S. banks are likely to face the "most acute political challenge" from populist forces on both sides of the aisle, Ms. Petrou wrote. Mr. Gardner suggested there is support for legislation to give smaller-size banks some regulatory relief, but he warned that could be "a vehicle for a  break up the banks' bill," which Mr. Trump has said he would support.

A number of analysts predicted that newly empowered Republicans will take steps to weaken the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, including ousting Director Richard Cordray next year. A federal appeals court ruling in October opened the door for the next president to remove the bureau's director at will and closely supervise and direct the agency.

"Our sense is that president-elect Trump could choose to dismiss Director Cordray and fill the post with someone more ideologically aligned with his world view," Isaac Boltansky, an analyst for Compass Point Research & Trading LLC, an investment firm focused on the financial-services sector, wrote in a note to clients.

Two potential candidates to head the consumer watchdog agency under a Trump administration are Texas Republican Rep. Randy Neugebauer, who didn't run for re-election, and Mark Calabria, director of financial regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute.

A Trump administration is also likely to delay, rewrite or scrap the Labor Department's fiduciary rule, analysts predict. The rule requires advisers to act in their clients' best interests when making recommendations on retirement accounts. Doing so, Mr. Gardner writes would be a net positive for brokers, life insurers and asset managers.

"There is now a path for Congress to block the rule in addition to Trump acting unilaterally to freeze it," Jaret Seiberg, an analyst with Cowen Washington Research Group, wrote to clients.

Still, he cautioned that firms must prepare for the new rules until any changes are made, which wouldn't happen any earlier than February. He also noted that a number of Republican lawmakers have advocated for the Securities and Exchange Commission to require all financial advisers to act in the best interests of clients.

"That would suggest there will be a replacement that imposes this standard without the same litigation threat as the Fiduciary Duty rule," Mr. Seiberg wrote.

Staples Tests Technology

By Kim S. Nash 
294 words
9 November 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Staples Inc. is ready to test a technology that lets companies order office supplies by voice.

Developers and e-commerce experts at the company worked with International Business Machine Corp.'s Watson artificial intelligence system to build the Easy System, which Staples expects to test with customers before the end of the year.

The retailer joins a range of large companies, including Facebook Inc., Automatic Data Processing Inc., and Humana Inc., using AI to build intelligent software agents that understand natural language and field multifaceted requests. Some see bots as the next user interface beyond swiping and clicking. Staples, for example, wants to let business customers order supplies that it may not carry, offering to find and deliver such products, said Faisal Masud, executive vice president of global e-commerce at the office-supply retailer.

The Easy System users initiate by pressing a red "easy" button is designed to learn with each interaction, said Mr. Masud. The cognitive computing scheme behind Watson lets Staples' algorithms learn the habits of individual customers. Eventually, for example, the system will understand that a customer's reference to "blue pens" means an 18-pack of Pentel ballpoints.

Staples uses application programming interfaces to connect its inventory and ordering systems to Watson, via IBM cloud technology. The system checks contracts and business processes set up for each customer to ensure an order meets appropriate approvals, he said. If an order exceeds a spending threshold, for example, it could be routed by text or email to a supervisor for an override.

If there is an issue the Easy System can't resolve, it will automatically invoke a live link to a human customer-service agent.

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

Coravin Inc., Burlington, Mass., which makes a device that allows users to access and pour any bottle of wine without pulling the cork, has sold $22.5 million of a $30 million equity round. In June 2015, the company raised a $13.6 million Series C-1 round led by Windham Venture Partners with participation from Quadrille Capital.

Deja Mi Inc., Raleigh, N.C., whose WedPics collects photos and videos taken by wedding guests in one location online, has issued almost all of a $900,000 round of equity and securities. A January filing showed the company increasing the size of an earlier equity round to $7.3 million. In 2014, the company secured a $1.5 million Series A round from investors including Great Oaks Venture Capital, IDEA Fund Partners and Triangle Angel Partners.

Impossible Objects LLC, Northbrook, Ill., a developer of technology to print 3-D objects with reinforced composites, has sold $1.7 million in equity. In 2014, the company received $2.8 million in seed funding led by OCA Ventures.

Luna Pharmaceuticals Inc., Providence, R.I., which makes the Premama brand of powdered drink mixes for expecting and new moms, among other things, has sold slightly more than half of a $3.5 million equity round. Cherrystone Angel Group, Radar Capital and River Hollow Partners are backers.

MachineMetrics Inc., Northampton, Mass., which develops production monitoring software for the manufacturing industry, has sold $2.1 million of a $2.6 million equity round. Long River Ventures invested in the company in June 2015.

Milo Biotechnology, Cleveland, which aims to reverse muscular dystrophy using gene therapy, has increased the size of a debt offering to $1.5 million and has issued $1.2 million toward the round. A June filing showed the company issuing half of an $800,000 offering. In September, WSJ Pro Venture Capital reported that the company had raised an undisclosed amount of funding from JumpStart Inc. and Rev1 Ventures.

MoveWith Inc., San Francisco, which connects users to fitness classes, has issued almost all of a $1.25 million debt, rights and securities offering. In January, the company received $3 million in seed funding from Accomplice, Forerunner Ventures and Founder Collective.

Roofstock Inc., Oakland, Calif., an online marketplace for occupied, single-family rental homes, has sold all but $2.9 million of a $20 million equity round. Last year, the company closed a $13.3 million in Series A round from Bain Capital Ventures, CSC Venture Capital, Grey Wolf Ventures, Khosla Ventures, Nyca Partners QED Investors and SV Angel.

Smartsheet.com Inc., Bellevue, Wash., a spreadsheet-inspired collaborative work tool, has opened a $5 million offering of rights and securities. In 2014, Insight Venture Partners, Madrona Venture Group and Sutter Hill Ventures invested $35 million in the company.

Parent of News-Aggregation App Toutiao Seeks $10 Billion Valuation; Beijing Bytedance Technology considers raising about $1 billion in latest funding round

By Alec Macfarlane, Kane Wu and Liza Lin

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

HONG KONG Investors are considering hanging a valuation of more than $10 billion on a highflying news-aggregation app known as Toutiao that has quickly become one of the hottest startups in China.

The news app's parent company, Beijing Bytedance Technology Co., is considering raising about $1 billion in its latest round, according to people familiar with the situation. The new valuation target of more than $10 billion, including the fresh funds for Beijing Bytedance, is a sign of the company's rapid progress after it was valued at just $500 million in June 2014.

Bytedance has started discussions with potential investors about the new fundraising round, but the situation remains fluid and terms could change as the company negotiates with prospective investors.

The Chinese startup's new fundraising bucks a trend of slowing appetite among investors for putting money into the industry. Investment in startups backed by venture capital fell 14% globally to $24.1 billion in the third quarter from the previous quarter, according to KPMG LLP. That was the lowest quarterly funding total since the third quarter of 2014. In China, venture-capital investments fell by nearly one-third to $3.9 billion in the third quarter from the second, KPMG said.

Toutiao, or "headlines" in Chinese, is a personalized news app that pulls together customized third-party content for users based on their interests. It does this by analyzing data taken from users' social-networking accounts, and keeps tabs on a user's interaction with the app so content becomes more tailored.

The bulk of Toutiao's revenue comes from advertising. Toutiao offers targeted advertising, and the company is able to use data analytics to break down user segments by age, location and interest. This allows advertisers to reach very specific consumer segments, according to a company presentation. Toutiao declined to provide revenue figures.

In September, Toutiao struck a partnership with Chinese e-commerce website JD.com that enables Toutiao users to directly browse and pay for products on the shopping site via the app. JD.com and its online merchants will also benefit from Toutiao's targeted advertising service, the companies said at the time.

The fresh funding round could come in handy as Bytedance seeks to expand overseas. Apart from Toutiao, the company also owns an English-language news app called TopBuzz and it led a $25 million funding round in October for Indian news aggregator Dailyhunt. It is also considering expanding into markets including the U.S., Brazil and Japan.

Its most recent publicly announced funding round was in June 2014, when Nasdaq-listed microblogging service Weibo Corp. invested in Toutiao. The tie-up gave the news aggregator greater access to Weibo's data trove. That round valued the company at $500 million. The company's other existing investors include venture-capital firm Sequoia Capital China and Russian billionaire Yuri Milner, who made his name with early bets on Facebook Inc. and Twitter Inc.

Toutiao recently scored a high-profile hire when it reeled in Liu Zhen, Uber Technologies Inc.'s former China strategy chief. She joined the Beijing-based startup's senior management ranks in October, according to a company spokesman. Ms. Liu joined after China's homegrown ride-hailing champion, Didi Chuxing Technology Co., "http://www.wsj.com/articles/china-s-didi-chuxing-to-acquire-rival-uber-s-chinese-operations-1470024403".

Toutiao was founded by 33-year-old serial entrepreneur Zhang Yiming in March 2012. As of Oct. 31, Toutiao claimed it had activated 600 million users and said it serves 66 million daily active users.

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

TOKYO -- SoftBank Group Corp. Chief Executive Masayoshi Son said Monday he would ease SoftBank's debt load by using a $100 billion fund he is establishing with other investors as his vehicle for large investments.

The comments by Mr. Son break with his longstanding willingness to use SoftBank's own funds for large investments such as Sprint Corp., the unprofitable U.S. wireless carrier SoftBank bought for $22 billion in 2013.

Last month, SoftBank said it would join Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund to create the $100 billion SoftBank Vision Fund.

SoftBank is set to provide around $25 billion, while the Saudi fund said it was looking to contribute up to $45 billion over the next five years. SoftBank said it was in talks with several potential investors to provide the rest of the war chest.

"From now on, investments that exceed tens of billions of yen will in principle be made through the investment fund. SoftBank will be insulated from risk," Mr. Son said.

He said the company aimed to lower its debt relative to operating cash flow. As of Sept. 30, SoftBank had 10.7 trillion yen ($102.42 billion) in net interest-bearing debt.

Mr. Son said the new fund would help SoftBank stay ahead in a future where artificial intelligence and the so-called Internet of Things dominate everyday life, thanks to the rapid evolution of processors.

He described SoftBank's recent $32 billion acquisition of British chip maker ARM Holdings PLC as the first step in what he described as "SoftBank 2.0." But SoftBank had too much debt to continue investing at this pace, he said.

"How do we remain aggressive while improving our balance sheet? The answer is the SoftBank Vision Fund," he said.

Mr. Son's comments came as SoftBank reported solid earnings, thanks to its strong domestic mobile-phone business. Turnaround efforts appear to be taking root at Sprint, which last month reported a narrower loss in the latest quarter.

Sprint continued to add customers and posted year-to-year revenue growth for the first time since its takeover by SoftBank.

For the July-September quarter, SoftBank posted a net profit of 512 billion yen, compared with 213 billion yen the year before, boosted by gains from the sale of stakes in Chinese e-commerce giant Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and Finnish game maker Supercell Oy. Revenue fell slightly to 2.14 trillion yen.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Investors bet against Mexico in case Trump wins; INVESTMENT STRATEGY

By Chris Flood 
466 words
7 November 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
03
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Three-quarters of the assets of BlackRock's ETF are being shorted

Investors have placed bets worth around $745m that Mexico's stock market will fall in the closing days of the US presidential race as opinion polls suggest Democratic candidate Hillary Clinton 's lead over Donald Trump , the Republican candidate, has narrowed.

The bets against the Mexican stock market have come via BlackRock's $1bn Mexico exchange traded fund. Approximately three-quarters of the ETF's assets are being shorted, suggesting some investors are preparing for a Trump win .

Around 80 per cent of Mexico's exports go to the US. The economy is expected to suffer if Mr Trump wins the White House, due to his protectionist trade and immigration policies.

The ETF bets against the Mexican stock market increased substantially following the FBI's decision to open a new probe into Mrs Clinton's use of a personal email server during her time as secretary of state.

"We have seen a big surge of demand for [Mexico bets via this ETF] in recent weeks," said Simon Colvin, a senior researcher at Markit, a data provider in London.

The price of the iShares ETF has declined around 6.7 per cent over the past two weeks, tracking the Mexican stock market in US dollar terms.

"[The ETF] and Trump's polling numbers have been moving in tandem," added Matthew Unterman, a director at S3 Partners, a US-based financial analytics provider.

Mr Unterman said that bets against Mexico's stock market via the ETF had shrunk in the first half of 2016 to around $315m by mid-June, coinciding with weakness in Mr Trump's ratings. But as support for Mr Trump rose in the second half of the year, so too did the short positions against Mexican equities.

Investors have pulled around $1.2bn in the first nine months of this year from the 30 Mexico ETFs listed in the US and on the domestic Mexico stock market, according to ETFGI, a London-based consultancy. Assets held in the 30 ETFs stood at $3.9bn at the end of September.

Volatility in the Mexican currency has also reached a five-year high, mirroring the twists and turns of the US presidential campaign.

Edward Glossop, a Latin America analyst at Capital Economics, a London-based consultancy, said victory for Mr Trump would have "significant repercussions" for Mexico, but a weaker peso should help to cushion the economy from the worst effects.

"The manufacturing sector and the states in the north-east where industrial activity is most concentrated would clearly have the most to lose. But the tourism sector should benefit from a weaker peso," said Mr Glossop.

Mexican stocks and ETFs join peso in Clinton relief rally

Jude Webber 
730 words
7 November 2016
16:30
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Who will win tomorrow s US election? According to Mexico s peso and stocks, Hillary Clinton is home and dry.

The peso, which has been a top barometer of election sentiment in recent months and is widely used as a proxy for emerging market risk, rallied 2.2 per cent to 18.6 per greenback on news over the weekend that the FBI has found nothing new in its 11th-hour investigation of Mrs Clinton s emails.

The stock market joined in the party: Mexico s blue-chip IPC index was around 2.6 per cent higher on Monday, the biggest one-day jump since late January, writes Jude Webber in Mexico City.

Mexican exchange traded funds also bounced back sharply: the US-listed iShares MSCI Mexico Capped ETF, which has been used by foreign investors to use as a proxy to bet on the Mexican equity market, jumped more than 5 per cent to 51.82   its biggest one day advance in nearly five years. That jump takes the ETF back above the level it was sitting at prior to the FBI s shock announcement on October 28 that it was opening a new investigation into Mrs Clinton s use of a private email server.

But has the market jumped the gun? What if Donald Trump triumphs tomorrow? What then?

Capital Economics is warning that the central bank might have to hike rates by a massive 100 to 200 basis points to steady the peso, which some in the market predict could crash to as low as 25 to the dollar on a Trump win.

To put that in perspective, Banxico has raised rates by 125 basis points in total since last December   including an extraordinary 50-point increase in February that was out of the usual calendar of policy meetings.

In the event of a Trump victory we would expect the central bank to react by hiking interest rates to support the peso, probably at an emergency meeting as soon as Wednesday morning. Banxico has past form in this regard. It held an emergency meeting in February when the peso was depreciating sharply and hiked interest rates by 50bp. It also held an impromptu press conference in the immediate aftermath of the UK s vote to leave the European Union in June, when the peso sold off again, at which Governor Carstens strongly hinted they would use the scheduled policy meeting in the following week to raise interest rates   the policy rate was then hiked by a further 50bp at that meeting. The exact size of the increase in interest rates this time around would depend on just how far the peso falls. But we suspect a hike in the region of 100-200bp, from 4.75% now, would be needed to provide meaningful support to the currency.

There seems to be a bit of a difference of opinion among Mexican policymakers about what to do. Agustn Carstens, the central bank chief, appears to think action is unavoidable   in recent days he has talked about a contingency plan being readied. Jos Antonio Meade, the finance minister, has played down such suggestions however, saying Mexicans would not need to wake up to a  precipitous  government reaction on Wednesday.

Mr Carstens has, in recent months, played down the prospect of more off-calendar emergency rate rises, but then, he has also likened Mr Trump to a hurricane for Mexico s economy. Banxico s next scheduled policy meeting is on November 17 and Capital Economics reckoned Mexico could follow up with a second post-election hike if the peso remained under pressure.

Capital flight and heightened uncertainty would weigh on growth and could well tip Mexico into recession, warned Alfredo Coutio, director for Latin America at Moody s Analytics USA. If that happened, he also saw the need for a fiscal and monetary policy to be tightened  more severely  and suggested more drastic budget cuts, higher taxes and the acceleration of some privatisations.

Higher rates, meanwhile, would squeeze Mexico s already unstellar economic growth and would  take the steam out of the services sector  which has been driving growth for the past year, Capital Economics said.

That picture for Mexico is not pretty. The markets want to believe there is no risk of that any more. We shall see.

The sale of P3 is one of the biggest "http://www.wsj.com/public/page/news-real-estate-commercial.html"in Europe this year.

And while transactions involving industrial property fell in the U.K. and Germany in the first nine months of this year compared with the 2015 period, they remain above their long-term average, according to property-data firm Real Capital Analytics. Transactions rose in smaller economies such as the Netherlands, Italy and Norway.

A big draw of warehouses over office towers is that the returns are better. The average capitalization rate a measure of income on property for European warehouses was 6.5% in the third quarter, compared with 5.9% for retail properties, Real Capital data show.

In addition to returns, the limited number of high-quality warehouses in Europe and the increasing need for them has bolstered demand, according to Anand Tejani, partner at TPG Real Estate.

"The e-commerce thing is real," Mr. Tejani said. "If you start to think about major companies like Amazon needing to deliver to consumers in a matter of hours, that highlights the important role that these warehouses are playing."

This isn't GIC's first foray into industrials. In 2014, the sovereign-wealth fund bought IndCor Properties, a U.S. warehouse platform, from Blackstone Group LP for $8.1 billion.

Blackstone has built another industrial property company in Europe. Logicor, set up in 2012, owns 660 warehouses in 18 European countries.

The company has hired two banks, one to explore the sale of the business and the other to study an initial public offering, a person familiar with the matter said.

Formerly niche real-estate sectors have been moving toward the mainstream. Historically, offices and shopping malls were the mainstays of property investors. They remain far larger sectors in terms of invested capital.

But warehouses, student housing, care homes and even garden centers have gained traction in recent years.

As investors pile in, returns have fallen. For instance, the return on European warehouses in 2013 was about 2% more than shopping malls, whereas that figure is now just 0.6% more, Real Capital data show.

It isn't just real estate that has crept higher on the wish list for global investors. Demand for other alternative assets, such as private equity, has been surging.

Ultralow interest rates and loose monetary policies at central banks around the world have sent returns on assets such as bonds lower, making alternative assets more attractive.

TPG and Ivanho Cambridge bought P3 in 2013. Since then, the firm has doubled in size, the joint statement said.

"We are confident of the long-term potential of the European logistics sector, and look forward to expanding this attractive platform," said Lee Kok Sun, chief investment officer at GIC Real Estate.

The Draper Venture Network, a global group of funds that includes Tim Draper's Draper Associates, is launching a program to give limited partners, including those outside the network, an opportunity to invest in later rounds for some of its most-promising companies.

The idea is to syndicate deals for six to 10 companies during the next year that are raising growth capital in later-stage rounds mostly larger than $30 million. Shares for DVN Select investors would come mainly from pro rata investment rights that network venture firms, who are mainly early-stage investors, can't fully execute. The companies might offer additional shares to DVN Select participants.

"The vision for the Draper Venture Network is to eventually form a different kind of fund, a  meta-fund' that contributes capital to its member VCs and to double down on the best companies in their portfolios," Mr. Draper said in an email.

DVN named six companies participating in the program, including home-services marketplace Thumbtack Inc., backed by Draper Associates. The others are data analytics specialist Ayasdi Inc., Middle Eastern ride-hailing company Careem FZ LLC, cybersecurity startup Cylance Inc., fashion website Lyst Ltd. and business-reviews site Trustpilot Inc. They are planning to raise capital in the next one to 10 months, according to Gabe Turner, the network's director.

Co-investing is popular in private equity but limited partners need the capability to independently evaluate deals quickly, something many lack, especially small endowments and family offices. Special purpose vehicles that enable VCs to syndicate pro rata rights in later rounds have "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFPEA0120150202eb22f5vu9" in the past several years.

"Through this program, investors get access to unique investment opportunities that are pre-emptively sourced by some of the best fund managers internationally," Mr. Draper said. "Since we are doing this deal-by-deal, they can participate only in companies that are compelling for them."

Mr. Draper said LPs in network funds increasingly want to invest directly in companies with a high probability of staging a successful exit within two years. Most have little exposure to later-stage companies through DVN funds. The program also gives investors the opportunity to invest in companies internationally.

For companies, DVN Select is advantageous because it broadens their investor base, Mr. Draper said.

Investors pay a 3% administrative charge to participate in each deal. Carried interest is waved for network LPs, but from outsiders DVN stands to collect a 10% carry, which rises to 20% if performance of the investment is 3X or greater.

DVN is launching the program at an LP meeting on Tuesday. The network has nine member firms. Besides Draper Associates they are 3TS Capital Partners, Dalus Capital, Draper Athena, Draper Esprit, Draper Nexus Ventures, Draper Triangle, Wamda Capital and Wavemaker Partners.

The network has been around since the early 1990s but "http://www.wsj.com/articles/garlands-take-drapers-global-vc-network-rides-on-1466422204" three years ago and is looking to expand.

The New York firm invests $50 million to more than $200 million in companies with revenue between $25 million to $125 million-plus and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $7 million to more than $20 million, according to the firm's website.

Investors have placed bets worth around $745m that Mexico s stock market will fall in the closing days of the US presidential race as "https://ig.ft.com/us-elections/polls" suggest Democratic candidate "https://www.ft.com/topics/people/Hillary_Clinton" s lead over "https://www.ft.com/topics/people/Donald_Trump", the Republican candidate, has narrowed.

The bets against the Mexican stock market have come via BlackRock s $1bn "https://www.ft.com/topics/places/Mexico" exchange traded fund. Approximately three-quarters of the ETF s assets are being shorted, suggesting some investors are preparing for a Trump win.

Around 80 per cent of Mexico s exports go to the US. The Latin American economy is expected to suffer if Mr Trump wins the White House, due to his protectionist trade and immigration policies.

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Are your online accounts safe with biometrics?

By Martin Arnold and Hugo Greenhalgh 
2,228 words
5 November 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
09
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fake fingerprints, selfie masks and voice tapping mean the wealthy should be worried by the latest mobile methods of accessing money. Martin Arnold and Hugo Greenhalgh report

Anyone who has struggled to remember the password for their banking app or fumbled with a card-reading device can appreciate the sheer joy of using their fingerprint, voice or face to access their bank account quickly via their smartphone.

Most big high street lenders now use the latest biometric technology to give customers a more convenient way to check their account balance or make payments. However, a big question remains unanswered: is it safe?

A cyber security expert from consultancy NCC recently visited the Financial Times to give a demonstration of how to hack into a smartphone's biometric authentication software.

Matt Lewis, NCC research director, showed how to make a copy of his own fingerprint using wood glue, candle wax and a printed circuit board that allowed your correspondent to hack into his smartphone.

He also tricked voice-recognition software by playing back recordings of his own voice and produced a 3D-printed mask of his face based on photos of himself, which was then worn by your correspondent to hack into his phone.

While you cannot forget your voice or face - making them a simpler way to check your identity - they are also much harder to change than your password if they are ever stolen by cybercriminals. This means that if biometrics becomes the dominant form of authentication it is likely to be much more damaging if the systems are hacked.

As more financial service providers launch biometric identity-checking schemes - such as MasterCard's so-called "selfie pay" service that lets people make mobile payments by photographing themselves, Wells Fargo's eye vein scanning system, or HSBC's Voice ID - experts say security will be a more pressing issue.

" Biometrics aren't the same as passwords - they aren't secret," says Mr Lewis, a former technology specialist at GCHQ, the government's electronic intelligence agency. "You need other elements to ensure fraud is prevented. If a database of fingerprints is hacked into, that could compound the problem."

Batten the hatches

Cyber security has already shot to the top of the boardroom agenda for banks, particularly after 76m customers at JPMorgan Chase, the biggest US bank, were shocked to learn that some of their personal data were stolen two years ago.

The seriousness of this threat was underpinned by one of the biggest bank robberies in history, carried out by cyber thieves in an audacious raid on the Bangladesh central bank in February. The crooks made off with $81m that was on deposit at the US Federal Reserve.

"It is a very intimate game between organised crime innovating attack methods and the banks coming up with defences against these," says David Ferbrache, technical director at KPMG and former head of cyber and space for the UK ministry of defence.

He reckons that organised crime groups are making so much money from hacking traditional password-based bank systems, that for now they will be in no rush to switch their attention to the challenge of cracking biometric controls. "But over time they will start to spend more time looking at biometric identity."

Most UK banks allow customers to log on to accounts using their smartphone's fingerprint reader. This has the advantage of keeping the biometric data on the phone, rather than stored centrally by the bank on a database that could become a target for hackers.

Robert Capps at NuData, which tracks people's behaviour through their phone to authenticate them, says most biometric checks are no safer than a traditional password. If hackers gain access to your password, they can set up a new mobile account pretending to be you. "It all comes down to the enrolment process - and that is back to the username and password," he says. "This is not the great panacea."

Several big financial institutions are now focusing on voice-recognition systems to identify their customers. Unlike phone-based fingerprint scans, these typically involve a customer's digital "voiceprint" being stored by a bank and used to verify who they are when they call.

Voicing the issue

Voice biometrics has a long history. The concept can be traced back to Alexander Melville Bell, father of Alexander Graham, who in 1867 published a book called Visible Speech, The Science Of Universal Alphabetics: Or Self-Interpreting Physiological Letters . Bell's focused not so much on the spoken word, but on how they were said.

Further developments by Russia and the US saw voice biometrics gain greater traction - and accuracy - in the 1970s and 1980s, but a series of false starts meant that the technology has not been widely adopted until today.

Now it is set to become big business - and not just for the banks. Research by Tractica, the market intelligence firm, suggests that revenues from voice biometrics could top $5bn by 2024, up from just $245m in 2015. It sees the greatest adoption among consumers using their mobile phones, as well as benefits for call centres and healthcare providers.

Other banks, such as Capital One, are using voice technology to enhance their services: its customers can now check balances and pay off credit card bills by speaking to Alexa, Amazon's voice assistant, which sits on voice command devices such as Echo.

But the telephone is also a popular medium for fraudsters. According to Financial Fraud Action UK, British consumers and financial institutions lost more than 750m over the course of last year - a jump of 26 per cent from 2014. Telephone banking fraud almost doubled over the same period, up by 92 per cent to 32.3m.

One out of every 2,500 calls to call centres run by banks and other financial services companies is fraudulent, data from Infiniti Research recently revealed.

How it works

The technology has improved greatly in recent years. Gone are the frustrations of repeatedly saying "yes" or "no" into an automated system that cannot understand your accent. Today's programmes can determine who is speaking as well as working out what they're saying.

There are nonetheless different technical approaches. Lloyds has agreed a deal with Pindrop, a voice fraud prevention and authentication company, to offer what the tech firm calls " phoneprinting " to its customers from the beginning of December.

This is not technically biometrics, but works in a similar fashion. The customer makes a call, and the programme analyses 147 different call features that range from the type of phone you're using to the type of background noise. "It adds a layered picture - everything outside the voice," explains Matt Peachey of Pindrop.

The key function is to deter phone fraud and improve customer security by highlighting unusual activity. Lloyds intends to take a further step and introduce full voice biometrics by the summer of 2017.

HSBC has opted for technology that cross checks 100 unique characteristics of a person's voice - including speed of delivery, natural cadence or pronunciation - as well as certain physical aspects that listeners cannot discern, such as the shape of the customer's larynx or their nasal tracts.

"The technology measures the way your muscles produce the sound rather than the sound itself," explains Francesca McDonagh, head of retail banking and wealth management at HSBC. "It will then record a voice print - my voice is my password."

It is a development that will be welcomed by those with many online accounts and an array of passwords. But concerns have been raised over whether customers will be happy divulging this information. Will they really be happy for their bank to know the shape of their larynx?

"The key perceived disadvantage for customers relates to privacy," says Anthony Duffy, UK director of retail banking at Fujitsu. "Some individuals are not comfortable with organisations - particularly large companies - holding biometric data.

If the "nightmare scenario" is that a large depository of biometric data - such as fingerprints - could be stolen, something approaching this has already happened: last year, hackers stole 5.6m sets of fingerprints from the US Office of Personnel Management, which oversees civil servants in the federal government.

"If a customer is logging on to check their balance face-recognition may be enough, but for a high value transfer they may say it has to be multi-touch identification where both fingerprint and face are required," says George Avetisov of Hypr, a biometric data encryption company.

Andr Malinowski, head of international business at Computop, says biometrics will never be watertight and customers should always insist on a secondary ID measure, such as an old-school password or Pin. "[With biometrics] we are talking about something you cannot change. And if it is compromised, it is compromised."

Behavioural systems

Ultimately, making mobile banking convenient while reassuringly secure is likely to rely on even more sophisticated systems using hundreds of data points - from how fast we type to where we are - to build a unique profile that can be used to recognise us whenever we use our phone.

These behavioural biometric systems are being piloted by several banks and have the advantage of being much harder to fake while not being usable across multiple accounts of the same person. "Wind the clock forward five years and you will see a combination of behavioural biometrics and quite sophisticated analytics and risk -scoring on each transaction," says Mr Ferbrache at KPMG.

If they can crack the security problem once and for all, the potential for savings by the banks and other financial services providers is huge. For example, MasterCard, the payments processor, has spent $1bn on security over the past three years alone. "More than 50 per cent of the global fraud in our ecommerce business is digital," explains Ajay Bhalia, MasterCard president of global enterprise risk and security.

With more than 2bn cardholders, MasterCard is ramping up its defences against fraud, investing in voice technology, but also looking into security measures based on fingerprints, facial recognition, heartbeat technology, eye veins and irises. It is running a pilot scheme with ABN Amro in the Netherlands and is looking to roll it out to 12 European markets, including the UK, in early 2017.

The hassle factor

In strengthening the barriers to fraud, though, banks and card providers are exercising an unspoken trade-off between efficiency and security. Customers become frustrated if it takes too long to go through the authentication process; banks then lose custom as people just put the phone down.

Yet given how time-consuming most biometric hacking techniques appear to be - such as copying someone's fingerprint or making a mask from their photo - some experts believe they are likely only to be worth doing for targeted attacks on wealthy people.

Most banks and financial institutions will retain several hurdles for wealthier customers to clear before allowing them access to their accounts. It may be more hassle, but if it's a question of ensuring fraudsters do not have access to your money, it is a price many will be willing to pay.

"When it comes to protecting consumers from cyberthreats, the greatest challenge is balancing security with convenience," says Keiron Shepherd, senior security specialist at F5 Networks. "However, these often lie down divergent paths. Like all things in security, voice biometrics is not the silver bullet, but another tool to defend against cybercriminals."

Voice training

Why a strong accent shouldn't be a problem

What happens if my voice changes? Or I have a strong regional accent?

Siri, an automated voice recognition personal assistant introduced by Apple on its iPhone 4S back in 2011, struggled at first with regional accents. Tabloid journalists gleefully reported that one particularly salty Scottish phrase was repeated by Siri as "Eurobond bank" - not quite the intention of the speaker.

But voice recognition services - and Siri - have improved greatly. Biometrics covers more than just the spoken word; it delves into how these words are articulated.

"There have also been concerns that voice biometrics might be undermined by factors such as regional accents, background noise or people having colds," says Anthony Duffy, UK director of retail banking at Fujitsu. "However, the latest iterations of such software make such concerns redundant."

Indeed, the majority of them gauge factors such as inflection and intonation; and take into account your accent.

"It's a pot pourri of factors," says Bruce Bomphrey, sales and business development director at Soitron UK.

This means it will take account of regional accents - and learn the way in which you speak. And if you have a heavy cold, it will compensate for those factors, by matching variables other than intonation, for example.

Research is being conducted on whether this holds true for another group: those undergoing - or having completed - transitioning from female to male or vice versa.

"It's a question of whether [transitioning] could affect the fundamental aspects of the voice," says Dominic Watts, senior lecturer in forensic speech science at the University of York. "The length of the vocal tract shouldn't change, but I suppose the difference could be if somebody was seeking to develop a lower- or higher-pitched voice."

While that might affect whether the voice recognition software works, the solution is simple: re-record your voice password.

Hiring a woman as an investing partner still makes headlines in Silicon Valley in 2016.

Sequoia "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sequoia-adds-first-woman-to-its-u-s-investing-team-1476995607", and many of the largest firms still have no female or nonwhite partners. Now in a year in which the U.S. could elect Hillary Clinton as the first female president venture capitalists say diversity remains a low priority, according to a new survey.

Only 4% of those investors in the survey ranked diversity as one of their top concerns, citing economic conditions and portfolio-company revenue growth higher on the list, "https://www.linkedin.com/pulse/startup-founders-investors-think-techs-diversity-solve-fairchild"

In 2014, Alphabet Inc.'s Google published its first diversity report, prompting a flurry of technology companies to follow suit. In 2015, Ellen Pao's gender-discrimination case against Kleiner Perkins Caufield Byers proved to be a lightning rod in venture capital for a wider discussion about sexism on Sand Hill Road.

But 2016 seems to be the year that venture capitalists are more likely to tweet, "I'm with Her" than to create initiatives that would help them hire or invest in her. Indeed 63% of the venture capitalists surveyed by LinkedIn say they're unaware of any official initiatives to increase the diversity among founders in their portfolio or employees.

Silicon Valley has long prided itself on its breakneck pace of innovation and change. Venture firms that began investing in computers in the 1970s are today pouring millions into frontier technologies like artificial intelligence, space travel, augmented reality and robotics.

And yet the gender and racial makeup of venture capital's most prominent firms has barely budged. A recent survey from PitchBook Data Inc. shows women make up 6.3% of investment professionals with board seats at U.S. firms.

Venture capitalists told LinkedIn they believe close to 30% of the investment partners they hire in the next five years will be female and racially diverse. But as today's dismal diversity numbers show, the problem won't solve itself without action.

Almost half of the venture capitalists surveyed by LinkedIn say they faced a "pipeline" problem: Too few startups they look to invest in have diverse founders. Another 35% said they saw no obstacle at all, 7% said diverse founders don't seek them out as investors and 6% said diverse founders typically don't start the type of companies they traditionally invest in.

In the list of obstacles preventing venture capitalists from investing in startups with racially diverse or female founders, they left out a big one: apathy.

FANNIE MAE

Treasury to Receive $3 Billion Dividend

Fannie Mae said it would send a $3 billion dividend payment to the U.S. Treasury in December. It also reported that revenue declined in its latest quarter but profit soared due to accounting benefits and fluctuating interest rates.

The mortgage-finance company posted net income of $3.2 billion for the third quarter, up from $1.24 billion a year earlier. Revenue dropped 4.1% to $5.61 billion.

Fannie and Freddie's regulator and some lawmakers have expressed concern over their dwindling capital reserves. Under the terms of the companies' bailout agreement with the Treasury, they send profits above their established capital reserve to the government, but these buffers are slated to wind down to zero dollars by 2018. So far, that drop in reserves hasn't caused Fannie or Freddie to require more bailout money. Fannie reported net worth of $4.2 billion as of Sept 30.

Still, any blip in the housing market or volatility in the derivatives values could cause Fannie or Freddie to need taxpayer funds.

Austin Hufford

HSBC

French Prosecutors See Tax-Evasion Case

French prosecutors recommended that HSBC Holdings PLC should stand trial for allegedly helping people evade taxes in France, a person familiar with the matter said Thursday.

If French judges decide to follow prosecutors' advice, HSBC could stand trial in Paris in the first half of next year. The judges also could dismiss the case.

Prosecutors began investigating HSBC in 2014 as part of a widening probe into whether the bank helped recruit customers in France, possibly breaching laws authorizing only French-registered lenders to sign up customers in the country. The investigation also examined whether the bank was complicit in laundering the proceeds of any tax evasion.

"We will continue to defend ourselves vigorously," a spokeswoman for the British bank said Thursday.

Noemie Bisserbe

S&P GLOBAL

Sale of J.D. Power Drives Up Profit

S&P Global Inc.'s bottom line more than tripled in the third quarter, boosted by the sale of J.D. Power, and the financial-information and analytics firm again raised its outlook for the year in anticipation of continued strong bond issuance.

S&P Global, formerly McGraw Hill Financial, reported third-quarter profit rose to $923 million, or $3.36 a share, from $281 million, or 92 cents, a year earlier.

Excluding a gain on the sale of J.D. Power and other items, adjusted earnings rose to $1.43 a share, topping analysts' expectations of $1.34. Revenue reached $1.44 billion, just above estimates.

For 2016, the company raised its adjusted earnings estimate to $5.15 to $5.25 a share based on strong year-to-date results.

Anne Steele

FANNIE MAE

Treasury to Receive

$3 Billion Dividend

Fannie Mae said it would send a $3 billion dividend payment to the U.S. Treasury in December. It also reported that revenue declined in its latest quarter but profit soared due to accounting benefits and fluctuating interest rates.

The mortgage-finance company posted net income of $3.2 billion for the third quarter, up from $1.24 billion a year earlier. Revenue dropped 4.1% to $5.61 billion.

Fannie and Freddie's regulator and some lawmakers have expressed concern over their dwindling capital reserves. Under the terms of the companies' bailout agreement with the Treasury, they send profits above their established capital reserve to the government, but these buffers are slated to wind down to zero dollars by 2018. So far, that drop in reserves hasn't caused Fannie or Freddie to require more bailout money. Fannie reported net worth of $4.2 billion as of Sept 30.

Still, any blip in the housing market or volatility in the derivatives values could cause Fannie or Freddie to need taxpayer funds.

Technology
CIO Journal: Developers, Companies Latch On to 'Chatbots'

By Kim S. Nash 
559 words
4 November 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Six months ago, Facebook Inc. said it would open its Messenger service, making it possible for businesses to build virtual assistants that can chat with the one billion-plus real people who use the communications platform each month.

Since then, developers have created more than 33,000 such "chatbots," which have icons and nicknames that at first glance might not appear that different from a friend or relative in a user's contact list.

Last month, MasterCard Inc. launched Kai, a bot for banks that makes it possible for Facebook Messenger users to check activity on their credit, debit and loan accounts and set up financial-management tools. Another Mastercard bot for merchants lets customers make purchases by sending a Facebook message and paying with Mastercard's digital wallet.

Such capabilities may be just the beginning for bots. A range of companies are betting that the convergence of messaging platforms, chatbots and increasingly powerful artificial intelligence will lead to the creation of a more natural interface between machines and humans.

"We are thinking of an intelligence center that will help you get to the right bot at the right time so you don't have to have a sea of icons that are sitting there that you don't know how to use," says Stan Chudnovsky, vice president and head of product for Messenger at Facebook.

Facebook is using its power to accelerate the growth of messaging and bots. In 2014, it pushed users to employ Messenger for direct messages, and the platform now has more than one billion users. That same year, Facebook acquired for $19 billion messaging service WhatsApp, which also now has one billion users.

April's debut of development tools for building Messenger bots has appealed to startups and established brands alike. Some users have complained that initial Facebook Messenger bots are slow, with limited conversational capabilities.

"There are growing pains," said Robin Chan, chief executive of Operator, a startup that makes shopping bots.

Facebook is meeting with developers and recently updated its tools to, for example, further customize conversation threads.

A Facebook user planning a vacation, for example, could send his criteria to Messenger, which would match the information to bots from travel-company partners. Partners would reply with offers. Some bots, learning the habits of their human masters, may anticipate work. "Your monthly golf date isn't yet scheduled. Would you like to call Kiawah Island?"

This is beyond today's simple bots that answer trivia questions or reorder laundry detergent based on data from prior purchases, says Roberto Masiero, senior vice president of innovation labs at Automatic Data Processing Inc.

"It's a tectonic shift in how we interact with computers," Mr. Masiero said.

Mr. Masiero predicts that bots will become the new interface for corporate software. Rather than launching individual software programs to change health benefits, say, or start a retirement account, employees could consult with bots that have learned their habits and use algorithms and machine learning to make suggestions, he said.

ADP software engineers are working with social anthropologists and copywriters to tinker with bot behavior. Tests show that for bots to earn human acceptance, they must not act too much like people or too much like computers.

COMPANIES
Germany benefits as investors frown on UK; Property

By Judith Evans 
336 words
3 November 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Germany has overtaken the UK to become Europe's most popular destination for property investment since the Brexit vote.

More investment flowed into German than British real estate in the third quarter for the first time in four years, with  ¬13.6bn committed against  ¬10bn for the UK, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

Meanwhile, a survey of 800 leaders in Europe's property industry found that Berlin, Hamburg and Frankfurt were the top three prospects for real estate investment among European cities - with London ranked 27th.

The UK vote to leave the EU had prompted investors to reconsider whether British bricks and mortar could still be considered a haven, said the report by the Urban Land Institute and PwC, the professional services firm.

"The fallout from the Brexit vote gives an extra boost to the already-strong German real estate market," said Lisette van Doorn, ULI's European head.

"Many real estate investors are willing to sacrifice some yield in return for lower risk. In this risk-off environment, the stability of German cities becomes even more attractive."

Some 90 per cent of those surveyed said UK property values would decline in the coming year.

Real estate investment trusts in Germany and Scandinavia had risen to trade at premiums to the value of assets, a sign that investors felt their cash was safer there, the report said.

UK Reits are trading at discounts of about 20 per cent to net asset value, according to Jefferies.

Real estate investment is declining across Europe. The total value of transactions in the third quarter was down 38 per cent from a year earlier to  ¬46bn; investment into the UK dropped 51 per cent, according to the RCA figures.

The hunt for German investments had boosted smaller cities such as Leipzig, which had attracted more than  ¬1bn this year, RCA said.

Political turbulence was the main worry for property investors, according to the ULI/PwC survey.

UK surrenders top spot to Germany in European property investment

Judith Evans 
477 words
3 November 2016
00:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Germany has eclipsed the UK to become Europe s most popular destination for property investment since the Brexit vote.

In the third quarter of 2016, more investment flowed into German real estate than UK for the first time in four years. Some  ¬13.6bn was committed to the German market against  ¬10bn for the UK, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

Meanwhile, a survey of 800 leaders in Europe s property industry found that Berlin, Hamburg and Frankfurt were viewed as the top three prospects for real estate investment among European cities   with London languishing at number 27.

London s decline came after the UK s vote to leave the EU prompted investors to reconsider whether British bricks and mortar could still be considered a safe haven, the report by the Urban Land Institute and PwC, the professional services firm, found.

 The fallout from the Brexit vote gives an extra boost to the already-strong German real estate market,  said Lisette van Doorn, European chief executive of ULI.

 With considerable political and economic uncertainty in Europe, many real estate investors are willing to sacrifice some yield in return for lower risk. In this risk-off environment, the stability of German cities becomes even more attractive. 

Some 90 per cent of those surveyed said UK property values would decline in the coming year.

Meanwhile, real estate investment trusts in Germany and Scandinavia have risen since the Brexit vote to trade at premiums to the value of their assets, a sign that investors feel their cash is safer there, the report said.

In comparison, UK reits are trading at discounts of about 20 per cent to net asset value, according to Jefferies, the investment bank.

However, the shift in Germany s favour took place in a context of declining real estate investment across Europe. The total value of transactions in the third quarter was down 38 per cent from a year earlier, at  ¬46bn; investment into the UK dropped 51 per cent, according to the RCA figures.

Even as it took over the top spot, Germany saw a 34 per cent drop in real estate investment from a year earlier, although the previous year s total was boosted by corporate takeovers.

The hunt for German investments has helped boost smaller cities in the country: Leipzig, for example, has attracted more than  ¬1bn of investment so far this year, a record for the city, RCA said.

International political turbulence, including forthcoming elections in France and Germany, was the main worry among property investors, according to the ULI/PwC survey, along with concerns about migration and terrorism.

Analysts have also suggested that the current property cycle may have peaked. Prices for core real estate   the properties most in demand   are  considered too high in many markets , ULI said.

Tech
Companies Rally to Build Chatbots for Messaging Services; Banks, airlines and others are developing virtual-software assistants to interact like humans

By Kim S. Nash 
870 words
3 November 2016
16:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Six months ago, Facebook Inc. said it would open its Messenger service, making it possible for businesses to build virtual assistants that can chat with the one billion-plus real people who use the communications platform each month.

Since then, developers have created more than 33,000 such "chatbots," which have icons and nicknames that at first glance might not appear that different from a friend or relative in a user's contact list.

Last month, Mastercard Inc. launched Kai, a bot for banks that makes it possible for Facebook Messenger users to check activity on their credit, debit and loan accounts and set up financial-management tools. Another Mastercard bot for merchants lets customers make purchases by sending a Facebook message and paying with Mastercard's digital wallet.

Such capabilities may be just the beginning for bots. A range of companies are betting that the convergence of messaging platforms, chatbots and increasingly powerful artificial intelligence will lead to the creation of a more natural interface between machines and humans.

"We are thinking of an intelligence center that will help you get to the right bot at the right time so you don't have to have a sea of icons that are sitting there that you don't know how to use," says Stan Chudnovsky, vice president and head of product for Messenger at Facebook.

Mr. Chudnovsky joined Facebook almost two years ago from PayPal Holdings Inc., where he was vice president of global strategy.

Mr. Chudnovsky has played a role in Silicon Valley since the 1990s, and has launched or helped fund startups sold to Monster Worldwide, Amazon.com Inc. and PayPal.

Facebook is using its power to accelerate the growth of messaging and bots. In 2014, it pushed users to employ Messenger for direct messages, and the platform now has more than one billion users. That same year, Facebook acquired for $19 billion WhatsApp messaging service, which also now has one billion users.

April's debut of development tools for building Messenger bots has appealed to startups and established brands alike. Some users have complained that initial Facebook Messenger bots are slow, with limited conversational capabilities. "There are growing pains," said Robin Chan, chief executive of Operator, a startup that makes shopping bots.

Facebook is meeting with developers and recently updated its tools to, for example, further customize conversation threads. "It's very early days for bots," Mr. Chudnovsky said. He predicts that within perhaps two years, people and bots powered by artificial intelligence will conduct complex dialogs.

A Facebook user planning a vacation, for example, could send his criteria to Messenger, which would match the information to bots from travel-company partners. Partners would reply with offers.

The goal for many companies is to build intelligent software agents that understand natural language and field multifaceted requests such as, "Help me rent a car in Philadelphia two Saturdays from now." Some bots, learning the habits of their human masters, may anticipate work. "Your monthly golf date isn't yet scheduled. Would you like to call Kiawah Island?"

This is beyond today's simple bots that answer trivia questions or reorder laundry detergent based on data from prior purchases, says Roberto Masiero, senior vice president of innovation labs at Automatic Data Processing Inc.

"It's a tectonic shift in how we interact with computers," Mr. Masiero said.

Mr. Masiero predicts that bots will become the new interface for corporate software. Rather than launching individual software programs to change health benefits, say, or start a retirement account, employees could consult with bots that have learned their habits and use algorithms and machine learning to make suggestions, he said.

ADP software engineers are working with social anthropologists and copywriters to tinker with bot behavior. Tests show that for bots to earn human acceptance, they must not act too much like people or too much like computers. Bots that refer to themselves in the third person sound unnatural, Mr. Masiero said. "I" and "we" work better. Bots that fire replies in milliseconds are unnerving, he said. His team has programmed delays into response time so that a bot replies no sooner than two seconds after a question.

"These emotional triggers are things we need to care about now," he said.

ADP aims to pilot bot interfaces on some of its human-resources software next year.

At American Airlines Group Inc., Chief Information Officer Maya Leibman said bots may improve customer service by providing faster access to information and a more consistent experience for fliers.

Developers at a recent tech conference at the airline demonstrated how to use Amazon's Echo, a voice-activated bot device, to check in for flights and retrieve other information. "We're a ways away from production but we are definitely excited about the future of [bot] technology," Ms. Leibman said.

"Companies need to see,  Oh my god, the convergence of bots and AI is an important investment for us to make,'" Mr. Chudnovsky at Facebook said. "There is a lot of work ahead but we feel like this is happening."

Regulator Compares Banks to Taxi Business Rattled by Uber

18:49 ET - The head regulator for national banks, Thomas Curry, during a speech in London, drew parallels between how emerging financial technology is disrupting the banking system to how Uber "upended" the taxi business. "If Uber turned your smartphone into a taxi dispatch, fintech is turning your phone into a financial advisor, a loan officer, a money transmitter, and an automated teller," he said in remarks prepared for a conference. There are more than 4,000 fintech companies and 99 million millennials in the US and United Kingdom who "are more open to new technologies and more willing to switch providers," he noted. "The US federal banking system is strong and vibrant, but to remain capable of serving the changing needs and preferences of customers, banks--and the banking system--must adapt." (Rachel.witkowski@wsj.com; @RachelWitkowski)

Analyst Ties Income Inequality Problems to Fed

18:27 - The Federal Reserve should stop studying how its monetary and regulatory policies may exacerbate income inequality and start acting. That's according to a speech expected to be delivered tomorrow by Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics. Petrou, in remarks prepared for a conference hosted by the Chicago Fed, outlines how she believes financial regulations, including capital and liquidity requirements, are having the unintended effect of depressing bank lending and hurting economic growth, which is worsening the gap between rich and poor Americans. Regulators should "act incrementally to solve problems," and continue making adjustments rather than taking a wait-and-see approach, Petrou argues. (Ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Continuing to rely exclusively on unconventional monetary policy will only prolong a massive transfer of wealth from savers to borrowers." (leslie.scism@wsj.com)

Vice Chairman Thomas Hoeing tells German business newspaper Handelsblatt it would be "very difficult" to reinstate the Glass-Steagall Act, a Depression-era law that separates commercial banking and securities activities at Wall Street banks. "Could you do it? Of course, you can do it." But he said there's still not enough support to break up the largest US financial institutions. "I don't see that as the likely path forward," he says. Keep Eligible Buyers of Bail-In Debt Broad, Says Banker

1451 GMT The list of those who can buy securities that would take a loss when a bank fails should be kept broad, says Mark Venus, Head of Recovery and Resolution Planning at BNP Paribas Thursday in Berlin. "Bail-in is an intelligent form of insolvency for banks," he says. "Insolvency hurts. It should hurt for all the stakeholders." He stresses that insured depositors "deserve complete protection," but those who are using the banks for investments, rather than simply for payments don't deserve special protection. If the list of those who can buy such securities is kept too narrow, then the loss risk would get concentrated on too small a group, raising the prospect that it would be declared too painful on that group to take the loss. (Todd.Buell@wsj.com, @ToddBuell)
