AudienceView Ticketing Corp., which provides e-commerce software for events and entertainment companies, said it received a growth investment from Rubicon Technology Partners.

Toronto-based AudienceView offers products for ticketing, customer relationship management, fundraising, e-commerce, marketing and analytics, among other services. The company serves customers in the sports, arts, live event and educational campuses areas.

AudienceView has more than $2 billion in annual transactions, according to a news release about the investment. The company serves 550 venues across 15 countries with ticketing and e-commerce services for events.

In 2014, AudienceView said it closed a new round of equity financing led by Canadian venture-capital firm Five Corners Capital. Another Canadian venture firm Ventures West Capital lists AudienceView as an investment on its website.

AudienceView retained Pagemill Partners as its financial adviser, according to a news release about the investment, terms of which weren't disclosed.

Rubicon, which has offices in Menlo Park, Calif., and Stamford, Conn., makes control equity investments of between $30 million to $75 million in lower midmarket enterprise software companies with revenue between $25 million and $100 million, according to the firm's website.

The ticketing and live event sector has been an area of investment for private-equity firms this year. In January, Vista Equity Partners said it would invest in Vivid Seats Ltd., a secondary tickets retailer. In early October, CVC Growth Partners and New Amsterdam Growth Capital announced they would invest $75 million in DTI Management, which provides live event ticket technology to partners including StubHub Inc. and Ticketmaster.

Michaels's Take: Next SEC Chief Could Face Confirmation Fight; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Thursday, November 3, 2016. 

2,326 words

The Wall Street Journal Online

Michaels's Take: Next SEC Chief Could Face Confirmation Fight

The Barred Brokers in Our Midst

Pro Interactive: How the Election Could Reshape Financial Policy

U.K. Lawmakers Must Vote on Brexit, High Court Rules

New Mortgage Rule Irks German Banks

Next SEC Chief Could Face Confirmation Fight

The end of a turbulent presidential campaign next week promises to invigorate the next source of conflict in Washington: White House appointments to lead federal departments and agencies.

While lawmakers sometimes clash over Supreme Court appointments or candidates for high-profile cabinet roles, it's uncommon to see divisions over lesser-known nominees, such as whoever is chosen to lead the Securities and Exchange Commission.

But that could be an outgrowth of next week's election. Depending on who occupies the White House and which party controls the Senate, the SEC nomination could spark a fight over an issue that incites passions in both parties: requiring public companies to disclose the funds they spend on politics.

Senate Democrats have insisted that political expenditures fall into the category of information that shareholders should know about a company, along with items such as its financial statements, the identity of its board of directors, and legal settlements with the government. The SEC has broad powers to tailor disclosure requirements, but Congress has intervened and required specific reporting on matters such as a company's "http://www.wsj.com/articles/schlesingers-take-how-the-sec-boosted-mine-safety-1464348472" or its use of "http://blogs.wsj.com/corruption-currents/2012/09/20/secs-conflict-minerals-rules-open-companies-to-activists/".

Opponents of a political-spending rule say the expenses are trivial for companies and immaterial to investment decisions. Laws that govern what information must flow to shareholders turn on the concept of materiality, meaning investors would consider the information within a decision to buy or sell the stock.

Friction over using the SEC to reveal more about corporate political spending has already bogged down the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-nominee-caught-in-crossfire-over-corporate-political-spending-1460410963" to join the agency as commissioners. In mid-October, Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) "http://www.wsj.com/articles/elizabeth-warren-to-obama-fire-sec-chief-mary-jo-white-1476439200" over her unwillingness to write such a regulation.

"Due to investor demand and Senate pressure this rule has justifiably become a linchpin issue and it is now one of the things that is and should be a test," said Lisa Gilbert, director of the Congress Watch division at the liberal advocacy group Public Citizen, who supports the call for a rule. "It seems clear that any plausible candidate for the SEC would need to have the right answer to this to get through this process."

That will certainly be the case if Hillary Clinton becomes president and Democrats gain control of the Senate. (Mrs. Clinton "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hillary-clinton-unveils-campaign-finance-plan-1441684802" that she would support the SEC writing such a rule.) On the other hand, the issue could be moot if Donald Trump wins.

If Mrs. Clinton prevails but Republicans still control the Senate, GOP senators would be highly motivated to reject a nominee who appears friendly to the regulation. Senate Majority Leader Mitch McConnell is a longtime opponent of campaign-finance restrictions who has equated political giving with free speech.

If Republicans hang on to the Senate, "the SEC can no longer become the playground of political polarization," said Tom Quaadman, executive vice president of the U.S. Chamber of Commerce's Center for Capital Markets Competitiveness, which opposes SEC involvement in campaign-finance disclosures.

"http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/The-Election-And-Regulation/"

"http://graphics.wsj.com/elections/2016/where-do-clinton-and-trump-stand-on-wall-street/"

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Pro First: The Barred Brokers in Our Midst

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-barred-brokers-in-our-midst-1478165406" who were permanently or temporarily barred by regulators from working at a brokerage firm have begun new careers as investment advisers. The transition is legal, but the nuances between the professions of broker and investment adviser can be lost on mom-and-pop investors, experts say, potentially exposing them to bad actors.

The former brokers, whose alleged misdeeds run from violations of brokerage-industry rules to outright fraud, can benefit from the fragmented landscape that governs investment advice in the U.S. Brokers are overseen by an industry self-regulator, the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority, and have to give only "suitable" advice on investments. Investment advisers are overseen by state regulators and the Securities and Exchange Commission and are held to a "fiduciary" standard that requires them to put clients' interests ahead of their own.

(This article is available to Pro Financial Regulation subscribers first, before being published to wsj.com.)

WSJ Pro Interactive: How the Election Could Reshape Financial Policy

The 2016 election has the potential to influence financial policy in significant ways, from new laws to new officials deciding how existing rules are implemented. Since first winning the presidency in 2008, Democrat Barack Obama, along with Congress, has rewritten the rules for American finance. His administration and appointees have continued to do so, even after Republicans gained new clout to shape the debate by taking the House in the 2010 elections and full control of Congress by winning the Senate in 2014.

Explore how different outcomes in the Nov. 8 elections could affect how Washington handles key financial regulatory issues. "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/The-Election-And-Regulation/", such as President Hillary Clinton, a Democratic-led Senate and a Republican-led House, or President Donald Trump and a GOP-controlled Congress.

U.K. Lawmakers Must Vote on Brexit, High Court Rules

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/brexit-legal-challenge-set-for-court-ruling-1478159686" Thursday that the government can't trigger negotiations to leave the European Union without a vote from Parliament, dealing a blow to Prime Minister Theresa May's plans.

The pound surged against the dollar following the decision. The government could now be forced to get parliamentary approval to trigger Article 50, the formal mechanism that begins a two-year window for exit negotiations.

SEC Probing Wells Fargo Around Sales-Practice Disclosures

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-probing-wells-fargo-around-sales-practice-disclosures-1478127877" whether Wells Fargo & Co. violated rules around investor disclosures and other matters relating to its recent sales tactics scandal, people familiar with the matter said.

The SEC sent requests for information to the bank asking for documents in recent weeks, following three Democratic senators' calls in late September for the SEC to investigate whether Wells Fargo misled investors and violated whistleblower protections while allegedly engaged in illegal sales practices, one of these people said. The probes are in an "early stage," this person added.

New Mortgage Rule Irks German Banks

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/new-mortgage-rule-irks-german-banks-1478165403" have come under fire from critics who say they choke off the flow of home loans in Europe's biggest economy and represent an overreaction to the 2008-09 financial crisis.

Lawmakers say the European Union's Mortgage Credit Directive protects consumers. Still, Berlin is considering a change following data indicating a sharp decline in mortgages since a German version of the rule took effect in March. European authorities agreed to the rules in February 2014 and Germany had until March of this year to make them national law.

U.K. Banking Watchdog to Set New Rules for Current Accounts

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-banking-watchdog-to-set-new-rules-for-current-accounts-1478160561" Thursday announced a plan to improve competition in the market for current accounts at retail banks, following a series of recommendations by the Competition and Markets Authority as part of its investigation into retail banking.

The regulator said it will consider rules needed to improve transparency for overdraft users and plans to start collecting data next year. It will also look into promoting innovation in the sector, as well as improving service and prompting increased customer engagement.

Regulators Take Action Against  Massive' Debt Collection Operation

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/regulators-take-action-against-massive-debt-collection-operation-1478115558"sued the leaders of a debt-collection operation Wednesday, alleging the two men have made "tens of millions of dollars annually" by directing a nationwide business based in Buffalo, N.Y., that "harassed, threatened, and deceived" millions of consumers.

The two defendants, Douglas MacKinnon and Mark Gray, directed the network of debt-collectors to intimidate consumers to get them to pay inflated debts or amounts they may not owe, according to the federal Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and New York Attorney General Eric Schneiderman.

Futures Exchanges Shift Rules on Block Trades in Win for Energy Companies

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/futures-exchanges-shift-rules-on-block-trades-in-win-for-energy-companies-1478105204"Inc. are loosening their rules on trades that involve large blocks of futures executed off exchanges.

Block trades are big futures transactions negotiated between two companies. They are crucial to U.S. energy markets, where a significant portion of trading in recent years has shifted away from over-the-counter swaps into block trades. Both are private deals often agreed over the phone, but in block trades, the two companies doing the deal allow a futures exchange to step in as a middleman.

Eight Financial Firms Fined Over Type of Variable-Annuity Contract

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/eight-financial-firms-fined-over-type-of-variable-annuity-contract-1478121267"firms more than $6 million and ordered five of them to return to customers more than $6 million following questionable sales of a type of variable-annuity contract.

Variable annuities are widely used for retirement saving. The contracts at the heart of the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority's action are so-called L-shares, a type of mutual fund shares that are held in variable annuities and are designed for short-term investors, offering shorter surrender periods in exchange for higher fees.

French Prosecutors Say HSBC Should Stand Trial for Alleged Tax Fraud

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/french-prosecutors-say-hsbc-should-stand-trial-for-alleged-tax-fraud-1478168745" should stand trial for allegedly helping people evade taxes in France, according to a person familiar with the matter, putting further pressure on the U.K.-based bank.

Magistrates placed HSBC under formal investigation last year as part of a widening probe into whether the bank helped recruit customers in France, possibly breaching laws authorizing only French-registered lenders to sign up customers in the country. The investigation also examined whether the bank was complicit in laundering the proceeds of tax evasion.

Banks No Longer Make the Bulk of U.S. Mortgages

Taking their place are nonbank lenders more willing to make riskier loans banks now shun.

Independent mortgage lenders have come close to the 50% mark before. But they haven't topped it in recent history, even in the run-up to the housing bust, making this a notable moment for the most important consumer-credit market.

Revamping Regulatory Processes

The U.S. regulatory system would benefit from further protections against regulatory capture and more expedited processes, "https://www.americanprogress.org/issues/economy/reports/2016/11/01/291499/reforming-regulation/"from the liberal Center for American Progress. The report cites research showing that regulatory actions are typically skewed towards special interest groups as a result of regulatory capture, whether via the revolving door of industry members taking jobs as regulators (and vice versa) and the comment process, which is heavily weighted towards industry perspectives.

Privacy, Not Crime, Drives Demand for Cryptocurrency

While bitcoin promises to provide a secure and anonymous way to make payments, analytics companies, many of whom are working with government research grants, have gotten "really good at exposing users' identities," "https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2016-11-01/bitcoin-isn-t-anonymous-enough". The U.S. government has an interest in decoding cryptocurrency user identities for various law enforcement reasons, including stopping money laundering, drug dealing and tax evasion.

Technology
China Circuit: For Safety's Sake, Venture Investors Go Offline

By Li Yuan 
832 words
3 November 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

With a cooling and uncertain market for tech startups, Chinese venture investors who once searched for China's GrubHub or Uber are hunting for China's Starbucks.

Heekcaa Lab, a chain of 50 upscale teahouses based in southern Guangdong province, in August received 100 million yuan, or about $15 million, from a pair of backers including IDG Capital Partners, one of the most well-known venture firms in China thanks in part to early investment in search giant Baidu and video site Tudou. Nie Yunchen, Heekcaa's 25-year-oldfounder, has grand ambitions to make traditional tea hip with the young masses the way Starbucks did for coffee in China.

One of Heekcaa's rivals in that effort is Inwe Cha, which a month earlier received 500 million yuan in funding from Richard Liu, founder and chief executive of Chinese e-commerce company JD.com.

Nontech companies aren't a new concept for venture investors in China, but they have recently drawn much more interest for several reasons.

As with their Silicon Valley counterparts, China's venture firms have raised large amounts of capital they need to deploy. While some technology areas, such as artificial intelligence, remain hot, investors generally in the past year have grown cautious regarding tech startups after years of intense interest.

Restaurant chains and other traditional consumer companies also offer a play on China's young, free-spending consumers. In a slowing economy, many of these companies also boast something tech startups often lack: strong revenue and a clear path to profit.

Mr. Nie, for instance, says his Heekcaa chain, which operates under the company name Shenzhen Magcc Catering Management, brings in revenue averaging more than one million yuan a month per store and has never been unprofitable. Its tea drinks sell for 20 to 25 yuan apiece.

With Wheat, a high-end bakery chain in Beijing that received an undisclosed amount of funding from investor Sinovation Ventures in March, says it never opens a store unless it can garner revenue of at least one million yuan a month.

The business is profitable, says co-founder Ye Jiazhi.

Gao Hongqing at Fortune Venture Capital, which manages 20 billion yuan, says the "crazy hot" investments in the past few years in startups that use subsidies to acquire users for ride-hailing and other sharing-economy services aren't sustainable, because their customers are bargain hunters and have no loyalty. "Low prices and subsidies are passe," he says. "It's time to return to the fundamentals." Fortune has invested in a chain of handmade-candy stores and an indoor-playground chain, among others.

Such offline plays can be "safer projects for investors as we're looking for the next big trend," said Wu Shichun, founding partner of Plum Ventures. Mr. Wu's firm, an early-stage investor focused on technology and media, has invested in nontech startups including a custom ice-cream shop in Beijing.

Venture capitalists say domestic investors who put money into yuan-denominated funds increasingly prefer less-risky investments. Zhang Ying, a partner at Sinovation Ventures, says that for investors seeking to exit by having their portfolio companies list on China's A-share markets, strong financial performance is crucial because Chinese securities regulations have profitability requirements for public listing. Besides With Wheat, Sinovation has backed a hair-salon chain and a hotpot-delivery service.

Investors in these nontech businesses are considering access to relatively wealthy -- but picky -- young consumers. A report by consultants BCG and Alibaba Group Holding's AliResearch Institute projected that by 2020, the number of upper-middle-class and affluent households in China -- those with annual disposable income of $24,000 or higher -- would double to 100 million and account for 30% of all urban households. That compares with 17% in 2015 and 7% in 2010.

Growth in Chinese consumption over the next five years would roughly equal a market 1.3 times the size of Germany or the U.K., said the December report. It also found that upper-middle-class Chinese consumers 35 and younger spend an average of 40% more than previous-generation consumers with similar incomes, while being more brand- and health-conscious.

Zhang Haiyan, a partner at Tiantu Capital, a private-equity firm known for its consumer-related investments, says he is surprised by how freely his younger colleagues spend. "Sometimes I worry if we pay them enough to support their lifestyle," he jokes. "Other times I wonder if we overpay them."

Zeng Xi, a 27-year-old product manager at a smartphone maker in the southern city Shenzhen, says he made several trips to Heekcaa outlets outside Shenzhen before the chain opened in Shenzhen, drawn by its hip decor and innovative drinks.

"Heekcaa meets a uniquely Chinese demand," he says.

MARKETS & INVESTING
Goldman takes stake in derivatives start-up; Equities

By Philip Stafford 
381 words
2 November 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
18
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A US financial technology company that aims to keep derivative traders out of trouble has won the backing of Goldman Sachs, Wells Fargo and high-speed trader DRW Holdings in its first fundraising.

New York-based Droit Financial Technologies has secured $16m to accelerate marketing of its software, which automates compliance with complex regulations in the derivatives market introduced in the wake of the financial crisis.

Droit's fundraising underlines a wave of interest from market participants in so-called "regtech" or regulation technology in trading that apply artificial intelligence and data crunching to real-time monitoring of financial services.

It is the first time Droit, founded in 2012 by a group of former derivative traders, quantitative analysts and technology specialists, has needed outside funds. The company had been profitable for the past 18 months, according to its chief executive Satya Pemmaraju , a former fixed income and derivatives trader at UBS.

Waves of rules covering all aspects of trading , from advanced risk checks to business conduct and reporting, are forcing banks and asset managers into significant IT overhauls. At the moment, most banks carry out compliance with their fragmented IT systems.

Reading data rapidly, the software will ensure that each trade has met a checklist of compliance requirements. Droit has "an excellent opportunity to become the industry standard for regulatory-compliant decision engines," said Joanne Hannaford, managing director of technology at Goldman.

Droit will focus on the industry's preparations for Mifid II, a sprawling piece of European legislation designed to toughen investor protections and make bond and over-the-counter markets more transparent. The rules will come into force from the start of 2018 . It also wants to open an office in Singapore.

"It's a very fast scaling over the next 18 months and we've got the backing of some very strategic partners," said Mr Pemmaraju.

Financial regulations apply to every single transaction, and those deals are executed in thousandths of a second, so rules needed to be applied to data in real-time, and in differing legal jurisdictions, Mr Pemmaraju said.

Pivot Investment Partners, a US venture capital firm, also participated in the fundraising. The four will collectively take a minority stake in Droit.

Citi takes a small step towards cracking the glass ceiling

Kate Burgess 
790 words
2 November 2016
20:50
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

ET, hero of the 1982 blockbuster celebrating otherness and openness to new experiences, was not a boy or a girl. ET was a plant.

Nearly 35 years later Citigroup spinners are celebrating the bank s openness through the promotion of Ebru Pakcan, a woman and a Turkish national. She is to head up treasury and trade finance in Europe and will sit on the bank s top executive committee in the region.

Ms Pakcan is not a plant but she is  other  in Citi. FT figures suggest more than four-fifths of the bank s senior employees are men.

And while she has not risen out of the testosterone-soaked, Ferrari-driving divisions such as investment banking, she has come up through another stereotypically blokish route   IT.

Banks are inching gingerly through the glass ceiling. Next stop for executives at corporate top tables is a seat on main boards.

That said, it is not quite a full hurrah. Ms Pakcan will be the fourth woman on a 22-member exec committee in Europe, Middle East and Africa.

The fact remains that there are still too few female bosses   about 26 per cent of the directors of UK s top 100 listed companies are daughters of Eve. And that is only part of the bigger drive to opening out British boards and freeing them from the  male, stale and pale  stereotype.

A government-backed report on diversity from Sir John Parker, chairman of miner Anglo American, shows that just 8 per cent of directors of the UK s biggest and most global businesses are other than white. Shame, in part because it underrepresents the 14 per cent of the British populace who don t define themselves as white. But the main point is to stop boards being comfortable billets for clubby types swapping war stories over the port and cigars. There is a clear business case for creating heterogenous boards to mirror the diversity of companies  customers.

Companies need to broaden the outlook of top teams, whether by recruiting from Mars or Venus   or indeed photosynthesising organisms, as long as they are not vegetables or wallflowers.

Scent of Persimmon

To the list of investment bubbles   which has included 19th century railways, 18th century South Sea merchants and 17th century tulips   may now be added another play on transport, trade and bulbous fibrous things: Chinese garlic. FT colleagues report that snowy weather and speculators have almost doubled its price in a year, writes Matthew Vincent.

But no one has ever suggested the bubble list should contain condensing boilers despite a similarly short-term spike in demand. That is largely because this demand was state engineered. In 2010, boiler sales leapt when 125,000 householders were given 400 vouchers to buy more efficient central heating (one over-enthusiastic Glaswegian plumber was fined 180,000 for making 2m unsolicited sales calls).

How, then, should investors treat a 19 per cent increase in Persimmon s new home sales in the last quarter, when 45 per cent involve the government s Help to Buy loan scheme, compared with 36 per cent nationally? Some say it explains why Persimmon has bucked a slowing mortgage market and architects  disdain for its  rabbit-hutch  homes. However, with operating margins nearing 24 per cent and not forecast to rise much higher, the forward indicator is perhaps not so much government largesse as patience with Persimmon s  cautious  land use. New-build prices, like that of garlic, are more about supply than taste.

Admiral red-Facebooked

Admiral became a big company through cheesy, cheap TV adverts and shaking up the staid world of insurance. On Wednesday it was itself shaken.

It had planned a big product launch that would mine the Facebook posts of 17 to 21 year olds   with their permission   to assess their risk as drivers.  Firstcarquote  sounded like an undergraduate s social science thesis: too few full stops or comments written in the pub would prompt premium quotes to soar.

But on launch day, Facebook became suddenly queasy about giving the insurer access to the data for commercial use and blocked the product. Perhaps the prospect of a public relations disaster loomed.

Or was Facebook trying to save the youth of today from their poor punctuation and inexperience? Lombard lost 20 at college by being the last one into the ubiquitous pyramid scheme decades ago. If Facebook had been around then, Lombard s lesson might have been less painful. Today, the big data question is who owns and controls information and what if users of social networks and customers don t want to be saved?

Markets
Privacy, Not Crime, Drives Demand for Cryptocurrency; New digital currencies have entered the market promising anonymous transactions.

166 words
2 November 2016
19:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

November 1, 2016

While bitcoin promises to provide a secure and anonymous way to make payments, analytics companies, many of whom are working with government research grants, have gotten "really good at exposing users' identities," "https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2016-11-01/bitcoin-isn-t-anonymous-enough". The U.S. government has an interest in decoding cryptocurrency user identities for various law enforcement reasons, including stopping money laundering, drug dealing and tax evasion. But since that undermines one of the primary purposes of cryptocurrency privacy other digital currencies have entered the market promising decentralized, anonymous transactions, writes Ms. Ou, a blockchain engineer at Global Financial Access, a fintech company in San Francisco. They are not doing so to facilitate criminal activity, she says, but rather to meet that market desire for private transactions. Criminal activity will continue to occur whether or not the government manages to unmask certain cryptocurrencies, she says.

--Gabriel T. Rubin

Business News: Humana Calls on Artificial Coach

By Kim S. Nash

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Humana Inc. has deployed artificial-intelligence software trained to detect conversational cues to coach call-center agents and supervisors in real-time, when calls with customers are going awry.

The software, built with AI tools from Cogito Corp., can, for example, recognize increasing agitation by "listening" for markers such as a steady rise in the pitch of a customer's voice or instances of a customer and an agent talking over each other. The digital coach, called Cogito Dialog, will then send an electronic alert, such as "speaking quickly" or "frequent overlaps," to prompt the agent to change technique.

Empathy, said Geeta Wilson, director of consumer experience, is a competitive advantage.

"Usually when folks are interacting with us, it's about procedures and medications," she said. "A health-care company has to have empathy and compassion."

As AI advances spur the first generation of commercial applications, called chat bots, based around natural-language processing, researchers and technology companies are studying how such systems can better detect conversational cues. One goal is to develop a better rapport with users.

Increasingly affordable artificial intelligence tools are also making possible software to help with customer service beyond systems that respond to keywords, said David Nichols, a principal at Ernst & Young LLC.

At Humana, Ms. Wilson is trying to build systems that learn and can predict customer behavior.

"We want to know what the consumer is going to do before they do it, so it feels like we know them," she said.

To build digital coaches, Humana developers earlier this year fed data from 100,000 customer service calls into the AI system, to analyze for such markers as pauses, key words and interruptions, then associated them with the outcomes of the interactions. Now digital coaches, built with tools from machine-learning startup Cogito Corp., listen for stress in live conversations and send real-time suggestions to an agent to talk less, for example, or moderate his tone.

"Those alerts help agents determine how they should change the conversation to make sure the member feels like they're being heard." Ms. Wilson said.

Pro Private Markets
Chatbot Platform Octane AI Raises $1.5M

By Cat Zakrzewski 
618 words
2 November 2016
18:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Octane AI Inc. on Wednesday launched its service that aims to make creating a chatbot for Facebook Inc.'s Messenger as simple as creating a website with WordPress.

The startup has partnered with the artists 50 Cent, Aerosmith and KISS as well as the Justin Bieber-backed Shots Studios.

To interact with an Octane-powered bot, people can go to one of the Facebook pages of Octane's partners and send a message.

The startup also said it has raised $1.5 million in funding led by General Catalyst Partners and others, including Boost VC, Dimension 6, Ustream Inc. founder Brad Hunstable, Hired Inc. co-founder Allan Grant and angel investor Lawrence Braitman.

The number of chatbots has ballooned in recent months, yet some people have remained unimpressed with the technology because most bots are unable to handle conversations. They rely on technology such as natural language processing that requires many conversations to train the bots.

Social media managers at Octane's partners can choose and customize plugins for a dashboard of features, such as merchandise to offer. Octane then allows its partners to quickly offer merchandise from the chatbots or even send notifications or schedule meetings.

For instance, the 50 Cent bot provides links to new music or videos, or allows users to buy G-Unit hats. The bot includes fire and gun emojis in its messages.

Octane's customers can also write the text of the automated messages to reflect their brands. The Octane-powered Aerosmith bot allows people to find tour dates or join the band's fan club, Aero Force One. It says phrases like "Hope you're havin a ROCKIN day!"

Octane's push to be the underlying bot platform comes about six months after Facebook showed developers how to build chatbots at its F8 Conference. Chatbots allow people to ask simple customer service questions or order goods through a conversational interface.

Facebook Messenger had zero bots in February 2016 and more than 18,000 by July 2016, according to Forrester Research Inc.

Octane's founders say that chatbots will become essential to every business. "Over time messaging apps are probably going to be the most popular way that people communicate, over email or anything else," said Octane co-founder Matt Schlicht. "And if that's the case, then it makes sense that every business is going to need to figure out how to interact with those people."

Mr. Schlicht, a former Ustream product manager, has been closely watching chatbots, and he launched the online publication Chatbots Magazine earlier this year. He also manages the largest Facebook group of chatbot developers. Mr. Schlicht launched Octane in May with former Mashable co-editor Ben Parr and Omegle creator Leif K. Brooks.

Octane AI isn't the only company attempting to be the backbone of chatbots. Y Combinator-backed Chatfuel also has a bot-building platform, supporting bots for companies like Uber Technologies Inc. and tech industry publication TechCrunch.

Mr. Schlicht joked that he wishes Facebook Chief Executive Mark Zuckerberg had set people's expectations lower and said, "Today, bots are stupid" when he announced support for third-party chatbots on Facebook Messenger.

People have come to think that chatbots are a form of artificially intelligent business representative that is very capable, he said. The reality, he added, is that businesses need to readjust their expectations and treat chatbots like lightweight mobile applications.

"They're very basic, they're very simple, but they can still provide a lot of value for you, even if they're not like AI," Mr. Schlicht said.

Jitters on Inflation Batter Bond ETFs

By Chris Dieterich 
717 words
2 November 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The bond market's October retreat fueled record withdrawals from some popular exchange-traded funds, the latest sign of investor anxiety over inflation.

Investors pulled $998 million last Thursday from iShares iBoxx High Yield Corporate Bond ETF, the oldest and largest junk-bond ETF. That is the largest daily withdrawal on record, said BlackRock Inc., the ETF's provider.

Investors also pulled $1.7 billion last week from the iShares iBoxx $ Investment Grade Corporate Bond ETF, the biggest weekly outflow since its inception in 2002.

The redemptions coincided with a global rout in government bonds that sent the yield on the 10-year U.S. Treasury note to its highest levels since late May. On Tuesday, the 10-year Treasury yield fell to 1.822%, as prices rose.

Bond investors are bracing for an interest-rate increase from the Federal Reserve and grappling with signs of rising inflation, which erodes the fixed value of bond payouts over time, in the U.S. and globally.

The BlackRock funds weren't the only ones under pressure. All told, investors pulled $2.2 billion from junk-bond ETFs last week, the most in six weeks, according to XTF, a market-analytics firm.

The withdrawals follow large gains this year in bond funds, reflecting a sharp rise in corporate-bond prices.

In 2016 through Monday, the iShares iBoxx High Yield Corporate Bond ETF has returned 11.3%, while the iShares iBoxx $ Investment Grade Corporate Bond ETF has returned 8.8%, according to Morningstar. These compare with a 5.9% return, including dividends, for the S&P 500.

"The market has been seduced into this reach-for-yield mode, and a lot of this is central-bank-policy driven," said Thomas Urano, head of taxable fixed-income portfolio management at Sage Advisory Services. "Reaching for yields blinds investors to what's happening with fundamentals, and it seems to me prudent for investors to lock in gains."

For some investors such as Mr. Urano, 2016's snapback in high-yield bonds smacked more of investors chasing big payouts than it did meaningful improvement in corporate health for junk-bond issuers.

Fund investors' piling into corporate bonds has some market watchers on edge, worried that spates of fund-related selling could exacerbate market swings. Fully 70% of the high-yield bond market, or $922 billion in assets, is held by mutual funds and separately managed accounts, an investment structure favored by financial advisers, according to recent research from Stephen Caprio and Matthew Mish, credit strategists at UBS Group AG.

Some 46% of the U.S. investment-grade market, or $2.7 trillion, is controlled by funds and SMAs. At the same time, high-profile investors, including Bill Gross, Howard Marks and Carl Icahn, have voiced concerns about potential liquidity mismatches between fast-trading ETFs and hard-to-trade corporate bonds.

Fund companies have long maintained that ETFs create more liquidity for the bond market at a time when traditional dealers -- banks -- find it increasingly unprofitable to hold bond inventories and match buyers and sellers.

"Outflows were orderly and [the high-yield and investment-grade ETFs] performed as clients have come to expect," a BlackRock spokeswoman said.

More broadly, recent large outflows are a sign investors are taking chips off the table as uncertainty about central-bank policy looms, said Chris Johnson, head of U.S. ETF distribution at RBC Capital Markets. Mr. Johnson said that, unlike earlier in the year, when corporate-bond ETF outflows were accompanied by hedging activity, recent withdrawals "signal that big, real money is thinking that this could be a tipping point for the asset class and are moving out or trimming their positions."

Jeff Knight, global head of investment solutions at Columbia Threadneedle Investments, said he isn't yet selling corporate credits because be believes they will hold up better than Treasurys after interest rates march higher. Still, he said that the rally for high-yield and investment-grade bonds is likely nearing its end.

"For us, it's too late to buy and too early to sell," Mr. Knight said. "I can see the case for risk-averse investors shifting away because there's not a lot of upside left."

Election 2016: Wall Street Targeted in Races

By Donna Borak 
747 words
2 November 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Eight years after the financial crisis, anti-Wall Street rhetoric remains a staple of congressional campaigns across the country, with Democrats and Republicans alike launching bank-bashing television ads in nearly three dozen competitive races.

In the hotly contested Indiana Senate election, for example, Democrat Evan Bayh's recent stints as an asset manager and as a regional-bank director are being used as reasons not to vote for him. "Evan Bayh went from the Banking Committee to a big bank boardroom. . . .," says the narrator of a 30-second ad paid for by the Senate Leadership Fund, a super PAC linked to Senate Majority Leader Mitch McConnell (R., Ky.). The spot brands Mr. Bayh "Wall Street's dream senator."

"That's pretty rich," said Bayh spokesman Ben Ray. "Evan voted for the toughest Wall Street reform since the Great Depression," he said, referring to the politician's backing for the 2010 Dodd-Frank overhaul act during his previous Senate term.

Kantar Media's Campaign Media Analysis Group, an analytics firm that monitors advertising in close contests, identified for The Wall Street Journal 34 Senate and House races in 25 states where candidates have used "Wall Street" or "banks" as invectives to try to tarnish their opponents.

That is a decline from the past two presidential-election cycles, said Steven Passwaiter, a Kantar vice president. Still, he added, "Wall Street is always a convenient tool. In these tightly contested races, portraying candidates as catering to the big banks can be used to swing enough votes."

Similar sentiments have coursed through the presidential race this year. Vermont Sen. Bernie Sanders regularly criticized Hillary Clinton during the Democratic primary for the close ties he said she and her husband, former President Bill Clinton, had cultivated with the financial industry.

Her Republican rival, Donald Trump, has echoed that rhetoric, blasting her during their final debate for her ads "paid for by your friends on Wall Street that gave so much money because they know you're going to protect them."

Mrs. Clinton has made strengthening financial regulation a core plank of her platform, while also pledging to enforce rules aimed at curbing Wall Street pay and giving enforcement agencies more resources to "prosecute wrongdoing."

While Mr. Bayh and Mrs. Clinton are both under fire for their finance connections, most of the targets are Republicans. Like Mr. Bayh, Pennsylvania Sen. Pat Toomey also faces harsh television ads alluding to his career before politics as an investment banker in the 1980s, calling him a "Wall Street wheeler-dealer."

"While on the Senate Banking Committee, Pat Toomey voted to rewrite rules to help bankers like him and voted to eliminate protections put into place to stop Wall Street's risky practices," says an ad released Oct. 7 by his Democratic challenger, Katie McGinty.

Toomey campaign spokesman Ted Kwong said the ad gives a misleading impression of the Republican lawmaker's record,saying that Mr. Toomey "led the fight in the Senate to end taxpayer bailouts of big banks" by voting in 2012 against extending a crisis-era program offering unlimited deposit insurance for certain accounts.

One of the embattled House Republicans, Bruce Poliquin of Maine, is taking heat for working at a Chicago savings and loan after college and then a New York fund-management company. In September, House Majority PAC, a group aimed at helping Democrats pick up House seats, released a 30-second ad with the narrator branding Mr. Poliquin a "Wall Street millionaire who puts himself first," adding: "People like that don't fit in up here."

Poliquin spokesman Michael Byerly said that when the lawmaker was in the financial industry, "he worked to help protect workers' pensions, including those at Bath Iron Works," a storied Maine shipyard.

Other lawmakers are being criticized for backing policies portrayed as benefiting the financial industry while they have taken contributions from employees in the sector.

Ohio Republican Sen. Rob Portman has weathered such attacks from his challenger, former Democratic Gov. Ted Strickland. "Wall Street has given Rob Portman millions in campaign contributions, and he pushed a plan to privatize Social Security and risk your retirement in the stock market," a narrator says on a 30-second Strickland ad released in July. "Maybe Rob Portman should be running for the senator of Wall Street, not Ohio?"

A Portman campaign spokesman didn't respond to requests for comment.

Goldman and DRW take stake in derivatives start-up

Philip Stafford 
475 words
1 November 2016
12:11
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A US financial technology company that aims to keep derivative traders out of trouble has won the backing of Goldman Sachs, Wells Fargo and high-speed trader DRW Holdings in its first fundraising.

New York-based Droit Financial Technologies has secured $16m to accelerate marketing of its software, which automates compliance with complex regulations in the derivatives market introduced in the wake of the financial crisis.

Droit s fundraising underlines a wave of interest from market participants in the use of so-called  regtech  or regulation technology in trading that apply artificial intelligence and data crunching to real-time monitoring of financial services.

It is the first time Droit, founded in 2012 by a group of former derivative traders, quantitative analysts and technology specialists, has needed outside funds. The company had been profitable for the last 18 months, according to its chief executive Satya Pemmaraju, a former fixed income and derivatives trader at UBS.

Waves of rules covering all aspects of trading, from advanced risk checks to business conduct and reporting, are forcing banks and asset managers into significant IT overhauls. At the moment, most banks carry out the compliance with their own fragmented IT systems.

Reading data rapidly, the software will ensure that each trade has met a checklist of compliance requirements. Droit has  an excellent opportunity to become the industry standard for regulatory-compliant decision engines,  said Joanne Hannaford, managing director of technology at Goldman.

Droit will focus on the industry s preparations for Mifid II, a sprawling piece of European legislation designed to toughen investor protections and make bond and over-the-counter markets more transparent. The rules will come into force from the start of 2018. It also wants to open an office in Singapore.

 With the fundraise we want to scale very quickly. It s a very fast scaling over the next 18 months and we ve got the backing of some very strategic partners,  said Mr Pemmaraju.

Financial regulations apply to every single transaction and those deals are executed in thousands of a second, so rules needed to be applied to data in real-time and in differing legal jurisdictions, Mr Pemmaraju said.

 You can t do [compliance] just in the US. Your clients are global, your trading and sales are global, you can t treat it locally. We approach law from a mathematical point of view,  said Mr Pemmaraju.  It s an incredibly complex world. We spent two-and-a-half years just planning and build out the infrastructure. 

Pivot Investment Partners, a US venture capital company, also participated in the fundraising. The four will collectively take a minority stake in Droit.

Fellow founding partners Anup Menon had senior technology roles at Barclays and Lehman Brothers while Brock Arnason headed fixed income e-commerce at Morgan Stanley.

Admiral looks at Facebook posts to gauge risk in younger drivers

Oliver Ralph, Insurance Correspondent 
493 words
1 November 2016
23:50
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

UK-based InsurerAdmiralhas come up with a way to crunch through social media posts to work out who deserves a lower premium. People who seem cautious and deliberate in their choice of words are likely to pay a lot less than those with overconfident remarks.

So ditch the exclamation marks and enthusiasm, and replace them with caution, hesitancy and carefully composed lists. The language you use in your Facebook comments might be worth a lot more than you realise.

Admiral is set to tryout the system on a new product called firstcarquote, which will provide car "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/UK_insurance_industry" to 17 to 21-year-olds. If customers agree to let the company look through their Facebook posts they could be eligible for discounts of up to 350 although the trial has not yet been launched.

 The way you write and the things you post online are shaped by your personality,  said Yossi Borenstein, an independent adviser, who helped develop the software.

 If you are a super-organised person, you are likely to take more time travelling so you are not likely to be in a hurry. An organised person might write things in a list or might be more specific rather than vague when it comes to arrangements.

 An overconfident person will use phrases such as  always ,  never  and exclamation marks rather than  maybe ,  perhaps  and  let me think about it . An overconfident person might be a risky driver. 

The type of language that Admiral will use is not fixed, however. Over time, it might find that some phrases are more relevant than others.  We are trying to link the data to actual driving outcomes. The algorithm will find links,  added Mr Borenstein.  It is a big data game. The more data we have, the more the things that we look for will change. 

The insurer said that drivers aged 21 or younger are 70 per cent more likely to be involved in an accident that causes an injury. However, younger people also lack the sort of long record that can help the insurers distinguish the safer drivers from the riskier ones. Using social media to assess younger drivers is, said Admiral executive Dan Mines, one way to redress the balance.

Admiral believes it is the first insurer to use Facebook data in this way. But insurers have been using social media for other purposes such as fraud detection for years.

In one recent example, Admiral said it received a claim for an accident involving two people who said they did not know each other. However, after examining Facebook posts, the insurer found out they were boyfriend and girlfriend.

Twitter has been used in a similar way. Aviva recently investigated a whiplash claim and found that the claimant had been tweeting about finishing seventh (out of 2,000 people) in a 10km race while he was supposedly injured.

Corrections & Amplifications:

SoftBank Group earlier this year sold about $3 billion in Alibaba shares and used another $7 billion worth of shares as collateral to raise additional money. An earlier version of this article incorrectly said SoftBank had sold its $10 billion stake in the company. (Nov. 2)

Although shares of Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. have surged 77% in price from a low in September 2015, many investors are selling the company short, anticipating trouble ahead for the Chinese e-commerce giant.

Alibaba, which has posted a string of quarters showing revenue gains, has appealed to investors as a way to profit on what has been to now "http://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-consumers-could-feel-pinch-of-debt-1475811848"in China. Still, there are persisting concerns the company's profitability is overstated, alongside broader uncertainty about China's economy, said Wedbush Securities Inc.'s Gil Luria.

And after a run-up in the stock's price and lofty expectations for growth, short sellers have been targeting Alibaba before "http://www.wsj.com/articles/alibaba-earnings-what-to-watch-1477986939" and China's Singles' Day on Nov. 11, the biggest e-commerce shopping day that last year netted the company a blockbuster $14.3 billion in sales.

Short selling involves an investor selling stock he or she doesn't yet own in a company, hoping to buy the shares and deliver them later when the price has fallen, thus netting a profit.

Short interest, or the volume of shares sold short and not yet covered, in Alibaba has surged 49% this year, an increase equivalent to about $3.9 billion, according to data from financial analytics firm S3 Partners.

Most of the bearish wagers were made in the second half of the year, as such bets against the company reached a record $12.9 billion at the end of September. The level of short interest has since slipped to $11.6 billion, data from New York-based S3 show.

Growing concern about the company has come as fresh doubts emerged on Oct. 19 over the reliability of official Chinese statistics after the country said the economy grew 6.7% for third consecutive quarter. Economists noted it is rare for a fast-growing economy to clock the same growth quarter after quarter.

"When the Chinese economy decelerates, Alibaba will decelerate as well," said Mr. Luria, who has a hold rating on Alibaba, setting him apart from the large number of analysts who advise investors to buy the shares, according to Thomson Reuters data. Mr. Luria doesn't see the Chinese economy continuing to grow at rates seen in the past.

Waning enthusiasm about the Chinese economy might also have been evident "http://www.wsj.com/articles/china-logistics-company-zto-express-drops-in-trading-debut-1477579281" Inc. The Shanghai-based logistics company that gets most of its business from Alibaba tumbled 15% from its initial public offering price in its debut on Oct 27.

Adding to the nervousness, SoftBank Group Corp. cashed in earlier this year on the rally in Alibaba shares, selling about $3 billion in shares and using shares worth $7 billion as collateral to raise additional money, according to a spokesman for the Japanese company.

Meanwhile, the options market is indicating a 5.4% price swing in Alibaba after the company reports earnings on Wednesday, in line with the 5.3% fluctuation the shares posted on average over the last eight quarters, data from Trade Alert show. The expected volatility in the stock has climbed to the highest level since February, FactSet data, based on options, show.

U.S. companies reporting quarterly financial results in the past couple of weeks have shown mixed results in China. Apple Inc. reported on Oct. 25 that fourth-quarter revenue from the greater China region tumbled 30% from the same period a year ago, though some of the decline was due to competition from local smartphone makers.

Kynikos Associates founder Jim Chanos is among the most outspoken bears on Alibaba and China. While Alibaba's stock has rallied 22% since he first publicly disclosed he was shorting the company's stock in November 2015, Chanos reiterated his view on CNBC last month, saying corporate governance and transparency issues still permeate the Chinese conglomerate.

Still, while the U.S. retail giant Amazon.com Inc. launched its "http://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-delivers-prime-program-to-china-1477647637" on mainland China, Needham & Co.'s Kerry Rice doesn't see the Seattle-based company successfully stealing market share away from Alibaba.

Alibaba is "ingrained in the way consumers purchase goods in China. It's very hard to break that pattern of behavior," Rice said, who has a buy rating on the shares.

It has "http://www.wsj.com/articles/brexit-vote-stunned-britons-awake-to-a-new-era-for-u-k-1466768937" to thank.

In December 2014, Abu Dhabi Financial Group LLC agreed to buy New Scotland Yard, the former headquarters of London's police force, for 370 million ($451 million). A new development with high-end condominiums, offices and shops is in the works for the central London site.

The deal officially closed Monday, when 90% of the money was paid to the Metropolitan Police, according to a spokesman for ADFG. The two-year gap came about because the police had to move into a new space.

A 10% deposit was paid when ADFG first agreed to the sale. Over those 22 months, sterling has fallen 22.7% against the United Arab Emirates dirham, mostly because the pound has plummeted since Britons voted to leave the European Union in late June.

"When you're transferring 90% of a big number, you're saving quite a lot," said Niccol Barattieri di San Pietro, chief executive of Northacre, the U.K. developer that will be spearheading construction.

For investors that agreed to buy U.K. real estate before Brexit, the discount from weakened sterling has been significant.

Wells Fargo & Co. completed the purchase of a London office building in July, just weeks after the Brexit vote, for nearly 300 million. The price was "http://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargos-discount-u-k-deal-is-the-new-normal-1473189057?tesla=y". But the drop in sterling knocked off another 12% off the price.

The post-Brexit fall in sterling has spurred bargain hunting among foreign real-estate investors. But "http://www.wsj.com/articles/european-property-investors-look-beyond-london-1477274461?tesla=y". Real-estate investment volumes in the U.K. were down 51% between July and September compared with the period in 2015, according to property data company Real Capital Analytics.

While the initial panic that rattled the real-estate sector in the weeks after the referendum has subsided, uncertainty about how the U.K. government will extricate itself from the EU and the potential for sterling to fall further has limited deal making.

In 2014, when the London property market was booming, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/canadian-property-investors-tap-london-1418184434" for the New Scotland Yard site.

The new development in Westminster will include six buildings with homes and commercial space. ADFG and London-based Northacre plan to complete the project by 2021.

The sale was part of a drive by London officials to reduce costs. By selling the old headquarters and moving into a new space, "we have been able to make savings to invest in front-line policing, improving the technological capability of the force and develop facilities," said Helen King, assistant commissioner of the Metropolitan Police, in a statement Tuesday.

Politics
Wall Street Plays Villain in Congressional Campaign TV Ads; Democrats and Republicans alike are targeting opponents with bank-bashing television ads

By Donna Borak 
1,008 words
1 November 2016
11:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Eight years after the financial crisis, anti-Wall Street rhetoric remains a staple of congressional campaigns across the country, with Democrats and Republicans alike launching bank-bashing television ads in nearly three dozen competitive races.

In the hotly contested Indiana Senate election, for example, Democrat Evan Bayh's recent stints as an asset manager and as a regional bank director are being used as reasons not to vote for him. "Evan Bayh went from the Banking Committee to a big bank board room &," says the narrator of a 30-second ad paid for by the Senate Leadership Fund, a super PAC linked to Senate Majority Leader Mitch McConnell (R., Ky.). The spot brands Mr. Bayh "Wall Street's dream senator."

"That's pretty rich," said Bayh spokesman Ben Ray. "Evan voted for the toughest Wall Street reform since the Great Depression," he said, referring to the politician's backing for the 2010 Dodd-Frank overhaul act during his previous Senate term.

Kantar Media's Campaign Media Analysis Group, an analytics firm that monitors advertising in close contests, identified for The Wall Street Journal 34 Senate and House races in 25 states where candidates have used "Wall Street" or "banks" as invectives to try to tarnish their opponents.

That's a decline from the past two presidential election cycles, said Steven Passwaiter, a Kantar vice president. Still, he added, "Wall Street is always a convenient tool. In these tightly contested races, portraying candidates as catering to the big banks can be used to swing enough votes."

Similar sentiments have coursed through the presidential race this year. Vermont Sen. Bernie Sanders regularly criticized Hillary Clinton during the Democratic primary for the close ties he said she and her husband, former President Bill Clinton, had cultivated with the financial industry. Her Republican rival, Donald Trump, has echoed that rhetoric, blasting her during their final debate for her ads "paid for by your friends on Wall Street that gave so much money because they know you're going to protect them."

Mrs. Clinton has made strengthening financial regulation a core plank of her platform, while also pledging to enforce rules aimed at curbing Wall Street pay and giving enforcement agencies more resources to "prosecute wrongdoing."

While Mr. Bayh and Mrs. Clinton are both under fire for their finance connections, most of the targets are Republicans. Like Mr. Bayh, Pennsylvania Sen. Pat Toomey also faces harsh television ads alluding to his pre-politics career as an investment banker in the 1980s, calling him a "Wall Street wheeler-dealer."

"While on the Senate Banking Committee, Pat Toomey voted to rewrite rules to help bankers like him and voted to eliminate protections put into place to stop Wall Street's risky practices," says an ad released Oct. 7 by his Democratic challenger Katie McGinty.

Toomey campaign spokesman Ted Kwong said the ad gives a misleading impression of the Republican lawmaker's record, saying that Mr. Toomey "led the fight in the Senate to end taxpayer bailouts of big banks" by voting in 2012 against extending a crisis-era program offering unlimited deposit insurance for certain accounts.

One of the embattled House Republicans, Bruce Poliquin of Maine, is taking heat for working at a Chicago savings and loan after college and then a New York fund management company. In September, House Majority PAC, a group aimed at helping Democrats pick up House seats, released a 30-second ad with the narrator branding Mr. Poliquin a "Wall Street millionaire who puts himself first," adding: "People like that don't fit in up here."

Poliquin spokesman Michael Byerly said that when the lawmaker was in the financial industry "he worked to help protect workers' pensions, including those at Bath Iron Works," a storied Maine shipyard.

Other lawmakers are being criticized for backing policies portrayed as benefiting the financial industry at the same time they have taken contributions from employees in the sector.

GOP Rep. Joe Heck, locked in a tight race for an open Senate seat in Nevada, has been hit with ads sponsored by the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, the largest government worker trade union, for his support for proposals allowing Americans to invest Social Security funds.

"In Washington, Congressman Joe Heck supports privatizing [Social Security], turning our savings over to Wall Street, and risking it on the stock market," the ad says. "Heck's plan means billions more for Wall Street, and they've donated over half a million dollars to his campaign."

The securities and investment industry has donated $552,580 to Mr. Heck, according to data compiled from the Center for Responsive Politics. "The implication that funds raised from the banking industry were an  investment' in Heck is highly misleading," says a statement provided by his congressional staff.

Ohio Republican Sen. Rob Portman has weathered similar attacks from his challenger, former Democratic Gov. Ted Strickland. "Wall Street has given Rob Portman millions in campaign contributions and he pushed a plan to privatize Social Security and risk your retirement in the stock market," a narrator says on a 30-second Strickland ad released in July. "Maybe Rob Portman should be running for the senator of Wall Street not Ohio?"

A spokesman for Sen. Portman's campaign didn't respond to multiple requests for comment.

Mr. Strickland used a similar tactic during the 2010 gubernatorial race against his Republican challenger, John Kasich, running an ad claiming, "John Kasich helped Wall Street predator Lehman Brothers destroy the world economy." Mr. Kasich joined Lehman Brothers' investment banking division after leaving Congress in 2001, and worked there until the firm's collapse in September 2008.

At least in Mr. Strickland's races, the Wall Street-bashing hasn't seemed effective. He was ousted by Mr. Kasich in that race. And polls show him trailing far behind Mr. Portman this year.

Markets
Inflation Jitters Spark Retreat From Bond ETFs; Investors pulled almost $1 billion from BlackRock fund in one day

By Chris Dieterich 
794 words
1 November 2016
19:54
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The bond market's October retreat fueled record withdrawals from some popular exchange-traded funds, the latest sign of investor anxiety over inflation.

Investors pulled $998 million last Thursday from iShares iBoxx High Yield Corporate Bond ETF, the oldest and largest junk-bond ETF. That is the largest daily withdrawal on record, said BlackRock Inc., the ETF's provider.

Investors also pulled $1.7 billion last week from the iShares iBoxx $ Investment Grade Corporate Bond ETF, the biggest weekly outflow since its inception in 2002.

The redemptions coincided with a global rout in government bonds that sent the yield on the 10-year U.S. Treasury note to its highest levels since late May. On Tuesday, the 10-year Treasury yield fell to 1.822%, as prices rose.

Bond investors are bracing for an interest-rate increase from the Federal Reserve and grappling with signs of rising inflation, which erodes the fixed value of bond payouts over time, in the U.S. and globally.

The BlackRock funds weren't the only ones under pressure. All told, investors pulled $2.2 billion from junk-bond ETFs last week, the most in six weeks, according to XTF, a market-analytics firm.

The withdrawals follow large gains this year in bond funds, reflecting a sharp rise in corporate-bond prices.

In 2016 through Monday, the iShares iBoxx High Yield Corporate Bond ETF has returned 11.3%, while the iShares iBoxx $ Investment Grade Corporate Bond ETF has returned 8.8%, according to Morningstar. These compare with a 5.9% return, including dividends, for the S&P 500.

"The market has been seduced into this reach-for-yield mode, and a lot of this is central-bank-policy driven," said Thomas Urano, head of taxable fixed-income portfolio management at Sage Advisory Services. "Reaching for yields blinds investors to what's happening with fundamentals, and it seems to me prudent for investors to lock in gains."

For some investors such as Mr. Urano, 2016's snapback in high-yield bonds smacked more of investors chasing big payouts than it did meaningful improvement in corporate health for junk-bond issuers.

Fund investors' piling into corporate bonds has some market watchers on edge, worried that spates of fund-related selling could exacerbate market swings. Fully 70% of the high-yield bond market, or $922 billion in assets, is held by mutual funds and separately managed accounts, an investment structure favored by financial advisers, according to recent research from Stephen Caprio and Matthew Mish, credit strategists at UBS Group AG.

In February, when U.S. crude futures plunged under $30 a barrel, the amount by which yields on junk debt exceeded those on Treasury debt, called the spread, reached 9 percentage points. Yield-seeking investors stepped in quickly, and the spread narrowed to about 4.6 percentage points in October, when oil prices traded back above $50, according to Bank of America Merrill Lynch.

Some 46% of the U.S. investment-grade market, or $2.7 trillion, is controlled by funds and SMAs. At the same time, high-profile investors, including Bill Gross, Howard Marks and Carl Icahn, have voiced concerns about potential liquidity mismatches between fast-trading ETFs and hard-to-trade corporate bonds.

Fund companies have long maintained that ETFs create more liquidity for the bond market at a time when traditional dealers banks find it increasingly unprofitable to hold bond inventories and match buyers and sellers.

"Outflows were orderly and [the high-yield and investment-grade ETFs] performed as clients have come to expect," a BlackRock spokeswoman said.

More broadly, recent large outflows are a sign investors are taking chips off the table as uncertainty about central-bank policy looms, said Chris Johnson, head of U.S. ETF distribution at RBC Capital Markets. Mr. Johnson said that, unlike earlier in the year, when corporate-bond ETF outflows were accompanied by hedging activity, recent withdrawals "signal that big, real money is thinking that this could be a tipping point for the asset class and are moving out or trimming their positions."

Jeff Knight, global head of investment solutions at Columbia Threadneedle Investments, said he isn't yet selling corporate credits because be believes they will hold up better than Treasurys after interest rates march higher. Still, he said that the rally for high-yield and investment-grade bonds is likely nearing its end.

"For us, it's too late to buy and too early to sell," Mr. Knight said. "I can see the case for risk-averse investors shifting away because there's not a lot of upside left."

Behavioral Analytics  Company Interana Raises $18 M

By Cat Zakrzewski 
292 words
1 November 2016
11:37
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Behavioral analytics company Interana Inc. said on Tuesday it has raised $18 million.

Vertex Ventures led the round, with participation from Battery Ventures, Data Collective, Allen & Company, Fuel Capital and Index Ventures. The Redwood City, Calif., startup has raised $46.2 million to date.

For clients ranging from Microsoft Corp., with its search engine Bing, to Sonos, Interana provides analytics on any data that has a time stamp. Interana Chief Executive Ann Johnson said the company's have relied on their experience from Facebook Inc. and Intel Corp. to build technology that employees in many different positions within a company will be able to use.

Ms. Johnson explained the Interana technology by talking about the company's customer Tinder, a dating app owned by Match Group Inc. On Tinder, people swipe right to express they find another Tinder user attractive. Ms. Johnson said men were indiscriminately swiping right, causing women to lose interest and use the app less.

"It was threatening their business model," Ms. Johnson said.

Using data from Interana, Tinder was able to add a number of features to cut down on this behavior. The app began to limit the number of right swipes a user could make and introduced features like "super like," a feature that can only be used once a day to show more interest than a regular right swipe.

Many of the four-year-old company's customers have been in the Silicon Valley technology sector. But Ms. Johnson said the company has recently closed some larger deals, and they're moving into more mature companies, particularly in telecommunications, cable, manufacturing and retailers.

Pro Private Markets
Relayr Powers Up $23M Series B for Industrial IoT Sensors

By Patience Haggin 
493 words
1 November 2016
11:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture capitalists are placing more bets on the promise of the industrial Internet of Things.

If industrial companies that operate heavy machinery use sensors to monitor their equipment's performance and predict when their machines need maintenance, they can prevent a costly or dangerous breakdown and do wonders for their insurer's bottom line.

That's why the corporate venture-capital arm of German reinsurance company Munich Re Group has made a $23 million strategic investment in relayr, a startup creating technologies for industrial customers to harness the Internet of Things.

Berlin-based relayr makes sensors that monitor industrial equipment to gather data or predict when machines will need maintenance, along with software and consulting services to help enterprise customers use them.

Schindler, the maker of elevators and escalators, uses relayr's sensors to monitor its machines. Other customers include Bosch and Dell. Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, which led relayr's Series A last year, and Munich Venture Partners, participated as returning investors in the Series B round.

"Just imagine a car that you never maintain. It doesn't tell you if you need more gas. You just drive until it's dead," said Kleiner partner Wen Hsieh, who led the firm's investment. "We don't drive cars until they die. So why do we do it with everything else?"

Even more promising for venture capitalists, industrial IoT infrastructure customers will be loyal, Mr. Hsieh predicted. "Once you install the sensor and connect to the cloud, no one's going to rip it out. So it's very sticky," Mr. Hsieh said.

Taking a similar view, several other startups doing related work have gathered funding recently.

Andreessen Horowitz led a $25 million Series A in Samsara Networks, which links sensors to machinery, in May 2015. Manufacturing analytics platform Sight Machine raised a $15.5 million Series B in March. In September C3 IoT, which offers software to build large systems of connected devices, raised a $70 million Series D led by its own founder, billionaire Thomas Siebel.

Munich Re's HSB subsidiary will become a relayr partner to offer customized insurance products for industrial customers who use relayr's sensors. The partnership will give relayr's customers "financial assurance that they will benefit from their IoT investments," said Greg Barats, president and chief executive of HSB.

The Berlin-based startup will use the new funding to help its engineering and product teams to grow, and to beef up its sales operation in the U.S. and Europe. It may also make small acquisitions or acqui-hires of companies that produce IoT technology complementary to its own stack, co-founder and Chief Executive Josef Brunner said. He said he wanted the company's current staff of about 100 to reach 150 by the end of the year.

Creighton's Take: Theresa May's Financial Bona Fides Are Good Sign for Fintech

Theresa May's Financial Bona Fides Are Good Sign for Fintech

The U.K.'s push to become a fintech hub might have a little-known ally: Its new prime minister, Theresa May, is probably the world leader most knowledgeable about financial affairs in at least a generation.

Few people are aware that Mrs. May, who succeeded David Cameron in the wake of Britons' historic vote in June to leave the European Union, worked on financial policy full time for almost 20 years before she was elected to the House of Commons in 1997. From 1977 to 1983 she was at the Bank of England, and from 1986 to 1997 at the Association of Payment Clearing Services, where she was head of the European Affairs Unit.

"It's like it never happened," said Sandra Quinn, who worked closely with Mrs. May, now 60 years old, at the Association of Payment Clearing Services for five years in the mid-1990s. "It's partly journalist ignorance, but also when Theresa first became an MP she didn't talk about it either," she added in an interview with The Wall Street Journal.

Mrs. May used her financial acumen Monday when she "http://www.wsj.com/articles/boes-carney-to-extend-term-by-one-year-to-june-2019-1477937474" for Bank of England governor Mark Carney in a rebuke to Brexit hardliners. Still, the decision comes just weeks after an apparent disagreement with Mr. Carney over monetary policy when she highlighted the impact of quantitative easing on inequality.

Mrs. May is probably good news for British fintech firms, too. The APCS, which was succeeded by the UK Payments Administration in 2009, was a not-for-profit trade group representing British banks' interests in the payments system, an area in which fintech firms are active.

"No one would need to brief her massively on these issues. All the basic stuff she'll never lose. You don't stop knowing how payments work once you learn it because it's so blooming complex," said Ms. Quinn, who still works in the finance industry.

The prime minister's office declined to comment.

The U.K. is already "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-takes-novel-approach-on-fintech-1460370600"in removing regulations that might stifle fintech firms' growth.

"The big thing we worked on was the regulation on cross-border payments in Europe, which back then was really more about linking domestic systems to other domestic systems," Ms. Quinn said.

Such experience could also be useful as the U.K. negotiates its exit from the EU. The extent to which British banks have access to EU financial markets will be a key negotiating chip.

"It's ironic she's taken power at the time all the talk is getting out of the EU," Ms. Quinn said.

So far the prime minister's experience doesn't appear to have helped the City of London much, with bankers saying they haven't had much of a chance to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-financial-firms-beef-up-contingency-plans-ahead-of-brexit-deadline-1475746698" for retaining EU access.

William Ahern, former deputy director for financial markets at the Bank of England, said he knew the prime minister in the early 1980s when she worked on monetary policy. "It was difficult to get hired by the Bank of England &.It was a good place to work and they had a lot of applicants," he added. Earlier, she had been a secretary to the Wilson committee on the functioning of financial institutions, Mr. Ahern said.

"She's the best boss I've ever worked for. She doesn't talk about herself. She can be really empathetic and very aware, keen to know what drives you, a fantastic role model," Ms. Quinn said.
