Squeezed budgets put focus on measuring value for money

David Bond 
750 words
31 October 2016
05:55
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

In 2007, Facebook chief executive Mark Zuckerberg predicted that trust would be the basis for marketing to consumers in the future.

 As people share more, they have access to more opinions from the people they trust about the products and services they use. This makes it easier to discover the best products and improve the quality and efficiency of their lives,  "http://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/newsbysector/mediatechnologyandtelecoms/9055830/Facebook-IPO-Letter-from-Mark-Zuckerberg.html" as the technology company launched its initial public offering.

Nine years on, Facebook has become one of two dominant forces in new media. According to a recent study from Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, the US venture capital fund, Facebook and fellow tech group Google now receive 75 per cent of all new spending on online advertising.

Ad industry executives initially expected the explosion in digital marketing to transform the way media agencies and brands measure the effectiveness of their advertising campaigns. A wealth of new data would allow them to get closer to customers and to improve accuracy of measurement. Instead it has raised more questions for an industry grappling with the structural shift away from traditional media such as TV, radio and newspapers.

In September, Facebook apologised after admitting it had wrongly calculated viewing times for its online videos, which led to renewed calls for the company, which has 1.6bn users, to change the way it measures advertising data. Until recently, Facebook had counted only video views of more than three seconds when it calculated its  average duration of video viewed .

The Facebook controversy followed an investigation into online ad fraud in the US by the Association of National Advertisers, a US trade body. More recently, in Japan the dominant advertising agency Dentsu apologised for overcharging the car company Toyota for online advertising.

Sir Martin Sorrell, chief executive of the world s biggest advertising group, WPP, "https://www.marketingweek.com/2016/08/24/sir-martin-sorrell-brands-are-starting-to-question-if-they-have-over-invested-in-digital/" among media buyers in August when he called for better measurement  not just offline but online too . He added:  The answer is not Facebook or Google data, we can t have the players being the referees. There has to be independence in terms of measurement. 

Facebook says it will give marketers more choices on measuring effectiveness by "https://www.facebook.com/business/news/new-ad-viewability-partners?__mref=message_bubble" with companies such as Nielsen, the US media researcher.

According to the research firm eMarketer, online advertising will this year "https://www.emarketer.com/Article/US-Digital-Ad-Spending-Surpass-TV-this-Year/1014469" to take the biggest proportion of the $600bn spent across the global industry in total each year.

Some industry commentators argue that the dominance of digital advertising has only underlined the need for a deep rethink on the way online data are measured if they are to help marketers work out best value for money.

Bob Hoffman, "http://adcontrarian.blogspot.co.uk/2016/10/data-facts-and-principles.html" blogger, recently lamented:  The online advertising industry is drowning in data, but has generated almost no useful facts or principles. The encyclopedia of things we don t know about online advertising since we started collecting  big data  is comical. 

For chief marketing officers, the uncertainty is another challenge at a time when global and political instability is already putting a squeeze on budgets.

Peter Field, a UK marketing consultant, says there is a disconnect between the standards people expect from digital and traditional media.

 The big question is what do people think they are getting when they buy a digital slot?  he says.  With TV or the press there are oversight bodies and legal frameworks which measure and give executives a clear picture. Suddenly marketers are prepared to throw away all that learning they have built up over decades. 

A growing band of tech companies is, though, offering new ways of measuring the impact of campaigns.

For instance, research firm Kantar Millward Brown, owned by WPP, uses techniques such as brain scanning and face tracking to assess human reaction to brands and advertising content. However, they are part of a mix of tools the company provides, points out Louise Ainsworth, its UK chief executive.

The power of digital advertising means marketing officers and agencies should take an integrated approach to how they spend their budgets, she says.  With any kind of measurement there are pros and cons   and digital measurement is particularly complex. 

Mark Ritson, a business school academic and branding consultant, is more forthright:  More than half of all UK advertising spend now goes into digital. [A majority] of that half goes to Google and Facebook. And these two companies are making it up as they go along.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
'Japanese equities will be interesting again'; FACE TO FACE: David Semaya

By Attracta Mooney 
1,186 words
31 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
04
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The chairman of Nikko Asset Management loves soba noodles and robots

David Semaya is a man of contrasts.

Wearing a white shirt and no jacket, the chairman of Nikko Asset Management, the $165bn Japanese fund house, strikes a relaxed pose. Beneath the calm veneer, however, there are signs of nervousness: his leg shakes under the table and at times he rushes his words.

The father of three is humble, almost apologetic when answering questions about his life and career. And yet this modesty belies some exceptional talents: he is a polyglot who was in a programme for gifted students while growing up in the US.

The 54-year-old says he had an "alternative education" when asked how and why a Floridian ended up learning Japanese and Chinese more than three decades ago. (He also studied Spanish and French.)

It is only when pushed about what an alternative education involves that he admits he was one of Florida's brilliant young people. "It is embarrassing, because I don't like to talk about myself, but I was in what we call - it is a terrible name - the gifted programme," he says.

It is hard to know whether this modesty is innate or whether Mr Semaya has developed it during his almost two decades in Japan - a country where humility is venerated.

Mr Semaya, who first visited Japan as a 17-year-old in 1978, is clearly a fan. During a follow-up phone call after our meeting, the keen trekker gushes about Japan's mountains and food, especially its soba noodles.

He worked in education when he first moved to Japan. He cast aside plans to become a lawyer after a friend he bumped into in Tokyo advised him to "get into finance".

At the time, Japan was booming. But in the decades since, growth has stagnated, thanks to a greying population, falling demand for goods, both domestically and for export, and stubborn debts.

Then came Abenomics - the "three arrows" of fiscal stimulus, monetary easing and structural reforms espoused in 2012 by Shinzo Abe, Japan's prime minister, to get the country back to growth.

But almost four years later, Abenomics has disappointed many. Some argue it has failed.

Yet Mr Semaya defends the country's growth plans. "My view is to give it a bit of time. You are seeing progress. Maybe it is slow for the outsider but for Japan we are moving at a rapid pace," he says.

He highlights attempts to improve corporate governance at listed Japanese businesses, seen as a vital way to attract foreign investment and grow the economy.

"A number of external directors [are] coming on to boards; [there is] real soul searching and navel gazing around what is good about the system that is in place, what some of the challenges are and how to move things forward. So we are seeing not only talk, but action," he says.

By focusing on governance, and issues such as the best use of capital over time, the outlook for Japanese businesses will improve, he adds. "You will see that the Japanese equity market will be an interesting one again, especially given the number of companies that have global exposure in their businesses."

Mr Semaya is dismissive of suggestions that the Bank of Japan will soon be forced to resort to helicopter money - an alternative to quantitative easing, where money is given to consumers or spent on infrastructure - to kick-start growth.

"It is much too early to speculate around that. Tourism is doing well. We are seeing demand for university grads go up. There is tightness in the labour markets here," he says. "[Helicopter money] is something for the future."

Although much of his career has been spent in Japan, the University of Florida graduate left the country in 2007 to become Europe, Middle East and Africa chief executive of Barclays Global Investors. During his time in London, the asset management arm of the British bank was sold to BlackRock, the world's largest fund house, for $13.5bn. The 2009 deal included a crown jewel in the form of iShares, the passive manager that has since become the world's largest exchange traded fund provider.

When questioned if selling BGI and the hugely profitable iShares was the right course of action, Mr Semaya will not say much other than that he was not involved in the decision. "Hindsight is very easy," he says.

He is also reluctant to discuss his departure as head of UK private banking at Barclays Wealth Management in 2013. Soon after his departure was announced, the private bank pulled out of more than 100 markets. "I had other ideas about the strategy of the business," he says simply.

He is more willing to discuss the future. Mr Semaya was hired in 2014 to help grow Nikko, which ranks as the third-largest Japanese asset manager, outside of its home market. Currently, about 30 per cent of Nikko's assets are managed for international clients.

"Over the next three to five years, we would love to get the balance to 50-50," says Mr Semaya, who returned to Japan two years ago. To do so, the Tokyo-based company has been acquiring stakes and teams around the world, including a global equity team based in Scotland.

Nikko has also been behind one of the most high-profile fund launches of recent years. In August 2015, it unveiled an actively managed robotics fund , one of just a few in the world looking to monetise the trend for artificial intelligence. The fund attracted 300bn ($3bn) in just three months.

The man whose hobbies all seem to revolve around the natural world - hiking, fly fishing, snorkelling - gets excited when talking about the potential offered by technology.

Mr Semaya believes robotics will mean big changes for the world. Just as cars replaced horses and buggies more than 100 years ago, many jobs and products can be replaced by robots, he argues.

"If I were a buggy maker in the 1880s, then I would have been dislocated within 20 years. I think we are going through that today. It is not comfortable for many people, but the opportunity set is obviously huge.

"It is the new industrial revolution."

CV

1992-2002 Various roles at Merrill Lynch in Japan and New York

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
Top Japanese insurers tune products for self-driving era;  " New age set to redefine premiums  " Looming risks include cyber attack

By Kana Inagaki and Leo Lewis in Tokyo 
488 words
31 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Japan's top insurers are studying new products to brace themselves for the age of self-driving vehicles , which may redefine insurance premium burdens for global carmakers as well as component makers and technology companies.

Studies have forecast a steep decline in insurance premiums as the number and severity of accidents fall with the emergence of autonomous vehicles.

But Japanese insurers MS & AD and Tokio Marine say the transition to driverless technology also brings opportunities with the increasing costs to repair cars , the emergence of new risks such as cyber attack and changes in the way insurance will be bought.

"We expect the weight of new types of insurance products for new risks such as cyber attacks will increase significantly," said Hideyuki Sakashita, manager at Mitsui Sumitomo Insurance.

Motor is one of the most important markets for the global insurance industry, accounting for $700bn of premiums every year, or 42 per cent of total property and casualty insurance.

Japanese insurers are seeking to stay ahead of the race by setting up teams - dubbed "epoch-maker's section" at MS & AD and "future technology team" at Tokio Marine - to collect research data on autonomous vehicle technology to plan new products.

The emergence of self-driving vehicles means insurance will be bought less by consumers and more by carmakers and producers of other technologies packed inside the car. One of the biggest challenges, officials say, is determining who is responsible in the case of a car accident or malfunction.

"The cause of an accident - whether it is the carmaker, the supplier, the internet provider, the electronic maps, software or artificial intelligence - could be countless [for self-driving vehicles]," said Kazuaki Kutsuzawa, manager at Tokio Marine & Nichido Fire Insurance.

The debate over responsibility among carmakers and their suppliers has already intensified in recent years even with existing vehicles. In the case of the mass recall of Takata-made airbags , the top carmakers and the airbag manufacturer are debating how to split the costs to repair the faulty airbags.

In the case of a cyber attack, insurance insiders say it would be difficult to determine who was to blame, making it difficult for consumers to claim. The longer it takes to settle the issue, the bigger the financial burden on car owners, especially with components inside connected cars becoming more expensive.

For carmakers building self-driving vehicles, the creation of insurance products to address the risks and responsibilities is critical to ensuring that consumers embrace the technology.

"Even if the technology is developed, it would be meaningless if consumers feel too uncertain to use the self-driving vehicles. Autonomous driving will only be accepted when there is assurance that there is insurance to provide a safety net," Mr Kutsuzawa said.

Pro Private Markets
Rising Star: Anika Agarwal Brings Problem-Solving Prowess to Insight

By Laura Cooper 
729 words
31 October 2016
13:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Anika Agarwal learned the art of problem solving at a young age.

Thanks partly to her father, who was a professor of computer science and engineering at Canada's McGill University when she was a child, the 35-year-old vice president at technology-focused private-equity firm Insight Venture Partners has cultivated critical thinking skills and a passion for technology.

Her ability to tackle even the thorniest challenges that face growing technology companies, combined with an enthusiasm for all things tech, has earned her the respect of her colleagues and placed her among a rising generation of potential leaders at her firm.

Anika Agarwal

Education

Ms. Agarwal received a bachelor's degree in computer science from Stanford University. She also holds a M.B.A. from Stanford Graduate School of Business.

Select Board Seats

Ms. Agarwal sits on the board of oil and gas software provider Drilling Info Inc., public safety software provider Appriss Inc., workplace wellness software provider Virgin Pulse, public safety software provider TriTech Software Systems Inc. and B2B-Center, an e-marketplace for corporate procurement in Russia.

Like many private-equity professionals, Ms. Agarwal began her career in banking, initially at Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. and later in the principal investment unit at Goldman Sachs Group Inc., where she got a taste of private-equity investing across a variety of industries. But her desire to get more deeply involved with technology companies ultimately drew her to private equity, starting at Vista Equity Partners, before she moved to the East Coast to join Insight in 2014.

"At Lehman I was in a two-year banking program," said Ms. Agarwal. "I liked being a banker but was definitely still on the sidelines. It's very different when you're putting your own capital at risk and to get to see [companies] from beginning to end."

At Insight, Ms. Agarwal's ability to juggle complex problems proved critical to helping one of the firm's portfolio companies Appriss Inc. navigate a complicated series of deals that almost fell through.

Insight first invested in Louisville, Ky.-based Appriss in 2014. As part of the deal, Ms. Agarwal joined the board of the company, which provides software that helps track criminal offenders, monitor public safety and check buying patterns and behaviors of users of certain over-the-counter medications that could be used to make illegal drugs, among other products.

Her drive and ability quickly impressed Michael Davis, the co-founder and chief executive of Appriss.

"I've never worked with someone who is available 24/7," Mr. Davis said. "I used to ask her  When do you sleep?' We would be exchanging emails at midnight."

Mr. Davis said Ms. Agarwal was instrumental in helping the company successfully complete two simultaneous transactions earlier this year. The company was working on the divestiture of a unit that provides technology to collect crash reports and electronic citations. At the same time, it was working to acquire Sysrepublic Inc., a retail analytics provider.

Among the biggest challenges Appriss faced in the deals were a series of complicated negotiations and structuring the deal financing from the banks.

The complicated divestiture almost fell through on three separate occasions, but Ms. Agarwal's deft handling of the situation ultimately helped steer them to the finish line, said Deven Parekh, a managing director at Insight who joined the firm in 2000. LexisNexis's Risk Solutions division bought the crash and project unit, and Appriss announced its acquisition of Sysrepublic in April.

Mr. Parekh said that the deals were among the more complicated situations that he has seen over the course of his many years of investing. But Ms. Agarwal's ability to dig in and solve problems for Appriss and other companies has put her among the first people that portfolio company management teams reach out to for help.

"The ultimate sign of when someone has developed as a professional in private equity is when the management team calls them first," he said. "They call her first. She's the first call, and that's true on a lot of the companies that I work with her on. The management team uses her as a thought partner and a management partner."

Will Kobe Bryant Score Again? The former basketball star talks about his venture-capital fund and what it takes to succeed

1,112 words
31 October 2016
02:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Kobe Bryant's stunning achievements in basketball are known the world over. Now he is out to prove himself anew, through Bryant Stibel, the venture-capital fund in which he is a general partner, and Kobe Inc., a multimedia content company. Mr. Bryant talked about his new challenges with Wall Street Journal Finance Editor Dennis Berman. Edited excerpts follow.

Talking percentages

MR. BERMAN: What was your field-goal percentage, 45% or so? Are you ready to shoot 5% as a good venture capitalist might be lucky to?

MR. BRYANT: No. I'm going to shoot higher than that. I'm used to having this patient approach. Because when you start training camp, you're building the tools necessary to win a championship. You have to have the patience.

MR. BERMAN: You're a student of footwork, right? To use a basketball analogy. What is the footwork of the craft you're building today?

MR. BRYANT: Focus. Focus. You have to go with things that you understand. Businesses that you know.

MR. BERMAN: Tell us about your fund. Tell us an investment or two you are excited about that shows the focus you've described.

MR. BRYANT: About three years ago, I started looking at what comes next. I started looking in the investment space. So Jeff Stibel and I started our fund and started investing our own capital. We have a couple of our CEOs here. Cindy Mi, from VIPKID.

MR. BERMAN: Where English-language teachers in the U.S., primarily, are teaching kids in China?

MR. BRYANT: It's about inspiring the kids in China to understand there's a much bigger world out there. And helping them have opportunities.

I think Walter Driver is here from Scopely [the mobile game developer], who's absolutely crushing it. The Walking Dead has absolutely been killing it. Walter and Cindy both are strong, extremely smart CEOs. But they have an even stronger team with them. Those are things we look at. You want a CEO that understands they need to build a team around them that's sharper and smarter than they are.

MR. BERMAN: Do you think your life, being on teams, helps you understand the team in a business setting?

MR. BRYANT: They're completely different disciplines, but the core competencies are absolutely the same. You have to rely on others to have success as a group. I had to learn the process. Five championships later, you understand how that works. So I don't have to learn that approach now.

MR. BERMAN: But you have to identify it in other people.

MR. BRYANT: Yes, you do. For me it's really easy. You sit down and you talk with somebody. When I talk with Cindy, and she's telling me about VIPKID, she cannot stop. It's one idea after the other. The passion comes out of them. You don't have to search for it.

Empathy's edge

MR. BERMAN: You said recently, along the way, you wish you had been more empathic.

MR. BRYANT: When we lost in the finals to the Celtics in 2008, that was the turning point. I started thinking about it. Because it's on me. If I didn't feel like my teammates were aggressive enough, that falls on me. I had to understand what their journey was. How are they feeling as people? What are they going through? And when you do that, you have that empathy, it helps you be a better leader. Because you know how to reach them better and communicate to them better.

MR. BERMAN: But could you have been as good as you were if you had the empathy from the start?

MR. BRYANT: Better. Because it cuts both ways, right? When you have empathy toward your opponents, it helps you destroy them quicker. Because you understand exactly who they are. You understand what buttons to push. You understand if they're insecure, if they fear embarrassment. And you can really hit those buttons to strike a nerve.

MR. BERMAN: Can you give an on-court example of that?

MR. BRYANT: Yes. There would be certain teams that had a player they just signed to a max contract, and a supportive player who was up for free agency who hadn't signed yet. So when we play them during the season, I'm saying, OK, we're going to double-team the guy that hasn't gotten the max contract yet. We're not going to let him get a shot off. And we're going to single-cover the guy that has the max contract. Then watch the guy that doesn't have the max contract bitch and complain about not getting the ball all night long and watch them divide each other.

MR. BERMAN: That's brilliant. The business equivalent?

MR. BRYANT: I don't know. Haven't been doing it long enough.

MR. BERMAN: Your celebrity has value. How do you personally separate what your celebrity brings and all the other thinking that you bring?

MR. BRYANT: It depends on the company. There's certain companies that line up with what the brand represents. There are other things that are more investment-focused.

Story lines

MR. BERMAN: Reed Hastings, the Netflix CEO, had this magical equation. He said, how do we translate the money that we invest into the joy for our users? So what is your secret sauce of human nature?

MR. BRYANT: You have to be true to who you are. Everybody has a different journey. But there's also a through line that connects all of us. And so the trick really becomes how do you communicate that? That's what I enjoy doing. That's the fun part.

MR. BERMAN: Another part of what you're doing is Kobe Studios, trying to get those story lines to people. Give us some examples of where you can give truth to people about themselves.

MR. BRYANT: Sports. For me, it's very easy to communicate that. I write all day and outline stories, a sports mythological universe.

MR. BERMAN: Do most kids today have the attention span?

MR. BRYANT: If the stories are good, kids will pay attention.

Clinton Could Push Postal Banking Plan; Progressives like the idea of postal banking, and big banks might not object, writes analyst Karen Petrou.

194 words
31 October 2016

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

October 28, 2016

If Hillary Clinton wins the presidency, there is a good chance she will endorse the idea of turning the U.S. Postal Service into a retail bank, "http://www.fedfin.com/blog/2204-karen-petrou-on-how-clinton-goes-postal", a consulting firm. The politics of the proposal may actually be manageable for a President Clinton, she argues. Progressives are strongly in favor of postal banking as an alternative to high-interest small-dollar loans, and big banks may decide it's not worth their while to fight a proposal that deals with products that they "don't want anymore." With big banks preferring to invest in financial technology rather than new brick and mortar infrastructure, they may not find it useful to fight over a plan that dovetails with their own changing business models. And if progressives are focused on postal banking, it could mean they are less focused on other regulatory matters that might affect big banks more, like bringing back the Glass-Steagall Act, Ms. Petrou writes.

Pro Private Markets
Rising Star: Tomasz Tunguz Might Be Silicon Valley's  Where's Waldo?'

By Cat Zakrzewski 
789 words
31 October 2016
14:52
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Regulars of San Francisco's South Park, the neighborhood where many venture-capital firms have set up West Coast outposts, often think they have spotted Redpoint Ventures Partner Tomasz Tunguz working late in the office.

But Mr. Tunguz always aims to be home for dinner with his family at 5:30 p.m. sharp. Passersby have been fooled by a cardboard cutout of him made for a technology conference that his co-workers place around the office to trick people.

It's understandable Mr. Tunguz could be perceived as being in two places at once. His experience as a Dartmouth College-educated engineer and his obsession with machine learning have led to a pace of six potentially promising investments at Redpoint.

Within Silicon Valley, the 34-year-old venture capitalist also has built a following as a writer. He has a popular VC blog and recently released his book, Winning With Data.

Tomasz Tunguz

Education

Mr. Tunguz earned a bachelor's degree in mechanical engineering, a bachelor's of engineering in machine learning and a master's in engineering management from Dartmouth College.

Career Path

After college, Mr. Tunguz worked as an engineer at Appian Corp., developing systems for the Department of Homeland Security. He then moved to Google Inc., where he managed the company's AdSense product for social networks. He joined Redpoint Ventures in 2008.

Board Positions

Mr. Tunguz sits on the boards of Axial Networks Inc., Electric Imp Inc., Expensify Inc., Looker Data Sciences Inc. and Dremio Corp. He's also a board observer at Quantifind Inc. and ThredUP Inc.

Every weekday at 5 a.m., Mr. Tunguz writes a data-driven blog post about startups. Many of his posts focus on cloud-based software companies or play off recent news, like Microsoft Corp.'s acquisition of LinkedIn Corp. Popular blog posts include "A Framework for Maximizing Startup Marketing Effectiveness" and a series called "Startup Best Practices."

Mr. Tunguz said the idea to start a blog came from attending board meetings. Entrepreneurs would ask questions about hiring or about what benchmarks they needed to hit before raising another funding round. He felt he needed to make that information available to more startups.

"As investors, we see all that data every day," Mr. Tunguz said. "We just haven't as an industry done a great job of aggregating it, collecting it and presenting it to entrepreneurs."

Mr. Tunguz said the blog has helped him build in-bound interest from entrepreneurs and given him an edge on competitive deals. Redpoint Partner Satish Dharmaraj said Mr. Tunguz has built such a strong reputation among founders that Mr. Dharmaraj often finds entrepreneurs want him on their board, even if Mr. Tunguz didn't bring the deal to Redpoint himself.

Mr. Dharmaraj has been a mentor to Mr. Tunguz and first called his idea for a blog "a yawn." He's been impressed, however, with how Mr. Tunguz has differentiated it from all the other VC blogs.

"He sets his mind to something and delivers with a ridiculous amount of hard work," Mr. Dharmaraj said.

Mr. Tunguz joined Redpoint eight years ago as an associate and rose through the ranks to general partner. He came to the firm after working as a product manager at Google Inc., overseeing AdSense for social networks.

Mr. Tunguz's bets include Looker Data Sciences Inc., Axial Networks Inc., Expensify Inc., Electric Imp Inc., ThredUP Inc. and Dremio Corp. Though they include both enterprise and consumer companies, Mr. Tunguz said the common thread among the companies is data. Looking ahead, he's interested in investing in other applications of artificial intelligence, like language processing.

Though he's proven he can edge out the competition on an investment, it remains to be seen if his unique data-driven approach will generate large returns for Redpoint. None of the companies he's invested in have exited yet.

Despite lacking a record of successful exits, he's proven he can still win competitive deals.

Looker Chief Executive Frank Bien said when his company was raising its Series A in 2013, the market was "hot" for business intelligence companies like his. He said many large venture-capital firms were interested in leading the round, but the company went with Redpoint because as a data company, they wanted to work with an investor who was so data driven.

"Venture capitalists always want to help you, Tom actually can help you," Mr. Bien said. "Tom is a long-view, build-a-business kind of a guy that's who you want around a table with you."

Write to Cat Zakrzewski at "mailto:cat.zakrzewski@wsj.com"

U.S. & CANADA

BUYOUTS

Arsenal Capital Partners, New York

Arsenal Capital Partners held a final closing for its fourth fund at the vehicle's $1.3 billion hard cap. The firm, which targets buy-and-build investments in midmarket health-care and specialty industrial companies, has backed two platforms from Arsenal Capital Partners IV LP. New investors in Fund IV include California State Teachers' Retirement System, PensionDanmark AS and Sentry Insurance. Arsenal typically focuses on platform investments with enterprise values of $100 million to $250 million. The firm seeks to expand them through add-on acquisitions of companies with enterprise values of $500 million or even $1 billion or more, said Arsenal Partner Terrence Mullen. The firm closed on $875 million for Fund IV's predecessor in 2013.

Dyal Capital Partners, New York

Dyal Capital Partners, a unit of Neuberger Berman Group LLC, so far has collected more than $4 billion in investor commitments for a fund that takes direct minority stakes in private-equity managers, said two people familiar with the situation. The tally for Dyal Capital Partners III LP has surpassed the firm's initial target of $3 billion to $4 billion for the fund, which is structured as an evergreen vehicle with an indefinite life. The fund may close at $4.5 billion or even close to $5 billion at the end of the year, one of the people said. Dyal seeks to take minority interests of about 20% in private-equity managers, providing capital for the expansion of business offerings and commitments to their own funds in exchange for a piece of their management and carried interest fees.

EQT, Stockholm

EQT held a final closing for its first fund dedicated to investing in the U.S. The fund, EQT Mid Market US, raised $726 million. The vehicle will acquire midmarket companies with $5 million to $40 million in earnings before interest, taxation, depreciation and amortization, said Matthew Levine, a partner at EQT Mid Market. EQT has invested from the fund in two companies: XP Solutions Inc., which makes drainage and flood-control software; and FocusVision Worldwide Inc., which provides technology services to market-research companies.

Falfurrias Capital Partners, Charlotte, N.C.

Falfurrias Capital Partners surpassed the $225 million goal for its third fund, closing on at least $237.3 million so far, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Falfurrias expects to hold another closing before the end of the year, which should take the fund closer to $275 million, said a person familiar with the fundraising. The firm plans to cap Falfurrias Capital Partners III LP at $300 million, the person said. The amount raised so far makes the fund nearly double the size of its prior fund, which wrapped up with $126 million in 2013. Falfurrias targets investments in companies with $3 million to $25 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm has invested from Fund III so far in one company: Marquis Software Solutions Inc., a Plano, Texas-based provider of data analytics software and services for financial institutions.

FTV Capital, San Francisco

FTV Capital, a growth-equity firm that invests in enterprise technology, financial-services and payments companies, completed fundraising for its fifth fund, FTV V LP, reaching its $850 million hard cap. The vehicle was seeking $700 million, according to a June 29 filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm closed the fund's predecessor in 2014 at $700 million, exceeding a $500 million target. FTV added new investor Texas Municipal Retirement System to the latest fund and attracted returning investors including Skandia Mutual Life Insurance Co., Hillman Co., New York pension funds and funds and accounts managed by NB Alternatives Advisers, said Karen Derr Gilbert, a partner at the firm. FTV invests $10 million to $85 million in companies with annual revenue of $10 million to $100 million.

Gemspring Capital, Westport, Conn.

Midmarket firm Gemspring Capital, which spun out of H.I.G. Capital last year, has begun raising its first pool of capital, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Gemspring Capital Fund I LP and Gemspring Capital Fund I-A, LP are looking to collect $300 million, according to the filings. The firm invests in companies in the U.S. and Canada with annual revenue of $25 million to $250 million, in industries including business services, industrial services, distribution and logistics, health-care services, specialty manufacturing, and media, technology and software.

Highview Capital, Los Angeles

Highview Capital, launched with the backing of investment firm Karlin Asset Management, is raising its inaugural $500 million evergreen fund. The fund will invest in and provide strategic and operational resources to midmarket businesses. Highview will offer a variety of transactions and investment strategies, including buyouts, corporate carve-outs, take-privates, recapitalizations, debt financings, restructurings and bankruptcies, and expedited sale processes.

Kayne Anderson Capital Advisors, Los Angeles

Kayne Anderson Capital Advisors collected $2 billion in investor dollars for its latest energy private-equity fund, which will target upstream oil and gas deals. Kayne Anderson Energy Fund VII LP was seeking $1.6 billion, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing a person familiar with the matter and an investment memorandum. The firm closed on its hard cap for the fund, above the $1.6 billion it collected for the fund's predecessor in 2012.

Metalmark Capital, New York

Metalmark Capital is in talks with investors in a bid to raise $1.5 billion for its third fund, said a person familiar with the matter. If the fund closes on target, it will be $1 billion less than the amount the firm collected for its second fund, Metalmark Capital Partners II LP, which closed at its $2.5 billion hard cap, Private Equity Analyst reported in 2012. Metalmark targets midmarket companies in industries such as energy, health care and industrials.

MTS Health Investors, New York

MTS Health Investors wrapped up its fourth fund at its $365 million hard cap to invest in the health-care services sector. The firm was seeking $300 million for MTS Health Investors IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. MTS raised at least $188 million for its third fund, according to a 2014 SEC filing. MTS targets U.S. companies with valuations of $25 million to $300 million and can pursue larger transactions alongside other sponsors.

New State Capital Partners, Larchmont, N.Y.

Midmarket investor New State Capital Partners collected $131 million for its first pool of capital, which is comprised of New State Capital Partners Fund LP and an affiliated fund. The fund was looking to raise $150 million, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing dated June 8. New State invests $10 million to $45 million of equity in companies with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $8 million to $25 million within the health-care, business services and industrials sectors.

NovaQuest Capital Management, Raleigh, N.C.

NovaQuest Capital Management is seeking $400 million for NovaQuest Private Equity Fund I LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The health-care investor operates through two strategies: NovaQuest Private Equity, which invests in midmarket health-care companies with annual revenue of $20 million to $100 million; and NovaQuest BioPharma, which primarily makes product-based investments in late-stage clinical and commercial biopharmaceutical programs.

Renovus Capital Partners, Wynnewood, Pa.

Renovus Capital Partners wrapped up its second fund focused on education deals at $300 million, which includes leverage from the U.S. Small Business Administration's Small Business Investment Company program. The fund, Renovus Capital Partners II LP, initially targeted $75 million for private investors with a $100 million hard cap, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing Founding Partner Jesse Serventi. The vehicle received backing from investors including the University of Texas Investment Management Co., Prudential Financial Inc. and SunTrust Banks Inc. Renovus initially raised $100 million for its debut effort in 2011 and later added commitments that brought the fund to a total of $185 million, including leverage from the SBA. The firm targets buyout and growth investments in companies with $2 million to $10 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization in industries including the education, training and human-capital sectors.

Riverside Co., Cleveland

Riverside Co. raised $650 million for microcap deals. Riverside Micro-Cap Fund IV LP closed at its hard cap after about a year of fundraising, surpassing a $500 million target, said Riverside Managing Partner Loren Schlachet. The vehicle's predecessor gathered $350 million in private capital along with $150 million from the U.S. Small Business Administration in 2014. Investors in the latest fund include the State of Michigan Retirement System and investment manager Makena Capital Management LLC. The vehicle invests in North American companies with up to $7 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. Riverside also completed fundraising for its first growth-lending vehicle, which aims to invest in enterprise software companies. Riverside Acceleration Capital closed at its $50 million target and has begun investing.

Vista Equity Partners, Austin, Texas

Vista Equity Partners collected at least $8.9 billion in investor commitments for its flagship fund, Vista Equity Partners Fund VI LP, according to an investor memorandum. The firm, which could raise about $10 billion for the fund, passed the initial $8 billion goal for its largest-ever war chest for software and information-technology buyout deals. The firm's latest buyout fund is expected to focus on companies valued at $400 million to $5 billion. Although the fund will make equity investments of $200 million to $1 billion, Vista has the firepower to do bigger deals and to pool capital with co-investors. The fund's predecessor closed at $5.78 billion in 2014.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Bain Capital, Boston

The credit arm of Bain Capital raised more than $500 million in equity commitments for its first business development company, which will target direct loans to small and midmarket companies, said a person with knowledge of the fund. Thanks to rules that allow BDCs to borrow up to one times the equity they collect, the credit unit, Bain Capital Credit, will have more than $1 billion in new money to invest. The firm previously raised about $400 million in equity commitments for a traditional private direct midmarket lending fund last year and manages a number of separate account vehicles focused on direct lending to midmarket companies. According to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission, the BDC will focus on direct loans to midmarket companies with annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $10 million to $150 million. As with most BDCs, Bain must invest at least 70% of the vehicle's capital into U.S. midmarket companies, but can invest up to 30% of the portfolio into what are termed "nonqualified" investments. The firm intends to devote any of its allocation for nonqualified investments into loans to overseas companies, primarily in Europe or Australia, this person said.

KSL Capital Partners, Denver

KSL Capital Partners is preparing to market a new credit fund with an anticipated target of about $750 million to invest in travel and leisure businesses, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm raised $378.5 million for its debut credit pool, KSL Capital Partners Credit Opportunities Fund LP, Private Equity Analyst reported in 2014, citing Chief Executive Eric Resnick. The firm, which traces its roots as a KKR & Co. portfolio company that bought hotels and resorts, began talks with at least some existing investors about its second fund in the summer, the person said. KSL last wrapped up $2.68 billion for its fourth private-equity fund focused on tourism and recreation in 2015.

Monroe Capital, Chicago

Monroe Capital collected $800 million in limited partner commitments for Monroe Capital Private Credit Fund II LP, exceeding a $600 million target. Monroe also intends to leverage the fund, potentially giving it about $1.5 billion of capital to invest. The firm plans to use the new fund primarily to provide senior secured loans and unitranche loans to midmarket companies in the U.S. and Canada, both those with and without private-equity backers. Monroe expects to build a portfolio of 40 to 50 companies for the fund at any one time, said a person familiar with the fund. Over its four-year investment period, it may invest in a total of 70 to 80 companies due to capital recycling, the person said. According to a due diligence memo from Dallas Police & Fire Pension System, which was considering an investment in the fund, Monroe offered investors the option of two subfunds, one levered and one unlevered. As of July 30, the fund had deployed $457 million across 32 loans, according to the memo.

NXT Capital, Chicago

NXT Capital wrapped up its latest private-debt fund, which will have about $900 million to invest. The vehicle, NXT Capital Senior Loan Fund IV LP, held a final closing on about $312 million of equity and is expected to have access to an additional $600 million through a syndicated credit facility provided by Wells Fargo Bank NA. The vehicle invests in senior-debt transactions that are originated and underwritten by NXT's corporate finance group. It primarily provides capital to private equity-backed U.S. midmarket companies. The firm received commitments for the fund primarily from institutional investors, including pension plans, insurance companies and foundations. NXT announced the close of its previous fund with an $800 million investment capacity in 2015.

Penfund, Toronto

Penfund, which makes debt and equity investments in North American midmarket companies, completed fundraising for its fifth vehicle, Penfund Capital Fund V, with 724 million Canadian dollars ($551.6 million). The firm's previous vehicle gathered C$460 million in 2012. The fifth fund, which collected 90% of its capital from institutional investors, is the first Penfund raised with capital from outside Canada, said partner Richard Bradlow. The firm makes junior investments in midmarket companies in North America, providing second-lien, mezzanine and high-yield debt. The firm also makes equity investments, generally co-investing alongside other sponsors. The new fund gives Penfund room for larger transactions than it had previously targeted, said Mr. Bradlow. The firm, which can invest up to C$140 million from the fund, has so far committed about one-third of the vehicle's capital, he said. Penfund invests within the consumer, health-care and business-services sectors.

Seacoast Capital, Danvers, Mass.

Lower midmarket lender Seacoast Capital closed its largest fund to date at $239 million. The vehicle, Seacoast Capital Partners IV LP, was supported by more than 85% of the limited partners that invested in its previous vehicle, which wrapped up in 2013 with more than $150 million. As with the previous fund, Seacoast received a Small Business Investment Company license for Fund IV, giving the firm access to as much as $150 million in matching funds from the U.S. Small Business Administration. Seacoast makes noncontrol investments of $3 million to $20 million, generally through a combination of subordinated debt and preferred or common equity. The firm targets U.S. companies in the manufacturing, distribution, business-services and consumer sectors with annual revenue of more than $10 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization exceeding $2 million.

SECONDARY

Lexington Partners, New York

Lexington Partners plans to soon hold a first closing of its latest midmarket secondary fund that will take the vehicle about three-quarters of the way toward its $2 billion target, said a person familiar with the offering. The firm expects to close on about $1.5 billion for Lexington Middle Market Investors IV LP, which would put the fund ahead of the $1.07 billion the firm raised for its predecessor in 2014. Lexington invests its midmarket secondary funds in portfolios of limited partnerships managed by firms targeting deals with enterprise values of $1 billion or less.

VENTURE CAPITAL

Amino Capital, Palo Alto, Calif.

Amino Capital, a firm focused on artificial intelligence and big data startups, collected $50 million for its second fund, Amino Capital II LP. Formerly known as zPark Venture, Amino Capital previously raised a $4.4 million first fund from among its general partners. The latest fund includes large Chinese technology companies as limited partners. The first fund made 39 seed investments, while the second fund also will invest in some Series A and B deals. The firm seeks to make the first investment in a startup and often looks for companies that are spinoffs from university research. The firm looks for startups in artificial intelligence or big data areas of expertise for its general partners, said Larry Li, a founding partner of Amino. The firm also is interested in startups building on hard science, said Sue Xu, a founding partner at Amino.

Baker & Eastlack Ventures, Del Mar, Calif.

New firm Baker & Eastlack Ventures has raised $3.6 million toward a $25 million fund aimed at medical-technology startups. Partners Kenneth Baker and Robert Eastlack began raising Baker & Eastlack Fund I LP in September with initial investments from individuals. The partners also are in discussions with a medical-device company that has expressed interest in the fund, Dr. Baker said. The team plans to fund eight to 10 medical-device startups that require less than $10 million to secure regulatory clearance, Dr. Baker said.

BioVentures Investors, Wellesley, Mass.

BioVentures Investors closed its fourth fund, BioVentures Investors IV LP, at $87 million to invest in medical-technology companies. The new fund was seeking $100 million, according to an August filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Since its first close, the fund has backed four companies: Deep Vein Medical Inc., which is developing a prosthetic venous valve; CoNextions Medical, a developer of technology for repairing tendons; Endotronix Inc., which is focused on advancing the treatment of heart failure; and Locemia Solutions, which the firm sold to Eli Lilly and Co. BioVentures has raised more than $220 million across its four funds.

Boost VC, San Mateo, Calif.

Adam Draper's startup accelerator Boost VC is raising a new fund, said two people familiar with the matter. The fund will be about $50 million in size and will target startups in such areas as virtual reality and blockchain technology, one person said. Boost, which raised a $6.6 million fund in 2014, invests $10,000 to $100,000 for a 7% stake in each of its accelerator participants.

Canvas Ventures, Portola Valley, Calif.

Canvas Ventures closed its second fund at $300 million, nearly double the size of the fund it raised three years ago. Canvas closed a $175 million fund in 2013 after the firm spun out of Morgenthaler Ventures to invest in early-stage software companies, with a focus on Silicon Valley. Canvas generally invests $5 million to $20 million in a company through Series A and B rounds. The firm will continue to look for financial technology and health-care information-technology companies to back, although it also is interested in hardware and artificial intelligence.

Compound Ventures, New York

Early-stage venture-capital firm Metamorphic Ventures has rebranded as Compound Ventures and raised a new $50 million fund, the firm said in a blog post. Compound's third fund will invest in early-stage startups, according to the post. Its second fund closed with less than $70 million in 2014. The firm has already made five investments out of the new fund in companies including video-conferencing company Pluot Technologies Inc. and loan provider Finova Financial.

Cultivation Capital, St. Louis

Cultivation Capital is seeking $100 million for a financial-technology fund, Cultivation Capital Fintech Fund LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm has so far secured at least $20.5 million for the fund, according to the filing. Cultivation Capital invests primarily in technology and life-sciences companies. The firm also backs accelerator programs focused on areas such as financial technology, agriculture technology and women-led companies.

Fifth Wall Ventures, Los Angeles

A new firm called Fifth Wall Ventures has raised more than $20.3 million for Fifth Wall Ventures SPV I LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Another SEC filing dated May 19 indicated the group was seeking to raise $150 million for a fund called Fifth Wall Ventures LP.

Firebrand Ventures, Kansas City, Mo.

A former managing director for Techstars is looking to collect $15 million to invest in early-stage startups in the Midwest. John Fein, who ran three Techstars accelerator programs in Kansas City, is the founder and managing director of Firebrand Ventures. No commitments were recorded for Firebrand Ventures LP in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Firebrand targets investments in cities including San Antonio, Minneapolis, Boulder, Colo., and Columbus, Ohio.

Foundry Group, Boulder, Colo.

Foundry Group closed a $500 million pool to make growth-stage investments in startups. The vehicle is the firm's second such fund, but unlike its predecessor, which financed companies Foundry Group initially backed from its early-stage funds, Foundry Group Next LP also will target growth deals in companies that are new to the Foundry family and will invest in other early-stage venture funds. The firm missed out on opportunities because its prior Foundry Group Select Fund LP couldn't make outside investments, Managing Director Lindel Eakman said in a blog post. The new fund is more than twice the size of the firm's $225 million Select Fund, raised in 2013.

Greenhouse Capital Partners, Sausalito, Calif.

Seed and growth-equity firm Greenhouse Capital Partners collected at least $5.8 million in new capital for Greenhouse Capital Partners II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Greenhouse seeks companies that promote healthy lifestyles and positive environmental impact in industries ranging from consumer products to Internet-of-Things technologies. The firm wrapped up its first pool of capital in 2007 at $11 million.

Greylock Partners, Menlo Park, Calif.

Greylock Partners, a backer of such highfliers as Facebook Inc., LinkedIn Corp. and Airbnb Inc., has closed its 15th fund with $1 billion, the same amount the firm secured for its 14th fund in 2013. The firm focuses on Series A and Series B investments, with more than 95% of investments from its last fund going into deals at these stages. For consumer investments, Greylock has been betting on network-focused businesses, such as LinkedIn, GoFundMe Inc. and Medium Corp., said James Slavet, a Greylock partner. On the enterprise side, the firm focuses on areas such as increasing productivity, revenue and security, backing companies like Cloudera Inc., Okta Inc. and AppDynamics Inc. The firm also is looking at emerging technologies and platforms, such as messaging, machine learning, robotics, virtual reality and augmented reality, to find the next Facebook or LinkedIn, Mr. Slavet said.

HealthX Ventures, Madison, Wis.

HealthX Ventures closed its debut fund with more than $20 million for investments in health-care software startups. HealthX Ventures Fund I LP, which the firm said was oversubscribed, was seeking as much as $30 million, according to an earlier filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. HealthX, which invests in companies' first rounds, has so far invested in five businesses from the fund.

Owl Ventures, San Francisco

Educational technology investor Owl Ventures is seeking to raise $175 million for a new fund, Owl Ventures II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm raised at least $100.1 million for its first fund in 2015, according to another SEC filing. It counts among its portfolio companies DreamBox Learning Inc., Quizlet Inc. and Accelerate Learning Inc., which all provide online learning tools.

Razor's Edge Management, Reston, Va.

Razor's Edge Management is seeking to raise $250 million for a second fund called Razor's Edge Fund II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm backs companies involved in enterprise technology sectors such as cybersecurity; data science and data infrastructure; emerging devices and sensor technologies; and space technologies.

Rev1 Ventures, Columbus, Ohio

Nationwide Children's Hospital has joined with Rev1 Ventures to form a $5.5 million seed-stage fund devoted to spinouts from the hospital's research institute. Nationwide Children's, of Columbus, Ohio, is finding that technologies that had been percolating in its labs are maturing to the point where they could be the basis for startups, said Timothy Robinson, executive vice president and chief financial and administrative officer. The hospital has made a point of trying to develop those technologies so they can be brought to the patient's bedside, he said. The fund, Rev1 NCH Fund I LLC, will finance four to six businesses over about three years, said Rev1 Chief Executive Tom Walker. The fund could back several types of companies given the various areas in which the hospital has expertise, said Mr. Robinson, including startups in genomics, tissue engineering, infectious diseases, neuroscience and cancer.

SignalFire, San Francisco

SignalFire, a venture firm that mines data on moves by top startup employees for investing signals, is seeking to raise $300 million to $350 million for its second fund, said people familiar with the matter. The firm is nearing a first close on the fund, one of the people said. SignalFire launched its first pool with $53 million in 2015 with backers including fund-of-funds Horsley Bridge Partners, Yahoo Inc. co-founder Jerry Yang and Slack Technologies Inc. co-founder Stewart Butterfield, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. SignalFire, which originally focused on seed investments as an "almost always lead or co-lead" investor on such rounds, also plans to invest in later-stage deals, said people familiar with the matter.

Signia Venture Partners, Menlo Park, Calif.

Signia Venture Partners raised $85 million for its second early-stage fund, Signia Venture Partners II LP. The firm previously raised $75 million for its first pool of capital, which closed in 2013. It has collected about one-quarter of its capital across both funds from its own general partners. The GPs invested $30 million of their own money into Fund I and $15 million of their own capital into Fund II. Signia invests in a small number of startups six to eight a year with a focus on changes in mobility and data. It can lead seed rounds of $1 million to $2 million and Series A rounds of $5 million to $8 million.

Spectrum 28, Menlo Park, Calif.

Spectrum 28, a firm founded by veteran investors from Harbor Pacific Capital and Lightspeed Venture Partners, wrapped up a $170 million fund to focus on companies in sectors such as finance, genomics, digital health, real estate and construction. The fund, Spectrum 28 Capital I LP, was oversubscribed and was initially targeting $125 million, said founders Kent Ho and Lyon Wong. The firm has roughly 15 limited partners including Nan Fung International Holdings Ltd. and Haitong International Securities Group Ltd. Mr. Wong said the firm will make about 40 investments from the fund, ranging from seed to Series C deals. The firm will typically invest $3 million to $6 million in companies, but can write checks ranging from $2 million to $8 million, Mr. Wong added.

Uprfront Ventures, Santa Monica, Calif.

Upfront Ventures is seeking $40 million for a new fund called Upfront IV Ancillary LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't record any capital commitments for the fund in the filing. Upfront primarily invests in seed and Series A financing rounds in technology companies across digital media, commerce, consumer goods and services, and software as a service.

Zetta Venture Partners, San Francisco

Zetta Venture Partners, an early backer of companies including software companies Domo Inc. and InsideSales.com Inc., held a final closing of $100 million for its second fund, from which it plans to invest in 20 or more companies. The firm closed its first fund at $60 million in 2015.

EUROPE

BUYOUTS

Apax Partners, London

Apax Partners will hold a final closing for its ninth buyout fund at its $9 billion upper limit, according to people familiar with the matter. The firm held a $7.9 billion first close on Apax IX, Private Equity Analyst reported in June. It is now in the process of tying up loose ends, the people said. One of the people added the fund was oversubscribed by about $2 billion. The fund's predecessor closed on $7.5 billion in 2013.

EMK Capital, London

A pair of U.K. midmarket private-equity executives is braving the market to launch a first-time investment fund. Edmund Lazarus, managing partner at Bregal Capital, and Mark Joseph, a former partner at midmarket buyout firm Oakley Capital, have set up a U.K. limited liability partnership called EMK Capital, according to a July filing with the U.K.'s Companies House. The fund has a 10-year lifespan and will seek investments "mainly in unquoted shares and securities," according to the filing.

Mayfair Equity Partners, London

Mayfair Equity Partners held a 400 million ($531.7 million) final closing for its debut fund, above a 350 million target. The pool received commitments from a number of institutional investors, including Universities Superannuation Scheme Ltd. and the European Investment Fund, said Daniel Sasaki, the firm's managing partner. Mayfair looks to invest within the consumer and technology, media and telecommunications sectors.

Portobello Capital, Madrid

Portobello Capital has started discussions with investors for its latest fund, which aims to raise  ¬500 million ($562 million), said people familiar with the matter. The firm's last buyout-focused pool, Portobello Fund III, closed on  ¬375 million in 2014. Portobello last year collected  ¬422 million for its first secondary fund, Portobello Capital Secondary Fund I. The firm typically invests  ¬10 million to  ¬100 million of equity in businesses based in Spain with enterprise values of  ¬50 million to  ¬500 million.

Procuritas Partners, Stockholm

Swedish private-equity firm Procuritas Partners is nearing a  ¬300 million ($334.7 million) final closing on its latest fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm started discussions with investors in June and is expected to close the fund on its  ¬300 million target in October, the person said. The vehicle, its sixth private-equity fund, is expected to be larger than Procuritas's previous fund, which closed on  ¬210 million in 2012. Procuritas invests in Nordic companies with enterprise values of  ¬20 million to  ¬100 million.

Union Investment, Frankfurt

Union Investment, the investment arm of Germany's DZ Bank AG, reached its  ¬320 million target for its infrastructure fund, UniInstitutional Infrastruktur SICAV-SIF, and made its final investment. The fund is invested exclusively in plants generating renewable energy.

Volpi Capital, London

Crevan O'Grady, formerly a partner and head of technology, media and telecommunications at 3i Group PLC, and Marco Sodi, a former head of European investments at Veronis Suhler Stevenson, are looking to raise  ¬300 million ($337.2 million) for their first fund at their new firm, Volpi Capital, said people familiar with the matter. Volpi will invest in European businesses with enterprise values of up to  ¬200 million.

Wermuth Asset Management, Berlin

German family office Wermuth Asset Management hit an initial close on a  ¬250 million ($281 million) private-equity fund that will invest in resource-efficient and renewable energy companies throughout Europe, said people familiar with the matter. Green Gateway Fund 2 received more than  ¬10 million of commitments, including a  ¬5 million commitment from a holding company owned by the Wermuth family and further commitments from Dutch impact investor Pymwymic and an unnamed institutional investor. Wermuth Asset Management is expected to reach a  ¬100 million first close on the vehicle in the first quarter of 2017, one person said. The fund is expected to make individual investments of  ¬5 million to  ¬30 million in growth-stage companies in the European Union. Its predecessor, Green Gateway Fund 1, raised  ¬100 million, short of its  ¬250 million target.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

AlpInvest Partners, Amsterdam

AlpInvest Partners, a fund-of-funds adviser backed by Carlyle Group LP, is seeking to raise $1.5 billion to take minority stakes in established private-equity managers, people familiar with the situation said. The fund would be the firm's first pool dedicated to such bets. AlpInvest's new strategy will enable it to tap into the network of firms with which it invests for deal flow, said industry participants. Its ambitions place it among the ranks of Neuberger Berman Group LLC's Dyal Capital Partners, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and others eyeing a portfolio of direct stakes in private-equity firms.

Dutch Mortgage Funding Co., The Hague

Asset manager Dutch Mortgage Funding Co. is eyeing up to  ¬10 billion ($11.16 billion) for a fund that will invest in Dutch residential mortgages. DMFCO is initially targeting  ¬1.5 billion, but aims to raise up to  ¬10 billion for the vehicle, said Managing Partner Jeroen van Hessen. The fund will be the firm's first vehicle to target investors outside of the Netherlands. DMFCO raised its  ¬9 billion debut mortgage lending fund in 2014. That fund, of which about  ¬6 billion has been invested so far, was deployed through DMFCO's mortgage provider Munt Hypotheken. For its latest fund, the firm will set up a new mortgage business to provide loans to Dutch consumers. The firm plans to bring its mortgage products under a new platform, Mr. van Hessen said.

MW Eaglewood, London

A subsidiary of Marshall Wace LLP is launching its first private equity-style fund to invest in marketplace lending and nonbank credit. The hedge fund's MW Eaglewood business is targeting institutional investors in Europe and the U.S. to raise a $300 million fund that will lock up capital for seven years but will pay a coupon rate, which hasn't yet been set, said a person familiar with the matter. The vehicle, which is expected to be raised by early 2017, will pursue an investment strategy similar to that of MW Eaglewood's existing investment trust P2P Global Investments, which invests in peer-to-peer lending, the person said.

Pemberton Asset Management, London

Pemberton Asset Management aims to raise 500 million ($664.7 million) for a new direct-lending strategy that will capture opportunities from the Brexit fallout in the U.K. midmarket sector. Managing Partner Symon Drake-Brockman said the strategy was being launched, in part, to "fill a gap that will be caused by some foreign banks reducing their exposure to the U.K. because of Brexit." Pemberton has secured cornerstone commitments from Legal & General Group PLC, which bought a 40% stake in the firm in 2014, and another unnamed financial institution. The strategy will supply senior secured loans and unitranche loans to U.K. midmarket companies, Mr. Drake-Brockman said. It will invest alongside banks, which have restrictions on the amount they can lend to individual companies in the U.K. market due to their internal credit limits. The fundraising comes as Pemberton nears a final close on its inaugural European midmarket debt fund, which is expected to close at more than  ¬1 billion, Mr. Drake-Brockman said.

UBS Asset Management, Zurich

UBS Asset Management closed its debut infrastructure debt fund, Archmore Infrastructure Debt Platform, at  ¬570 million ($635.9 million) to invest in opportunities throughout Western Europe. The capital was raised from 17 institutional investors, including insurance companies, pension funds and family offices, from eight European countries and Japan. Since the fund's first close in 2014, UBS has deployed  ¬220 million in four investments within the transportation, energy and social infrastructure sectors.

Pro Private Markets
VC Focus: Venture-Capital Investment Stabilizes as Late-Stage Bubble Deflates

By Russ Garland with reports from Tomio Geron 
1,049 words
31 October 2016
13:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The age of the unicorn seems to be over in venture capital as fewer giant rounds for later-stage companies have caused investment levels to fall closer to where they were before the run-up began in 2014.

At the peak of the mini-bubble, investors, including mutual funds and private-equity firms, pumped money into startups with valuations of a billion dollars or more, dubbed unicorns. In the third quarter of 2015, equity financings for U.S.-based venture-backed companies hit $20.07 billion, more than double the amount from the same period two years earlier, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by Private Equity Analyst published Dow Jones & Co. This year, the third quarter posted total investment of $11.72 billion, closer to 2013 levels.

Later rounds accounted for $7.02 billion during the third quarter, a bit less than the second quarter's $7.34 billion, according to VentureSource. That is more than the $5.43 billion they garnered in the third quarter of 2013 but well below the $12.79 billion in later-stage capital raised in last year's third quarter at the peak of the unicorn phenomenon.

The median size of a later-stage round slipped to $8.45 million in the third quarter, the lowest level since the third quarter of 2010. A year ago, the median was $18 million as money poured into companies with valuations of $1 billion or more.

Comparing third-quarter deal lists for 2015 and 2016 drives home the sense that giant later-stage rounds have lost their magic. There were 36 financings of $100 million or more in the third quarter of 2015, according to VentureSource. Topping the list were Uber Technologies Inc. and Social Finance Inc., which each raised $1 billion.

From July through September of this year, there were 11 deals of $100 million or more. The largest was an $850 million round for Airbnb Inc.

Although the pace has slowed, highly valued companies that are performing well can raise big rounds, which can skew quarterly numbers. In the second quarter of 2016, two companies, Uber and Snapchat Inc., raised a total of nearly $4.8 billion, accounting for 30% of investment in U.S. venture-backed companies during that period.

"Some categories are getting significant capital and continuing to see a bid-up on valuation, and then there are other areas that are less interesting," said Aspect Ventures co-founder Jennifer Fonstad, speaking at the recent WSJ Pro Private Equity Analyst Conference in New York. "A lot of that's driven by an economic cycle, and so, for example, at the beginning of an economic cycle there's a lot more investment in consumer-oriented software and services and e-commerce &and at the end of the cycle, you tend to see investments in enterprise-oriented businesses, like security."

Venture firms aren't having trouble raising funds, so investors are ready to make investments, despite the slowdown at the later stage, said Keval Desai, a partner at InterWest Partners. Investors have been shifting their attention to new hot categories such as machine learning, computer vision, virtual reality and augmented reality, he said. "Early stage has been pretty healthy, and deals are getting done at the seed and Series A level," Mr. Desai said.

Investment in first rounds during the third quarter totaled $1.89 billion, according to VentureSource. That is slower than last year's pace but far from the drop off seen in later rounds.

Most of the later-stage money went to technology companies, and they have felt the brunt of the investment slowdown. Health-care investment has been steadier. Investors pumped $3.23 billion into health care in the third quarter, up from $3.05 billion in the second quarter and down slightly from $3.61 billion in the third quarter of 2015.

Early this year, some technology company stocks in the public markets dropped sharply and the venture market also slowed, said David Yuan, a general partner at growth-stage firm Technology Crossover Ventures. But that has started to shift with more reasonable valuations for private companies and with recent IPOs such as Nutanix Inc. and Twilio Inc., Mr. Yuan said. "I think we're in a transition phase here. We've seen activity pick up considerably."

"People faced a lot of headwinds over the last three quarters," said Ben Narasin, a partner at Canvas Ventures. "But now we've moved into a calm sea with an occasional gust of wind."

Fintech Investors Find Comfort in Insurance

By Yuliya Chernova

The unexpectedly large turnout at a recent insurance technology conference in Las Vegas speaks to the changes in the landscape of the financial technology sector.

Organizers of InsureTech Connect had to scramble to pump audio and video from the opening session into an overflow room after a security guard blocked entry to the main hall as it got full, said Jay Weintraub, founder of the inaugural conference. He said he had set an aggressive target of 1,000 attendees, but 1,500 showed up.

Lending startups ruled the roost a year ago, but now many are in retreat. Conversely, companies operating in the insurance industry are picking up steam and gaining more attention.

About $945.6 million flowed into fintech in the third quarter of 2016, according to VentureSource, a 57% drop from the same period a year ago, when VCs deployed $2.2 billion into the broader sector. The amount invested in the most recent quarter was also down from the second quarter, when fintech companies raised $1.04 billion.

Investment in insurance technology companies is on the rise. VCs put $167.8 million to work in insurance in the third quarter. The sector saw more funding in each of this year's quarters than in any of the similar periods last year.

"Over the past 18 months or so the number of startup founders interested in the insurance space has grown dramatically," said Rodolfo Gonzalez, a partner at Foundation Capital. For venture investors who are looking for the next sector and view lending as "saturated," Mr. Gonzalez said, developments in insurance are encouraging.

Business
Where Are the Tech IPOs? Michael Grimes of Morgan Stanley says not to worry, they're coming back next year

851 words
31 October 2016
02:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

As managing director and head of global technology investment banking at Morgan Stanley, Michael Grimes is one of the tech industry's most prominent bankers.

He sat down with Wall Street Journal Financial Editor Dennis Berman to discuss the dearth of tech initial public offerings and why he thinks that's about to change.

Here are edited excerpts:

Pent-up demand

MR. BERMAN: You're arguably the most important banker in Silicon Valley. So where are the IPOs?

MR. GRIMES: They're coming. There have been 14 so far this year in tech, which is down from an average of 35 to 40 and probably over 40 for multiple years since 2004. I'd say next year you're going to be back probably to triple the current volume, about 30 or 40. There's really robust, pent-up demand right now.

MR. BERMAN: Why haven't companies in 2016 been willing to sell their shares?

MR. GRIMES: I think there's a lag effect. If we look back to January-February, if we look back at the prior 12-month class of tech IPOs, 80% of them were trading below issue price. There was a paucity of demand, and that kind of slows the pipeline.

Probably around the April-May time frame, that changed. And it's taken a while to percolate through. Add in the fact that anybody who wanted to raise money privately in the 2013 to 2015 time frame could, so [many startups] have capital and some have said they can benefit by staying private.

But the market now is really healthy. Demand is probably as high as I've seen it. Supply will catch up.

MR. BERMAN: Is there a mismatch between what the price expectations are between the sale side and the buyers?

MR. GRIMES: There has been over the past 12 months, but the mismatch is shrinking.

MR. BERMAN: And where are they meeting? More on the buyer's side or more on the seller's side?

MR. GRIMES: They're starting to meet closer on the seller's side. It has been on the buyer's side, for sure. But it has been moving up and it will continue to move up because of the capital being returned to investors from all the M&A activity.

MR. BERMAN: You have some pretty shocking numbers about the amount of money that's come in, and the amount of money that needs to go out. Can you share?

MR. GRIMES: It's as wide of a disparity as we've seen in 20 years. There has been $125 billion returned through tech M&A, just the cash portion, into the hands of investors last year. Another $75 billion this year so far, so $200 billion or $205 billion cumulative over that year and a half.

If you add up new issuance of all IPOs in tech and all secondary offerings, it's about $25 billion to $26 billion. So an eight-to-one ratio, double or triple what it has been in a year in the last 15 years.

New buyers emerge

MR. BERMAN: So a big part of this whole equation is M&A. And something fascinating is happening there. Can you explain?

MR. GRIMES: It's really that the buyers for U.S. tech assets have changed quite a bit, maybe permanently, and this is really new news.

Five to 10 years ago, the tech giants or tech midcaps won most tech assets. What has happened quietly is that they've gone from winning 58% to 60% to half that as three new groups of buyers have [emerged].

Private-equity firms are buying public tech companies. They are outbidding strategic buyers, and that's new. It has upped their win rate.

Then there are foreign buyers. China has gone from $300 million to $40 billion in a $250 billion tech M&A market. That's a pretty big portion up from a very small portion, so they're winning, too.

And the most interesting [group is the nontech firms.] General Electric's Jeff Immelt said at the end of 2013 that if you went to sleep an industrial company, you woke up the next morning a software, data and analytics company. So they've always been kind of kicking the tires on tech assets when we have them for sale, but they hadn't been paying up. Look what has happened in the past year.

MR. BERMAN: They have no other choice, right?

MR. GRIMES: Now they have no choice. They see the imperative. So Verizon Communications wins Fleetmatics Group, and Wal-Mart wins web retailer Jet.com. And they paid up. So they're up 250% in terms of volume.
