Business
The State of the Tech Startup: A Bit of a Mixed Bag; Fan Bao and Bill Gurley say valuations have pulled back, but it's far from a collapse

889 words
31 October 2016
02:13
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Startup valuations have inflated to extraordinary levels witness the rise of "unicorns," or startups with valuations of at least $1 billion. But fears of a big slowdown are rippling through Silicon Valley and China.

To get a handle on the situation, The Wall Street Journal's editor in chief, Gerard Baker, spoke with the founder and chief executive of investment bank China Renaissance, Fan Bao, and Bill Gurley, general partner of venture-capital firm Benchmark. Here are edited excerpts of the conversation.

The valuation picture

MR. BAKER: Bill, a year ago, you talked with one of my colleagues about valuations for some of these unicorns and about how choppy some of them looked. There's been somewhat of an adjustment in the last year. Tell us what's going on.

MR. GURLEY: It's a bit of a mixed bag from what I've seen. You do have an increase in the number of bankruptcies, layoffs, downsizings. But you haven't had the kind of wholesale kaput that you had in 2001 that scared everyone out of the water. As a result, you simultaneously still have new money coming in. That new money is not as educated as the previous money.

MR. BAKER: There's concerns about a lot of overvaluations in the Chinese market. What's your sense of where the Chinese tech market is?

MR. BAO: I think the early-stage deals, the temperature cooled down a little bit. But the late-stage deals, they still attract a lot of liquidity. The banks are providing financing behind that.

MR. BAKER: One big difference between now and, say, 2000-2001 is the rate environment. In 2000, the rates were starting to go up. Even though you've had a bit of a backup of bond yields in the last month, you've still got this very low-yielding environment.

MR. GURLEY: I don't think there's ever been a time where global interest rates have been this low for this long.

I'll borrow from Stan Druckenmiller, who gave a speech a year and a half ago. He said, "You're just going to see asset bubbles everywhere." Certainly, real estate, energy. This is happening everywhere because money's looking for a home.

MR. BAKER: Venture-capital investment in the U.S. from China, that's something else that continues to drive up valuations or prevent them from falling back down to more earthly levels. Do you expect that to continue?

MR. BAO: I think on the demand side, definitely, there is a need. Just simply a lack of enough investment opportunities in China. Secondly, the currency issue, RMB's depreciation. And obviously there is more demand on the part of investors to diversify assets outside China.

MR. BAKER: We haven't seen the big IPOs that we'd seen over the last few years. Is the hunger for IPOs coming back?

MR. GURLEY: One of the things that is particularly unique about this moment is we have record venture returns and record-low liquidity.

I think of it as paper returns no different than you might have had in other financial bubbles where things get marked up, and they're not actually trading at their value.

People that invest in the venture funds, they make distributions to their endowments and foundations. And they get bonused based on those returns. Because they're clocking it and it's working, their boss says, "Maybe we should give more money to this sector." It's a system that can be self-reinforcing in a very negative way.

MR. BAKER: Let's talk about Chinese acquisitions in the U.S. Do you expect to see more of that in the next year?

MR. BAO: There's been a lot of deals on the content side. There should be more deals on the technology side as well. Because if you look at our national champions, I think they realized they aren't invested in core technology compared to their U.S. counterparts. So there is a lot of catch-up to do. The best place to pick it up is still in this market.

Picking up companies

MR. BAKER: What about M&A among Silicon Valley companies? Whenever anybody's in play, there's a rumor that Google is interested. Time Warner. Do you expect to see Google, Facebook, Apple going big there?

MR. GURLEY: I think from a venture capitalist's perspective, it's almost an ideal situation. Because you don't have a single kingdom.

When I first got started, Microsoft owned the world. You had to deal with what they did. Here, we've got five companies of significant market cap [Google, Facebook, Apple, Amazon and Microsoft] who are all competing with one another. Many of them have large cash balance sheets. So that sounds to me like a perfect recipe.

Pro Private Markets
LBO Focus: Tech Boom Helps Sponsors Recover M&A Ground in Third Quarter

By Chris Cumming 
1,329 words
31 October 2016
13:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

After sputtering at the start of the year, private-equity deal makers hit the gas in the third quarter with the help of a jump start by the technology sector.

Tech was far and away the most popular area for deal making by financial sponsors in the third quarter, and a string of big deals in the sector helped private equity shake off the mergers and acquisitions lull that began the year.

Though the overall number of deals and total deal value slipped from the third quarter of last year, the tech sector more than pulled its weight, accounting for 46.2% of the total value of sponsor-backed M&A volume announced in the third quarter of this year, according to data tracker Dealogic Ltd.

"Dollar value and volume are both down from last year but not cratering, and we're also seeing an increase in both from [the first quarter of] this year," said Barry Curtis, who heads the private-equity group in Deloitte & Touche LLP's M&A advisory-services practice. "Tech is clearly leading the way."

Tech once was considered a bad fit for the leveraged-buyout model, but times have changed. Three of the five biggest private equity-backed deals from July through September were for technology companies: Apollo Global Management LLC's $4.4 billion purchase of Rackspace Hosting Inc., Platinum Equity's $4 billion deal for Emerson Electric Co.'s computing and power business and KKR & Co.'s $3.3 billion takeover of Epicor Software Corp.

This cluster of large deals made it an unusually good quarter for private equity-backed tech deals there were 40 deals announced, worth a combined $21.17 billion and is a reflection of private-equity firms' growing comfort in committing large sums to the sector, said Hugh MacArthur, a partner and the head of the global private-equity practice at consulting firm Bain & Co.

Private-equity firms have turned their sights from turnarounds to large, healthy tech companies, which often garner higher prices and lead to bigger deals. Other pricey private equity-backed tech deals in the third quarter were TPG's acquisition of a majority stake in McAfee, the computer-security unit of Intel Corp., and Vista Equity Partners' $1.61 billion take-private deal for network security company Infoblox Inc.

Tech could continue to be the major driver of sponsor-backed M&A for some time because of the amount of cash private-equity firms are preparing to deploy in the sector. Vista, of Austin, Texas, has collected at least $8.9 billion for its latest fund targeting software and information-technology buyouts and could gather as much as $10 billion, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. Thoma Bravo, a firm that invests in software and tech-based services, closed a $7.6 million fund in September.

All this fresh capital could help fourth-quarter sponsor-backed M&A activity approach or even exceed that of the third, industry observers say.

Along with their healthy appetite for tech, private-equity firms are beginning to dip their toes back into energy, betting that oil prices could be ready to rebound.

"There is a tremendous pipeline of deals [and] a tremendous desire to get things done and push things through," said Mr. MacArthur. "We can't tell what deals will go through, but we'd expect to see a [fourth quarter] that is ahead of even the [third-quarter] base."

The third quarter was strong overall compared with historical trends for private equity-backed M&A, though propped up somewhat by big tech deals. Sponsors announced 160 investments totaling $45.79 billion, the third-largest quarterly dollar figure since 2007, according to Dealogic.

Still, the number of deals and the total deal value were lower than the same period a year ago, when PE firms announced 215 acquisitions for a total of $56.74 billion. The decline in PE-backed deals mirrored a dip in U.S. M&A overall, with total announced deal value slipping to $371 billion, 43% lower than in the third quarter of 2015.

But the third quarter was an improvement from the early months of the year, when many firms found their deal pipelines empty after a year of frenetic activity, and economic anxiety abroad particularly over China's slowdown caused others to put deals on hold. In the first quarter, 157 U.S. sponsor-backed deals worth a total of $17.51 billion were announced, the lowest quarterly deal volume in four years. Deal volume rebounded to a healthy but unspectacular $33.95 billion in the second quarter.

M&A has been muted this year compared to last because of "pricing and a little bit of caution," as sponsors have begun to shy from the high prices some sellers are demanding, said Mr. Curtis. This is particularly true for larger acquisitions, which typically command higher multiples. The largest U.S. private equity-backed deal announced this year was Hellman & Friedman's planned acquisition of health-care services provider MultiPlan Inc. for $7.5 billion.

Instead of chasing whales, private-equity firms have sought to scoop up some smaller fish, focusing on deals in the $1 billion to $4 billion range or slapping together a series of small deals to gain scale.

"A lot of private-equity [firms], given the high valuations, are finding deals in the middle market and making a series of add-on transactions later to form a large company that they can exit successfully," said Mr. Curtis.

Apart from tech, other important sectors had muted quarters. The $21.17 billion in tech deals dwarfed the next most active sector, professional services, which had 23 deals announced totaling $6.07 billion in value, according to Dealogic. The largest was the purchase by Onex Corp. and Baring Private Equity Asia of Clarivate Analytics, Thomson Reuters Corp.'s intellectual property and science business, for $3.55 billion, according to Dealogic.

The second-biggest sponsor-backed deal last quarter, behind Apollo's purchase of Rackspace, was the $4 billion takeover of mixed-martial-arts fight organizer Ultimate Fighting Championship by the talent agency WME-IMG Hollywood, the investment firm of billionaire Michael Dell, and private-equity firms Silver Lake and KKR & Co.

Deals in health care, one of the most popular sectors for private equity over the past few years, slipped somewhat after a strong second quarter. It was the third most popular sector, with 23 deals totaling $4.53 billion, a dip from the second quarter's total announced deal volume of $11.56 billion in 16 deals. The largest health-care deals in the quarter were Advent International Corp.'s purchase of a 50% stake in InVentiv Group Holdings Inc. for $1.9 billion and Cinven's $1.4 billion acquisition of clinical-trial company BioClinica Inc.

Still, health care's long-term resilience and growth trends suggest private-equity interest won't wane any time soon, industry participants say.

"Health care has the recession-proof dynamic that private equity likes, and that allows firms to lean in a little more on pricing," said Andrew Cristinzio, the head of private equity for consulting firm PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP.

Though private-equity firms are eschewing the $6 billion or $7 billion deals they sought before the financial downturn of the last decade, the M&A pace should remain robust through the next year, despite complaints about high multiples, observers say. With cost of capital low and stock prices high, the time "is very ripe for corporate acquisitions," and "private equity is definitely keeping its share" of the M&A market, said Mr. Curtis.

"It's putting capital to work and it's very active, but we're just not seeing the big public-to-privates that we've seen in the past," he said.

Unbabel Gets $5M for AI-Powered Translation Services

As businesses increasingly court international customers, they run into language barriers.

Y Combinator alum Unbabel Inc. is a software specialist that hopes to act as a multilingual liaison with a combination of artificial intelligence and human interpreters to help companies translate customer service requests quickly. It network of about 40,000 human editors supports 24 different languages.

Unbabel's software roughly translates a message from a customer service representative, and then one of Unbabel's contractors checks and makes any needed corrections. The San Francisco company announced on Monday it raised $5 million from lead investors Notion Capital and Caixa Capital, having raised $8 million to date.

Faber Ventures and Schilling Capital Partners also participated.

The three-year-old company initially focused on email requests and was able to translate questions from customers and responses from companies in less than 20 minutes.

But as customer service increasingly moves to chat and messaging platforms, Unbabel Chief Executive Vasco Calais Pedro says the company has sped up the time it takes to do a translation. It now can translate a message in less than 90 seconds.

Mr. Pedro said the company brought on London-based as Notion Capital as an investor because of its understanding of European markets.

"Europe is the biggest translation market," he said.

The company lists Oculus VR, Pinterest Inc., Trello Inc., Change.org Inc. and MyFitnessPal Inc. among its customers on its website.

WSJDLIVE (A Special Report) --- Where Are the Tech IPOs? Michael Grimes of Morgan Stanley says not to worry, they're coming back next year

By Dennis Berman 
822 words
31 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

As managing director and head of global technology investment banking at Morgan Stanley, Michael Grimes is one of the tech industry's most prominent bankers.

He sat down with Wall Street Journal Financial Editor Dennis Berman to discuss the dearth of tech initial public offerings and why he thinks that's about to change.

Here are edited excerpts:

MR. BERMAN: You're arguably the most important banker in Silicon Valley. So where are the IPOs?

MR. GRIMES: They're coming. There have been 14 so far this year in tech, which is down from an average of 35 to 40 -- and probably over 40 for multiple years since 2004. I'd say next year you're going to be back probably to triple the current volume, about 30 or 40. There's really robust, pent-up demand right now.

MR. BERMAN: Why haven't companies in 2016 been willing to sell their shares?

MR. GRIMES: I think there's a lag effect. If we look back to January-February, if we look back at the prior 12-month class of tech IPOs, 80% of them were trading below issue price. There was a paucity of demand, and that kind of slows the pipeline. Probably around the April-May time frame, that changed. And it's taken a while to percolate through. Add in the fact that anybody who wanted to raise money privately in the 2013 to 2015 time frame could, so [many startups] have capital and some have said they can benefit by staying private.

But the market now is really healthy. Demand is probably as high as I've seen it. Supply will catch up.

MR. BERMAN: Is there a mismatch between what the price expectations are between the sale side and the buyers?

MR. GRIMES: There has been over the past 12 months, but the mismatch is shrinking.

MR. BERMAN: And where are they meeting? More on the buyer's side or more on the seller's side?

MR. GRIMES: They're starting to meet closer on the seller's side. It has been on the buyer's side, for sure. But it has been moving up and it will continue to move up because of the capital being returned to investors from all the M&A activity.

MR. BERMAN: You have some pretty shocking numbers about the amount of money that's come in, and the amount of money that needs to go out. Can you share?

MR. GRIMES: It's as wide of a disparity as we've seen in 20 years. There has been $125 billion returned through tech M&A, just the cash portion, into the hands of investors last year. Another $75 billion this year so far, so $200 billion or $205 billion cumulative over that year and a half.

If you add up new issuance of all IPOs in tech and all secondary offerings, it's about $25 billion to $26 billion. So an eight-to-one ratio, double or triple what it has been in a year in the last 15 years.

MR. BERMAN: So a big part of this whole equation is M&A. And something fascinating is happening there. Can you explain?

MR. GRIMES: It's really that the buyers for U.S. tech assets have changed quite a bit, maybe permanently, and this is really new news. Five to 10 years ago, the tech giants or tech midcaps won most tech assets. What has happened quietly is that they've gone from winning 58% to 60% to half that as three new groups of buyers have [emerged]. Private-equity firms are buying public tech companies. They are outbidding strategic buyers, and that's new. It has upped their win rate.

Then there are foreign buyers. China has gone from $300 million to $40 billion in a $250 billion tech M&A market. That's a pretty big portion up from a very small portion, so they're winning, too.

And the most interesting [group is the nontech firms.] General Electric's Jeff Immelt said at the end of 2013 that if you went to sleep an industrial company, you woke up the next morning a software, data and analytics company. So they've always been kind of kicking the tires on tech assets when we have them for sale, but they hadn't been paying up. Look what has happened in the past year.

MR. BERMAN: They have no other choice, right?

MR. GRIMES: Now they have no choice. They see the imperative. So Verizon Communications wins Fleetmatics Group, and Wal-Mart wins web retailer Jet.com. And they paid up. So they're up 250% in terms of volume.

By Telis Demos

31 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B12
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Silicon Valley banking upstarts have technology for almost everything, but they are still trying to catch up with thieves posing as customers.

New research shows that on an average day, about 4.5% of people who take an unsecured personal loan go back for seconds at other lenders later that day. The behavior, called "loan stacking," more than doubled from 2013 to 2015, according to credit-reporting firm TransUnion.

Here is why the lenders care: Borrowers who take out a second loan within 15 days are four times as likely to be later identified as fraudsters, the credit firm found. A third loan makes borrowers 10 times as likely to be frauds. "When money is moving out the door so quickly, people take everything in one shot and game the system," said Pat Phelan, senior vice president at TransUnion.

Online lenders sometimes have a tougher job than banks or credit unions to screen customers, especially because many tout the speed with which consumers can get loan approvals. It is also more common for online lenders to attract people they have never done business with before, because they don't offer other financial products such as checking accounts or credit cards. The web-based companies typically make decisions to lend people up to tens of thousands of dollars in as little as a day or two on the basis of a borrower's credit and income history.

Similar frauds, by contrast, are much less common with bank-issued credit cards. An earlier TransUnion study estimated that only 0.1% of credit-card applicants took a card, made a few payments, and then maxed it out and vanished, a scam known as a "bust-out."

Now, some of the biggest online lenders, including LendingClub Corp. and Avant Inc., are taking more steps to combat the problem by sharing data about who is taking out their loans as well as using new technology to verify borrowers' identity through mobile phones.

TransUnion this month launched an exchange where lenders can share data in real time about who is seeking loans, for the purpose of detecting multiple applications. Using a neutral party such as TransUnion, lenders can see if people are taking out multiple loans without directly sharing customer information. LendingClub and Avant are among those that have signed up.

Separately, LendingClub, Prosper Marketplace Inc. and other firms last week said they joined a consortium created by ID Analytics LLC to detect multiple loan applications. "Leveraging collective data . . . will further strengthen the experience for borrowers and lenders alike," a LendingClub spokesman said. He said the lender "relies on a variety of internal and external tools" to analyze fraud risk.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

28 October 2016

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Mess Spreads at India Conglomerate Tata

India's most important conglomerate is in turmoil. Investors seem to be taking the news too lightly.

The board of Tata Sons ousted its first nonfamily chairman, Cyrus Mistry, earlier this week, reversing what investors had seen as a fresh start for the tightly controlled group of companies, which generates $100 billion in sales across 29 listed companies and other unlisted ones. Interests span tea and coffee, IT outsourcing and Jaguar automobiles.

Mr. Mistry fired back Wednesday in a letter, accusing his former employer of hiding $18 billion of potential write-downs over an unspecified period across five Tata companies, including Tata Motors and Tata Steel Europe. Mr. Mistry complained of split power centers and being cornered into being a "lame duck." Notably, he alleges potential financial issues at the various companies and that Ratan Tata's behind-the-scenes maneuvers reduced the board members to "mere postmen," whose actions risked the company's violating securities regulations.

Shares of Tata Motors, which owns luxury brand Jaguar Land Rover, have fallen 6% since Mr. Mistry's ousting but still are up 34% this year. JLR, which makes up about three-quarters of the unit's revenue, faces storm clouds in its best market, China. A string of recent quarterly earnings from Chinese car makers points to severe pricing pressures and a turning tide. Tata Motors gets a credit-rating lift from parent Tata Sons. If this is imperiled, things look less robust for its expensive capital structure.

The other major concern should be with Tata Steel. The highly indebted steel company -- with debt approximately 10 times earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, according to Lucror Analytics -- remains fraught with issues including the contentious sale of its U.K. operations and a now-teetering plan to combine its European operations with Thyssenkrupp AG.

Shares of most of the Tata listed companies have fallen this week but not by much, given the dysfunction shown. Meanwhile, yields on Tata Motors and Tata Steel bonds due 2020 have ticked up since Mr. Mistry was ousted, as bondholders fret about whether Mr. Mistry's complaints are real or merely the ravings of a disgruntled ex-chairman.

It is worth noting that Mr. Mistry's family retains 18% of holding company Tata Sons, so his incentives to create value remain.

Back in 2012, it took the conglomerate a year to appoint Mr. Mistry as chairman when Mr. Tata, the family patriarch, supposedly stepped aside. With no heirs, Mr. Tata was, in effect, anointing Mr. Mistry, whose sister is married to Mr. Tata's half brother, as the next generation.

Now, Mr. Tata is officially back at the helm -- but with a huge mess on his hands.

-- Anjani Trivedi

U.K. Economy Can't Ignore Brexit Forever

The U.K. economy hasn't been knocked off course by Brexit yet. That will curb the Bank of England's appetite for action for now.

Thursday's data showed forecast-beating growth in the third quarter of 0.5%, certainly welcome news, and in line with the average pace of the previous four quarters, suggesting some momentum. Gross domestic product is now 8.2% above its precrisis peak, the Office for National Statistics said.

But there also are familiar reasons for concern. U.K. officials have talked a lot about rebalancing the economy to revive manufacturing and reduce reliance on services, but nothing has changed. Growth in the third quarter was all down to services, with agriculture, production and construction output declining.

GDP is backward-looking, and the Brexit debate has moved on since the end of September. The clash between the politics and economics over the U.K.'s departure from the European Union has only really just got going. Sterling has fallen by more than 5% against the dollar in October, shaken by rhetoric from U.K. politicians that has suggested a greater risk of economic disruption in the future.

That is likely to push inflation up further and faster than the BOE had been expecting. It also likely is to lead to real wages being squeezed again in 2017 -- a painful prospect for U.K. consumers. The lack of clarity over the shape and form of Brexit is likely to weigh on investment, too.

The BOE came in all guns blazing in August, cutting rates and restarting bond purchases. Next week's policy meeting looks unlikely now to lead to new action, as the economy has clearly been more resilient than policy makers feared.

The reaction of sterling, which has become the Brexit barometer, tells the story: It rose briefly against the dollar Thursday morning but then fell back. Good economic news is only worth so much in the face of so much political uncertainty.

-- Richard Barley

Deutsche Is

Missing Out

On Trading

Bond trading has given a big boost to Barclays and Deutsche Bank in the third quarter, but the recovery isn't nearly as spectacular as for U.S. rivals.

The difference in performance between the two sides of the Atlantic Ocean will add to the view that stronger and less distracted U.S. banks are continuing to take market share from European rivals, many of which are still restructuring and reshaping their businesses eight years after the financial crisis.

Deutsche's bond-trading revenue was up 14% in the third quarter from the same period last year, a much weaker recovery than at Barclays, which had 40% growth in credit and macro revenues combined.

Barclays is much more tilted toward U.S. markets, and its numbers were in line with U.S. peers, most of whose third-quarter revenues were up by 35% to 50% from the same quarter last year. However, Barclays reports its revenue in pounds, and so got a big boost from the fall in the British currency since the Brexit vote at the end of the second quarter.

With sterling down drastically against the U.S. dollar and the euro since the summer, Barclays's credit and macro revenues in constant currencies might actually have fallen quarter over quarter, depending on the mix of businesses between the U.K. and U.S., and be up only perhaps 20% compared with the third quarter last year.

Numbers like these show that even with a recovery in bond trading, European banks still seem to be falling behind.

-- Paul J. Davies

Overheard

One-time sports-apparel juggernaut Under Armour exhorts potential customers to "Protect This House."

The company itself racked up a formidable record in protecting its reputation for consistency.

It has strung together 24 consecutive quarters of beating analysts' consensus expectations and nearly that many of achieving at least 20% year-over-year sales growth.

But, like all sorts of impressive individual statistics, they pale in comparison to the scoreboard investors really care about: the share price.

They had no complaints about Under Armour for years as the share price surged over 30-fold in a little over six years through last September.

Since then, though, it has shed more than two-thirds of its value, including a 13% plunge Tuesday after the company reported its quarterly results.

No, Under Armour didn't lag behind expectations. In fact, it beat analyst consensus by 17%, according to FactSet.

Yet the stock has now dropped following eight of its past 13 results announcements, not counting a plunge after a surprise profit warning earlier this year.

With investors no longer impressed by the big numbers it is putting up, this house is in need of some remodeling.

Borrower or Fraudster? Online Lenders Scramble to Tell the Difference; As potentially fraudulent behavior increases, lenders step up security checks on applicants

By Telis Demos 
953 words
27 October 2016
16:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Silicon Valley banking upstarts have technology for almost everything, but they're still trying to catch up with thieves posing as their customers.

New research shows that on an average day, about 4.5% of people who take an unsecured personal loan go back for seconds at other lenders later that day. The behavior, called "loan stacking," more than doubled from 2013 to 2015, according to research from credit-reporting firm TransUnion.

Here's why the lenders care: Borrowers who take out a second loan within 15 days are four times as likely to be later identified as fraudsters, the credit firm found. A third loan makes borrowers 10 times as likely to be frauds. "When money is moving out the door so quickly, people take everything in one shot and game the system," said Pat Phelan, senior vice president at TransUnion.

Online lenders sometimes have a tougher job than banks or credit unions to screen customers, especially since many tout the speed with which consumers can get loan approvals. It is also more common for online lenders to attract people they have never done business with before, since they don't offer other financial products such as checking accounts or credit cards. The web-based companies typically make decisions to lend people up to tens of thousands of dollars in as little as a day or two on the basis of a borrower's credit and income history.

Similar frauds, by contrast, are much less common with bank-issued credit cards. An earlier TransUnion study estimated that only 0.1% of credit-card applicants took a card, made a few payments, and then maxed it out and vanished, a scam known as a "bust-out."

Now, some of the biggest online lenders, including LendingClub Corp. and Avant Inc., are taking more steps to combat the problem by sharing data about who is taking out their loans as well as using new technology to verify borrowers' identity through mobile phones.

TransUnion this month launched an exchange where lenders can share data in real time about who is seeking loans, for the purpose of detecting multiple applications. Using a neutral party such as TransUnion, lenders can see if people are taking out multiple loans without directly sharing customer information. LendingClub and Avant are among those that have signed up; TransUnion says eight of the 10 largest online lenders are participating.

Separately, LendingClub, Prosper Marketplace Inc. and other firms this week said they joined a consortium created by ID Analytics LLC to similarly detect multiple loan applications.

"Leveraging collective data...will further strengthen the experience for borrowers and lenders alike," a LendingClub spokesman said. He said the lender "relies on a variety of internal and external tools" to analyze fraud risk.

The online lenders are expanding their methods to catch up with big banks, which Mr. Phelan of TransUnion said use voluminous data histories and vast compliance departments to try to spot people with fraudulent identities. Some banks are also using biometric technology, such as fingerprints and selfies, to double-check the identity of their existing customers.

From J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. to Bank of America Corp., lending giants are increasingly competing with online upstarts. Both of those banks have more than 20 million users for their mobile applications, according to S&P Global Market Intelligence a channel they can use to offer new services to existing retail customers.

J.P. Morgan finance chief Marianne Lake said on the company's earnings call this month that the bank was working to clear anti-money-laundering and know-your-customer hurdles so it could sign up more customers digitally rather than in branches.

Online lenders are also ramping up security as they boost their loan growth and as they face scrutiny from investors, who in recent months have become more concerned about the quality of loans and their performance.

Kabbage Inc., an online business lender that launched in 2011, this year began allowing borrowers to apply entirely through a mobile app. This asks applicants to use their phone to take a picture of a government ID, such as a driver's license.

That photo of an ID is verified by Mitek Systems Inc., which charges a transaction fee for the service, a follow-on to a popular feature offered by the company that analyzes pictures of checks taken by mobile phones.

Before starting the new verification product, Kabbage had been rejecting as many as 3% of its daily online loan applications as potential cases of fraud, said the firm's CEO, Rob Frohwein. The firm feared it might be turning away some legitimate borrowers, he added.

Now, Kabbage anticipates getting that rate down to about 0.5%. "Additional technology doesn't mean fraud attempts will go down, but we'll be able to accept more applications" as the firm gets more adept at rooting out suspicious borrowers, Mr. Frohwein says.

Avant, the Chicago-based online lender, also recently began using its mobile app for new borrower applications, which allows it to capture the photo of the borrowers' ID as an element in its verification process, according to the company.

Not all online lenders use a mobile app for new borrowers. At LendingClub borrowers apply through a website, and are called on the phone to confirm information or request additional documents when the lender deems it necessary. Prosper works with software company The ID Co. to help confirm applicants' identities with their bank accounts, as one tool to identify customers.

Last Stand for New Jersey's Scott Garrett?

Should "http://www.wsj.com/articles/wall-street-braces-for-new-democratic-muscle-in-the-senate-1477564202" next month, Rep. Scott Garrett of New Jersey has a good chance of being one of the top Republican casualties.

Mr. Garrett, 57 years old, has generally been seen throughout his political career as an ally of financial firms and a harsh critic of financial regulators. He is a founding member of the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/house-conservatives-pose-a-hurdle-for-kevin-mccarthys-rise-to-speaker-1444154897", an alliance of hardline conservatives that last year led the drive to oust then-Speaker John Boehner. He is also chairman of an important House Financial Services subcommittee, a powerful post that oversees financial regulators including the Securities and Exchange Commission.

Yet Mr. Garrett's support among financial firms has all but dried up and now he's in the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/n-j-republican-congressman-scott-garrett-is-in-competitive-race-with-newcomer-1469654735" against Democrat Josh Gottheimer, a former Microsoft Corp. executive.

Mr. Garrett's change of political fortune stems from anti-gay remarks he reportedly made to Republican lawmakers in a closed-door meeting last year that were then leaked to the media. Almost immediately, big banks like Goldman Sachs Group Inc. stopped contributing to him, as did the Big Four accounting firms.

The drop-off in contributions from financial firms has been stark, particularly in the accounting industry. While accountants and accounting political action committees have previously been among Mr. Garrett's top donors, they have donated only $17,008 to Mr. Garrett's campaign and his leadership PAC in the current campaign cycle, down from $78,999 in the 2014 cycle, according to data recently compiled by the Center for Responsive Politics.

The Big Four accounting firms believe Mr. Garrett is now out to get them, according to people familiar with their thinking. Just before the House recessed last month, the New Jersey lawmaker "http://www.wsj.com/articles/gop-lawmaker-wants-to-allow-auditor-watchdog-to-disclose-disciplinary-cases-1474590364" siding with the firms' regulator in a fight about whether the regulator's enforcement actions should be made public. That legislation, which has been introduced by other lawmakers in prior years but never moved through Mr. Garrett's panel, is stridently opposed by the firms. In an interview, Mr. Garrett denied he was seeking retribution, saying the Big Four simply don't wish to have "transparency and accountability in their business dealings."

Meanwhile, Mr. Garrett "is in deep electoral danger and has no one to blame but himself," according to the Cook Political Report. A recent poll by Garin-Hart-Yang for the Democratic-aligned House Majority PAC finds Mr. Gottheimer surging ahead of Mr. Garrett 48% to 41% after a sustained barrage of ads accusing Mr. Garrett of bigotry and holding views of a "rural Alabama conservative," according to Cook.

Rep. John Lewis (D., Ga.), the famed civil-rights leader, "http://www.nj.com/bergen/index.ssf/2016/10/civil_rights_leader_stumps_for_gottheimer_in_heated_congressional_race.html" in New Jersey this week, saying the candidate will bring people together.

In an unexpected twist, some top Republicans are supporting the Democrat. Meg Whitman, the chief executive of Hewlett Packard Enterprise Co. and a longtime Republican fundraiser, told The Wall Street Journal she is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hewlett-packards-whitman-shakes-up-new-jersey-house-race-1476147040" because she believes the incumbent lawmaker is a "Republican extremist." Mr. Garrett responded in the Journal by doubting Ms. Whitman's Republican bona fides.

Mr. Garrett could yet prevail, but the momentum in the race certainly appears against him.

 Michael Rapoport contributed to this article.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Wall Street Braces for New Democratic Muscle in the Senate

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/wall-street-braces-for-new-democratic-muscle-in-the-senate-1477564202" has Wall Street increasingly focused on the battle for control of Congress and bracing for a less friendly environment if Democrats retake the Senate and give new clout to the party's left wing.

"It's not Hillary Clinton we are worried about. It's the influence that the liberals have on her," said Richard Hunt, president and chief executive of the Consumer Bankers Association trade group. He cited Democrats on the Senate Banking Committee, particularly Elizabeth Warren of Massachusetts, who has regularly put the financial industry on the defensive, most recently during hearings over Wells Fargo & Co.'s scandal.

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/rubins-take-progressives-set-terms-for-possible-clinton-appointments-1477394711"

TARP Official to Congress: Require CEOs to Confirm No Wrongdoing at Their Firms

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/tarp-official-to-congress-require-ceos-to-certify-that-there-is-no-wrongdoing-at-their-companies-1477490810?tesla=y" on Wednesday asked Congress to require chief executives on Wall Street and elsewhere to certify annually that they found "no criminal conduct or civil fraud in their organization."

The proposal could have far-reaching effects if adopted. Under current rules, prosecutors often have a hard time proving intentional wrongdoing on the part of senior officials, who are several layers removed from misconduct of lower-level employees.

Democratic Senators Call for Stricter Rules on Wall Street Pay

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/democratic-senators-call-for-stricter-rules-on-wall-street-pay-1477522126" urged federal regulators to strengthen proposed rules governing Wall Street pay practices, saying the recent scandal at Wells Fargo & Co. underscored the need to hold executives accountable for misconduct.

The request comes as policy makers rush to complete the rules before President Barack Obama leaves office in January. The senators laid out their ideas in a letter addressed to the heads of six federal agencies responsible for crafting the pay rules. The lawmakers who signed the letter include Sens. Robert Menendez of New Jersey; Sherrod Brown of Ohio, the top Democrat on the Senate Banking Committee; and Elizabeth Warren of Massachusetts, the leading congressional critic of Wall Street.

Market Volatility Has Currency Traders Abandoning a Tool to Stop Losses

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/market-volatility-has-currency-traders-abandoning-a-tool-to-stop-losses-1477560601", worried that events such as the pound's recent collapse could become more common, are abandoning an automated trading mechanism that was supposed to protect them from losses after big market swings.

The trading mechanism, known as a stop-loss order, is meant to sell an investor's position as soon as a currency hits a preset level against another currency. Banks and electronic trading platforms often struggle to execute the orders at exactly the level an investor requested, resulting in trades below the indicated price even when markets are relatively calm. The rise in "flash crashes" sudden drops that often quickly reverse has made the orders even more risky and ineffective, investors say.

WSJ Pro: OCC Offers to Restructure to Assist Innovation at Banks and Fintech Firms

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/occ-offers-to-restructure-to-assist-innovation-at-banks-and-fintech-firms-1477515650" on Wednesday released its plan for restructuring the agency to make it easier to work with banks and fintech firms eager to launch innovative financial products.

The changes to the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency follow comments from the industry and consumers on how to regulate the emerging fintech sector. The final recommendations center on creating a stand-alone office next year to be the "clearinghouse" for all fintech requests, ranging from launching fintech pilot programs, training agency staff and coordinating with other regulators.

WSJ Pro: Brexit Won't Lead to Financial Regulation  Bonfire,' Says Bank of England Official

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/brexit-wont-lead-to-financial-regulation-bonfire-says-bank-of-england-official-1477491580" bankers Wednesday not to expect a post-Brexit bonfire of financial regulations.

Sam Woods, deputy governor for prudential regulation, said in speech in London's financial district that Britons' decision to take the U.K. out of the European Union wouldn't lead to a rolling back of postcrisis financial reforms. Regulators around the world have stuffed banks with extra capital and ordered them to hold piles of easily-salable assets to prevent a repeat of the banking blowup that by 2009 had led to a global recession.

SEC Proposal Eases Path of Dissidents Waging Campaigns for Corporate Boards

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-to-vote-on-proposal-easing-path-of-dissidents-to-corporate-boards-1477491499" a company's board of directors would get a boost for their campaigns under a proposal approved Wednesday by the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission.

The SEC's proposal, which passed on a 2-1 vote, would require companies to give shareholders a single ballot listing all candidates in a contested board election. The SEC would have to take public comments on the proposal and vote on it a second time before the new rule could take effect. Currently, voters typically receive two sets of ballots, each featuring a rival slate of board candidates.

Follow the regulatory process with WSJ Pro's "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/rules/"

Morgan Stanley Will Keep Commission-Based IRAs

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/morgan-stanley-will-keep-commission-based-iras-1477508411" brokerage customers keep commission-based accounts once new government conflict rules take effect, a move directly opposite the approach that its biggest rival on Wall Street, Merrill Lynch, is taking.

Under the move, Morgan Stanley will allow its nearly 16,000 brokers to use an exemption under the so-called fiduciary rule to continue offering individual retirement accounts that charge investors for each transaction made. The fiduciary rule, drafted by the Labor Department and set to start taking effect in April, requires brokers to put the interests of retirement savers ahead of their own and firms to justify varying levels of compensation in IRAs.

Lynn Tilton Loses Delaware Court Fight Over Zohar Funds' Records

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/lynn-tilton-loses-delaware-court-fight-over-zohar-funds-records-1477518636" has found that turnaround executive Lynn Tilton's Patriarch Partners breached its duty to hand over business records when it surrendered control of the investment funds that fed cash to her collection of troubled companies.

Alvarez & Marsal, which stepped into the role of managing the $2.5 billion Zohar funds earlier this year, won a tug of war over information with Ms. Tilton, a former distressed-company financier who now manages her family office. The ruling comes as Ms. Tilton is on trial in New York on charges of civil fraud brought by the Securities and Exchange Commission. The SEC contends that she hid losses in the investment funds, dubbed Zohar I, II and III, from investors. She has denied the SEC allegations.

Reaction to AT&T-Time Warner Deal Shows Presidency's Growing Reach

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/reaction-to-at-t-time-warner-deal-shows-presidencys-growing-reach-1477501112?tesla=y" they don't have much sway over the economy: Congress controls the budget, the Federal Reserve sets interest rates, global markets determine the price of oil.

But they do control one immensely powerful lever: a sprawling regulatory state. The rules the executive branch writes and how its appointees enforce them increasingly shape economic and business behavior. Democrats have long been the most enthusiastic proponents of the regulatory state, but in Donald Trump they have a fellow traveler, albeit one with different motives.

Wall Street Leads in Campaign Contributions

The financial sector has been the largest source of campaign donations to federal candidates and parties during this election cycle, "http://ourfinancialsecurity.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/WallStreetMoneyOct2016.pdf", an advocacy group. Individuals and entities from the financial sector have donated nearly $800 million between January 2015 and Sept. 20, 2016. The sector spent another $667.2 million on federal lobbying from January 2015 through June 30, 2016, making it the third-largest spender on lobbying. More than 350 financial firms and trade associations spent at least $500,000 on both campaign contributions and lobbying expenditures during this period, the report finds.

What Would It Take to Cut 70% of Federal Regulations?

Donald Trump has said he would cut anywhere between 10% and 70% of all regulations if he were elected president. While they believe the 70% target is "far more ambitious and likely impossible," "https://www.americanactionforum.org/insight/trump-regulation-70-percent-cut/" sketch out what such a plan might look like. Such sweeping changes to regulations could cut compliance costs by as much as $777 billion. They estimate that $36.3 billion would come from repealing Dodd-Frank, the second-largest single reduction besides repealing the Affordable Care Act, which they estimate would save $51.6 billion in regulatory costs.

Elizabeth Warren Helps Hillary Clinton, and Herself

Even while supporting a Clinton administration, Sen. Elizabeth Warren is likely to cause it headaches on a range of issues, "http://blogs.wsj.com/washwire/2016/10/26/elizabeth-warren-helps-hillary-clinton-and-herself/". With Democrats projected to win a slight majority in the Senate, "a Clinton administration would need to compromise with Republicans to get the 60 votes necessary to get anything out of the Senate," he writes. And that may clash with "the left's desire to get as much as possible as quickly as possible."
